Oath on Exiting: Does Consent Without Order Break the Oath?
Rulings on oath not to exit when carried out by order, consent, or coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether an oath not to exit is broken when the person is carried out by order, with consent without order, or by coercion, and explain the attribution principle.

Core Idea
If you order to be carried out, you break your oath; if you only consent, you do not.

Oaths often involve actions done by others. This passage clarifies when an extraction is attributed to you, breaking your oath. The key is whether you gave an order. Without an order, the act is not yours, even if you consented. Coercion also protects you from breaking the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three ways a person can be carried out: by order, with consent without order, and by coercion.
Each scenario has a different ruling.
2
Understand the attribution principle
Recognise that only the action of someone acting under your order is legally attributed to you. Consent without order does not transfer the act.
This is a core Hanafi usul principle.
3
Apply the analogy to entering
The same ruling applies if the oath is about entering rather than exiting, because the cause (order) is the same.
This analogy shows the generality of the principle.
4
Note the Maliki exception
The text mentions that Imam Malik holds that if the oath-taker did not resist the carrier, the oath is broken, even without an order.
This is a valid ikhtilaf.
5
Confirm the correct Hanafi view
The relied-upon Hanafi view is that consent without order does not break the oath; coercion never breaks it.
The text says 'ala al-sahih (on the correct view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخرج أي لو حلف أن لا يخرج من البيت أو المسجد فأخرج محمولاً بأمره يحنث؛ لأن فعل المأمور يضاف إليه، وهذا الحكم لا يختص بالحلف على الخروج؛ لأنه لو حلف أن لا يدخل فأدخل محمولاً بأمره حنث. رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٢/١] (فتح) حنث لانعقاد اليمين على فعل نفسه، وفعل المأمور مضاف إليه بواسطة أمره، فانتقل إليه الحكم. (عيني) وبرضاه إلخ أي لو حلف أن لا يخرج فأخرج محمولاً برضاه من دون أمره، لا يحنث على الصحيح؛ لأن الفعل إنما ينتقل بالأمر ولم يوجد ولو أخرج مكرهاً بأن حمله إنسان وأخرجه كارهاً لذلك، فلا يحنث بالاتفاق؛ أما إذا توعده فخرج بنفسه، حنث ؛ لما عرف أن الإكراه بهذا المعنى لا يعدم الفعل عندنا، وعن مالك: لو لم يستصعب على الحامل حنث. (فتح عيني)
He does not exit? i.e., if he swore that he would not exit from the house or mosque, and he was carried out by his order, he breaks his oath; because the action of the commanded is attributed to him, and this ruling is not specific to the oath of exiting; because if he swore not to enter and was entered by his order, he breaks the oath. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq) (Fath). He breaks his oath because the oath is on his own action, and the action of the commanded is attributed to him through his order, so the ruling transfers to him. (Al-'Inayah). And with his consent, etc.: i.e., if he swore not to exit and was carried out with his consent without his order, he does not break his oath in the correct view; because the action is only transferred through order, and it did not exist. And if he was carried out coerced, i.e., a person carried him and he disliked it, then he does not break his oath by consensus. But if he was threatened and exited himself, he breaks his oath; because coercion in this sense does not negate the action according to us. According to Malik: if he did not make it difficult for the carrier, he breaks his oath. (Fath, 'Inayah)
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for an oath not to exit a place when the person is carried out. The key distinction is whether the extraction was by order, with consent, or by coercion. If the person gives an order to be carried out, the action is attributed to him, and the oath is broken. This applies equally to entering. If the extraction is with consent but without an order, the action is not attributed to him, so the oath is not broken in the correct Hanafi view. If the extraction is coercive (the person being carried against his will), then by consensus the oath is not broken. However, if the person is threatened and then leaves on his own, the oath is broken because coercion does not negate the action under Hanafi law. The passage also notes a minority Maliki view.

Key Terms
محمولاً بأمره (mahmūlan bi-amrihi): Carried out by his order: the action is attributed to him, breaking the oath.
مكرهاً (mukrahan): Coerced, i.e., extracted against his will, which does not break the oath by consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears not to exit his house. His friend picks him up and carries him out with his consent but without his order. According to the correct Hanafi view, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he consented.
B) No, because the action was not by his order.
C) Yes, because he did not resist.
D) No, because consent is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath is broken only when the act is attributed to you through your explicit order, not mere consent or coercion.

Oath on Exiting with Exception for a Funeral
Ruling on oath not to exit except for a funeral; subsequent actions do not break oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath with a specific exception and identify which subsequent actions break the oath.

Core Idea
An oath with an exception limits the prohibition to acts outside the exception; actions within the exception do not break the oath.

This lesson examines the effect of an exception (istithnā') in an oath. In Hanafi fiqh, if someone swears not to exit except for a funeral, exiting for that purpose does not break the oath. Even if additional errands are performed after the funeral, the exit itself was valid. Understanding this nuance prevents mistaken rulings on oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its exception
Read the oath phrase: 'I will not exit except for a funeral.' Note the explicit exception.
2
Determine the action within the exception
The person exits for the funeral. This falls within the exception, so the oath is not broken by this act.
3
Evaluate subsequent actions
After the funeral, the person performs another need (e.g., going to a shop). Determine if this is part of the same 'exit' or a new action.
Hanafi: the exit is complete upon leaving for the exception; later actions are separate arrivals, not part of the same exit.
4
Conclude whether the oath is broken
Since the exit was for the permitted exception, and the subsequent action is a separate event, the oath remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مكرهاً لا، كـــ لا يخرج إلا إلى جنازة فخرج إليها ثم أتى حاجة. لا يخرج أو أي لا يحنث الحالف الجنازة لا يذهب إلى مكة فخرج يريدها ثم رجع حنث، وفي لا يأتيها لا. ليأتينه فلم يأته أي أو حلف لا يذهب حتى أي حال كونه يريد مكة مات حنث في آخر حياته ليأتينّه إن استطاع. الحالف أي الحالف أي أو حلف لا يأتي مكة (مستخلص)
Or coercion: no. Like: He does not exit except for a funeral, so he exited for it, then he did another need. He does not exit? i.e., the swearer is not breaking the oath by the funeral. He does not go to Makkah: if he exited intending it, then returned, he breaks his oath. And regarding not going to it: no. That he comes to him: if he did not come to him until he died, he breaks his oath at the end of his life, provided he is able...
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a person swears not to exit his house except for a funeral. If he exits for the funeral and then after that performs another errand, he does not break his oath. The reason is that the exit was for the permitted exception, and the subsequent movement is a separate action. The commentary in Fath explains: the exit is from inside to outside, and for the funeral, exiting the door is sufficient; going to another need afterwards is not part of the same exit but a new arrival, so it does not break the oath. The matn also mentions other rulings but the focus here is the funeral exception.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): Exception in an oath: if the oath includes an exception like 'except for a funeral', actions within the exception do not break the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swore, 'I will not exit my house except for a funeral.' He left for a funeral and afterwards went to a shop. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he left the house.
B) No, because the exit was for the funeral.
C) Yes, because he did an additional action.
D) No, because the oath is fulfilled by the funeral.

Key Takeaway
An oath with a specific exception is not broken by actions that fall within that exception, even if additional separate acts follow.

Oath on Going to Mecca: Khuruj vs Dhahab vs Itiyan
Detailed ruling on oath not to go to Mecca: conditions for breaking the oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khuruj, dhahab, and ityan in Hanafi oath law and apply the correct ruling to determine when an oath is broken.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, swearing not to go to Mecca breaks the oath upon departure, not arrival, except when the verb 'ityan' is used.

Oaths are serious commitments in Islamic law. When someone swears not to go to Mecca using words like 'exit', 'go', or 'come', the timing of when the oath is broken differs based on the verb's implication. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies these nuanced distinctions, showing that departure with intention suffices for khuruj and dhahab, while ityan requires actual arrival. Understanding these differences is essential for correctly applying kaffarah obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Verbs
The passage discusses three Arabic verbs: khuruj (exit), dhahab (go), and ityan (come). Each has a different legal effect in oaths regarding Mecca.
Khuruj and dhahab are treated similarly; ityan is distinct.
2
Understand the General Rule for Khuruj
For khuruj, the oath is broken when the person leaves his town's inhabited area ('umran misrihi) with intention to go to Mecca. Even if he turns back, he breaks the oath because the condition (departure with intent) is met.
Leaving for a funeral breaks the oath immediately upon leaving the door, but travel requires passing the built-up area.
3
Apply the Analogy to Dhahab
According to the correct view (sahih), dhahab is analogous to khuruj: it means departure, not arrival. Thus, the oath is broken upon departure with intention, not upon entering Mecca.
Some scholars said dhahab requires arrival, citing Quran 20:43, but the majority view is that common usage supports departure.
4
Note the Exception for Ityan
Ityan (coming) implies actual arrival. Therefore, if someone swears 'la ati Mecca', he does not break his oath until he physically enters Mecca, regardless of intention.
Intention is not required for ityan because arrival is unambiguous.
5
Consider the Role of Intention
For khuruj and dhahab, intention (niyya) at the moment of departure is necessary because departure could be for another purpose. For ityan, intention is irrelevant because arrival is the sole criterion.
This highlights the importance of linguistic precision in formulating oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخرج أو لا يذهب إلخ أي من حلف لا يخرج إلى مكة أو حلف لا يذهب إلى مكة، فخرج مريداً إليها، ثم رجع من الطريق حنث؛ لأن الشرط وجود الخروج على قصد مكة وقد وجد والذهاب بمعنى الزوال عن المقر، وكذا إذا حلف لا يروح إلى مكة، فهو أيضاً بمعنى لا يخرج، ويشترط للحنث أن يجاوز الحالف عمران مصره مع نية الخروج إلى مكة، والفرق بين الخروج إلى مكة حيث لا يحنث إلا بمجاوزة العمران، وبين الخروج إلى الجنازة حيث يحنث بالانفصال عن الباب، ولا يشترط فيه مجاوزة العمران؛ لأن الخروج إلى مكة سفر، ولا سفر قبل مجاوزة العمران والذهاب كالخروج في الصحيح. وقيل: لا يحنث في الذهاب ما لم يدخلها؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿اذْهَبَا إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ ﴾ (طه :(٤٣)، والمراد الإتيان فلا يحنث كما في الصورة الآتية، وجه الأول: أنه بمنزلة الخروج، يقال: ذهب إلى مكة إذا زال عن مكانه فلا يقتضي الوصول، ولهذا صح أن يقال: ذهب إلى "مكة قبل الوصول إليها كما يقال: خرج إلى مكة" بخلاف الإتيان، ثم في الخروج والذهاب تشترط النية عند الانفصال للحنث، وفي الإتيان لا يشترط بل إذا وصل إليها يحنث نوى أو لم ينو؛ لأن الخروج متنوع يحتمل الخروج إليها وإلى غيرها وكذا ،الذهاب فلابد من النية، بخلاف الإتيان؛ لأن الوصول غير متنوع. (مستخلص، فتح) ثم رجع قبل الوصول إليها قصد غيرها أم لا . (ط) عيني) لا : أي لا يحنث بالخروج بل بالوصول؛ لأن الإتيان عبارة عن الوصول. (عيني)
He does not exit or does not go, etc.: i.e., whoever swears not to exit to Mecca or swears not to go to Mecca, and he exited intending it, then returned from the road, he breaks his oath; because the condition is the existence of exit with the intention of Mecca, and it occurred, and 'going' (dhahab) means departure from one's place. Likewise, if he swore not to go to Mecca, it is also in the sense of not exiting. It is a condition for breaking the oath that he passes the inhabited area of his town with the intention of exiting to Mecca. The difference between exiting to Mecca—where he does not break his oath until passing the inhabited area—and exiting to a funeral—where he breaks by separating from the door and passing the inhabited area is not required—is because exiting to Mecca is travel, and there is no travel before passing the inhabited area. And 'going' (dhahab) is like exiting in the correct view. Some said: he does not break his oath in 'going' until he enters it, based on His statement: 'Go to Pharaoh' (Ta-Ha 43), and the intended meaning is 'coming' (ityan), so he does not break his oath as in the following case. The first view's reasoning is that 'going' is like exiting; one says 'he went to Mecca' when he departs from his place, which does not require arrival, so it is correct to say 'he went to Mecca' before arriving, just as one says 'he exited to Mecca', unlike 'coming' (ityan). Then, for exiting and going, intention at departure is necessary for breaking the oath; for coming, intention is not necessary, rather when he arrives he breaks the oath whether he intended or not, because exiting is varied (it could be for Mecca or elsewhere), and likewise going, so intention is necessary, unlike coming, because arrival is not varied. (Mustakhlis, Fath) Then he returned before arriving at it, whether he intended something else or not. (T, 'Inayah). No: i.e., he does not break his oath by exiting but by arrival, because 'coming' means arrival. ('Inayah)
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of oaths concerning going to Mecca. It distinguishes between three terms: khuruj (exiting), dhahab (going), and ityan (coming). The ruling: If a person swears 'I will not exit to Mecca' or 'I will not go to Mecca', and he leaves his town with the intention of going to Mecca but turns back before arriving, he breaks his oath. The condition is that he must have passed the built-up area of his town ('umran misrihi) because traveling to Mecca constitutes a journey, and a journey is considered to begin only after leaving the town's outskirts. This contrasts with exiting for a funeral, where simply leaving the door suffices. The term dhahab is synonymous with khuruj in this context, meaning departure from one's place, not arrival. However, some scholars (indicated by 'qila') argue that dhahab implies arrival, citing the Quranic verse 'Go to Pharaoh', which commands arrival; thus, the oath would not be broken until actually entering Mecca. The passage resolves this by clarifying that dhahab in common usage means departure, so it is correct to say 'he went to Mecca' before arriving. Therefore, the condition for breaking the oath is intention at the moment of departure. For ityan (coming), no intention is needed; the oath is broken upon actual arrival because arrival is unambiguous. The passage ends with a note that returning before arriving does not break the oath, regardless of intention.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khurūj): Exiting from a place; in oaths regarding Mecca, it means leaving the city's inhabited area.
الذهاب (al-dhahāb): Going, synonymous with khuruj in the context of oaths; it means departure, not necessarily arrival.
الإتيان (al-ityān): Coming or arriving; in oaths, it requires actual arrival at the destination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears 'I will not go (adhhabu) to Mecca.' He leaves his town intending Mecca but returns before reaching the built-up area of another town. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he left with intention.
B) No, because he did not arrive.
C) Yes, because he left his town.
D) No, because he returned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, the verb used in an oath determines when the oath is broken: 'khuruj' and 'dhahab' break upon departure with intention, while 'ityan' breaks upon arrival.

Ruling on Oath to Come to Someone: Death Prevents Fulfillment
Ruling on oath to come to someone: death of either party breaks the oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an absolute oath to come to someone is broken if either the swearer or the one sworn to dies before fulfillment.

Core Idea
If either party dies before the oath is fulfilled, the oath is broken—only impossibility matters.

In absolute oaths, the possibility of fulfillment is the key. This passage clarifies that death of either the swearer or the person sworn to makes fulfillment impossible, thus breaking the oath. The commentary resolves a pronoun ambiguity to confirm that the ruling applies symmetrically to both parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath type
The passage discusses an absolute oath (yamīn muṭlaqah) without a time limit. Recognize that this type of oath obligates immediate or indefinite fulfillment.
Absolute oaths are contrasted with conditional or time-bound oaths.
2
Understand the condition for fulfillment
The oath is not broken as long as both parties are alive, because fulfillment is still possible.
Death ends the possibility, triggering the breaking of the oath (ḥinth).
3
Determine when ḥinth occurs
If either the swearer or the person sworn to dies before the oath is fulfilled, the oath is broken. The death of the person sworn to makes coming to them impossible.
The principle is that objective impossibility breaks the oath, regardless of fault.
4
Resolve the pronoun ambiguity
The commentary clarifies that the pronoun in 'until he died' refers to either party, not just the swearer, ensuring the ruling is general.
This prevents the misconception that only the swearer's death breaks the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يأته إلخ : أي من حلف ليأتين زيداً مثلا يعني يأتي منزله أو حانوته لقيه أو لم يلقه فلم يأته حتى مات حنث، وأصل هذا أن الحالف في اليمين المطلقة لا يحنث ما دام الحالف والمحلوف عليه قائمين؛ لتصور البر، فإذا مات أحدهما فإنه يحنث، وبهذا ظهر أن الضمير في قوله : "حتى" "مات" يعود إلى أحدهما أيهما كان لا أنه خاص بالحالف، وكذا الضمير في آخر حياته" يعود إلى أحدهما وليس بخاص بأحدهما، لكن الضمير الأول إن كان إلى الحالف كان الثاني كذلك، وإن كان إلى المحلوف عليه كان الثاني أيضاً إليه، ولا يتصور أن يكون الأول –
Then he did not come to him, etc.: i.e., whoever swore that he would come to Zayd, for example, meaning come to his house or shop, whether he met him or not, and he did not come to him until he (one of them) died, he breaks his oath. The origin of this is: a person in an absolute oath does not break his oath as long as both the swearer and the one sworn by (the object) are existing, because fulfillment is possible. If one of them dies, then he breaks his oath. Thus it is clear that the pronoun in his saying 'until he died' refers to either one, not specifically the swearer. Similarly, the pronoun in 'the end of his life' refers to either one and is not specific to one. But if the first pronoun refers to the swearer, then the second also refers to him; if it refers to the one sworn by, then the second refers to him as well. It is not possible that the first…
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage discusses an oath to come to someone (e.g., 'I swear I will come to Zayd's house'). The principle is that in an absolute oath (without a time limit), the swearer does not break the oath as long as both he and the person he swore to are alive, because fulfillment is still possible. If either party dies before the oath is fulfilled, the oath is broken because fulfillment becomes impossible. The commentary clarifies that the pronoun in 'until he died' can refer to either the swearer or the person sworn to. The page cuts off before completing the discussion.

Key Terms
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamīn al-muṭlaqah): An absolute oath without a time limit or condition; it remains in effect as long as both parties exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Zayd swears, 'I will come to Amr's house.' Before Zayd goes, Amr dies. According to the Hanafi ruling, does Zayd break his oath?
A) No, because he was not at fault.
B) Yes, because the person sworn to died.
C) No, because he can still go to his grave.
D) Yes, because the oath was conditional.

Key Takeaway
In a Hanafi absolute oath to come to someone, death of either the swearer or the person sworn to breaks the oath due to impossibility of fulfillment.

Oath with Exception: Subsequent Actions Do Not Break the Oath
Ruling on oath with exception; definition of khuruj applied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath with an exception and apply the definition of khuruj to determine whether a subsequent action breaks the oath.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, an oath not to leave except for X is satisfied by the initial exit; further actions are not new exits.

This lesson examines a specific ruling on oaths: if someone swears not to leave the house except for a funeral, then leaves for the funeral and afterwards goes to another errand, does the oath break? The Hanafi answer is no, based on a precise definition of the word 'exit' (khuruj). Understanding this ruling clarifies how exceptions in oaths operate and prevents common misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its exception
The oath is 'I will not go out except for a funeral.' The exception is the funeral. Identify the prohibited act (going out) and the permitted exception.
2
Define khuruj (exit) as per Hanafi usul
The text defines khuruj as 'separating from the interior' (al-infiṣāl min dākhil). This is a key linguistic definition that governs the ruling.
The definition is taken from the commentary of al-'Ayni.
3
Apply the definition to the scenario
When the person steps out for the funeral, that initial separation constitutes the exit. The exception is fulfilled at that moment.
4
Distinguish subsequent movement
After the exit, the person's further journey (e.g., going to another need) is not a new exit but rather movement outside. The word 'coming' (ityān) refers to arrival, which is different from exit.
The text states 'they differ' (taghāyarā).
5
Conclude that the oath is not broken
Since the only prohibited act (exit) was performed for the exception, and no further exit occurs, the oath remains intact. The person does not become a ḥānith (oath-breaker).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَو مُكرَهاً لا، كـــ لا يَخرُج إلّا إلى جَنازةٍ فخَرَج إليها ثُم أتى حاجةً. لا يَخرُج أي لا يَحنَث. (عيني) لا يَخرُج إلخ شَبَّهَ عَدَمَ الحِنثِ في المَسألةِ السَّابقةِ بِعَدَمِ الحِنثِ في هَذِهِ المسألةِ أي كما لا يَحنَث لو حَلَف أن لا يَخرُجَ مِن دارِهِ إلّا إلى جَنازةٍ فخَرَج مِنها إلى الجَنازةِ ثُم أتى حاجَةً أُخرى؛ لأن المَوجودَ هو الخُروجُ المُستَثنى والمُضِيُّ بَعدَ ذلك لَيسَ بِخُروجٍ؛ لأن الخُروجَ عِبارَةٌ عَنِ الانفِصالِ مِن داخِلٍ، فيَكفي في قَصدِ الخُروجِ إلى الجَنازةِ الانفِصالُ مِن بابِ الدَّارِ؛ لِأنه بِذلك يُعَدُّ خارِجاً، والإتيانُ إلى حاجَةٍ أُخرى عِبارَةٌ عَنِ الوُصولِ إليها فَتَغايَرا، فلا يَحنَثُ. (فَتْح)
Or [if he is] coerced, no. Like: He swore not to go out except for a funeral, so he went out for it then came for a need. He does not go out meaning he does not break the oath. ('Ayni) 'He does not go out, etc.' compares the absence of oath-breaking in the previous case to that in this case, i.e., just as he does not break the oath if he swore not to go out of his house except for a funeral, then went out from it to the funeral, then came for another need—because what existed is the excepted exit, and going after that is not an exit. Because 'going out' means separating from the interior, so for the intention of going out to the funeral, separating from the door of the house suffices, as by that he counts as being outside, and coming for another need means reaching it, so they differ, so he does not break the oath. (Fath)
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses an oath where one swears not to leave the house except for a specific purpose, e.g., a funeral. The author explains that if the person leaves for the funeral and then proceeds to another errand, the oath is not broken. The reasoning is that 'exit' (khuruj) is defined as the act of separating from the interior of the house. Once the person steps out for the excepted purpose, that definition is satisfied. Subsequent movement after the exception is not a new exit but rather a continuation of journeying. The term 'coming for another need' (al-ityān) refers to arriving, which is distinct from exiting. Therefore, the oath remains unbroken. This follows the Hanafi principle that an exception in an oath nullifies the obligation for that specific act.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khurūj): In this passage, it means the act of separating from the interior of a house or place, which constitutes the fulfillment of the action sworn about.
الحنث (al-ḥanath): The breaking of an oath, incurring sin and usually requiring expiation (kaffarah).
الإتيان (al-ityān): Coming to a place or reaching a destination; distinct from the act of exiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does someone who swears not to go out except for a funeral, then goes to the funeral and then to another errand, not break his oath according to this text?
A) Because he intended to go to the funeral only
B) Because the exit is only the initial separation from the house, and further movement is not a new exit
C) Because he is coerced
D) Because he did not actually leave his house

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath not to leave except for a specific purpose is only broken by the act of exiting; once that exit occurs for the exception, further movements do not constitute new exits.

Oath Not to Go to Mecca: When Is It Broken?
Clarifies when an oath not to go to Mecca is broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when an oath not to 'go out' or 'go' to Mecca is broken according to Hanafi law, and distinguish the conditions for khuruj, dhahaba, and ityan.

Core Idea
An oath not to 'go out' to Mecca is broken upon departure past the city limit; 'going' (dhahaba) is equivalent to 'going out'.

Oaths often use nuanced verbs that can change the ruling. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the difference between 'going out' (khuruj), 'going' (dhahaba), and 'coming' (ityan) in the context of an oath not to go to Mecca. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly determining when an oath is broken and whether expiation is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three verbs and their legal definitions
Find the terms khuruj, dhahaba, and ityan in the passage. Note how the author defines each: khuruj as departure with intention to travel, dhahaba similarly, and ityan as arrival.
The passage equates dhahaba with khuruj in the correct view.
2
Understand the condition of passing built-up area for travel
The passage states that for breaking an oath to travel to Mecca, the oath-taker must pass the built-up area ('umran) of his city. This distinguishes travel (safar) from local outings.
For a funeral, breaking occurs upon leaving the door; no travel condition.
3
Analyze the dispute about dhahaba
Some scholars argue that dhahaba requires arrival, citing the Quranic verse 'Go to Pharaoh'. The Hanafi position is that dhahaba only means departure.
The Hanafi evidence is linguistic: one can say 'he went to Mecca' before arriving.
4
Recognize the role of intention
For khuruj and dhahaba, intention at departure is required because the action is ambiguous. For ityan, arrival alone breaks the oath regardless of intention.
Intention is not needed for ityan because arrival is unambiguous.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case where someone swears not to 'go' to Mecca, then departs but returns before passing the city limits, determine whether the oath is broken.
Result: No breaking because the built-up area was not passed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يَخرُج أو لا يَذهَبُ إلخ أي مَن حَلَف لا يَخرُج إلى مَكَّةَ أو حَلَف لا يَذهَبُ إلى مَكَّةَ، فَخَرَجَ مُريداً إليها، ثُم رَجَعَ مِنَ الطَّريقِ حَنِثَ؛ لأن الشَّرطَ وُجودُ الخُروجِ على قَصدِ مَكَّةَ وقَد وُجِدَ، والذَّهابُ بِمَعنى الزَّوالِ عَنِ المُقَرِّ. وكَذا إذا حَلَف لا يَروحُ إلى مَكَّةَ، فهو أيضاً بِمَعنى لا يَخرُج. ويُشتَرَطُ لِلحِنثِ أن يُجاوِزَ الحالِفُ عُمرانَ مِصْرِهِ مَعَ نِيَّةِ الخُروجِ إلى مَكَّةَ. والفَرقُ بَيْنَ الخُروجِ إلى مَكَّةَ حَيثُ لا يَحنَثُ إلّا بِمُجاوَزَةِ العُمرانِ، وبَيْنَ الخُروجِ إلى الجَنازَةِ حَيثُ يَحنَثُ بِالانفِصالِ عَنِ البابِ. ولا يُشتَرَطُ فيهِ مُجاوَزَةُ العُمرانِ؛ لأن الخُروجَ إلى مَكَّةَ سَفَرٌ، ولا سَفَرَ قَبلَ مُجاوَزَةِ العُمرانِ، والذَّهابُ كالخُروجِ في الصَّحيحِ. وقيل: لا يَحنَثُ في الذَّهابِ ما لَم يَدخُلها؛ لِقَولِهِ تعالى: ﴿اذهبا إلى فرعون﴾، والمُرادُ الإتيانُ فلا يَحنَثُ كَما في الصُّورَةِ الآتِيَةِ. وَجهُ الأوَّلِ: أَنَّهُ بِمَنزِلَةِ الخُروجِ، يُقالُ: ذَهَبَ إلى مَكَّةَ إذا زالَ عَن مَكانِهِ، فلا يَقتَضِي الوُصولَ، وَلِهذا صَحَّ أن يُقالَ: ذَهَبَ إلى مَكَّةَ قَبلَ الوُصولِ إليها، كما يُقالُ: خَرَجَ إلى مَكَّةَ، بِخِلافِ الإتيانِ. ثُم في الخُروجِ والذَّهابِ تُشتَرَطُ النِّيَّةُ عِندَ الانفِصالِ لِلحِنثِ، وفي الإتيانِ لا تُشتَرَطُ بَل إذا وَصَلَ إليها يَحنَثُ نَوى أو لَم يَنوِ؛ لأن الخُروجَ مُتَنَوَّعٌ يَحتَمِلُ الخُروجَ إليها وإلى غَيرِها، وكَذا الذَّهابُ، فَلابدَّ مِنَ النِّيَّةِ، بِخِلافِ الإتيانِ؛ لأن الوُصولَ غَيرُ مُتَنَوِّعٍ. (مُستَخلَص، فَتح). ثُم رَجَعَ قَبلَ الوُصولِ إليها قَصدَ غَيرَها أم لا. (ط عيني) لا: أي لا يَحنَثُ بِالخُروجِ بَل بِالوُصولِ؛ لأن الإتيانَ عِبارَةٌ عَنِ الوُصولِ. (عيني)
He does not go out or he does not go, etc.: That is, whoever swears not to go out to Mecca or swears not to go to Mecca, and he went out intending it, then returned from the road – he breaks the oath, because the condition is the existence of going out with the intention of Mecca, and it existed. And 'going' (dhahaba) means departing from one's place. Likewise, if he swore not to go (yaruhu) to Mecca, it also means not to go out. It is necessary for breaking the oath that the oath-taker passes the built-up area of his city with the intention of going out to Mecca. The difference between going out to Mecca, where he does not break the oath except by passing the built-up area, and going out to a funeral, where he breaks the oath by separating from the door, is because going out to Mecca is a journey, and there is no journey before passing the built-up area. And 'going' (dhahaba) is like 'going out' (khuruj) according to the correct view. It is said: He does not break the oath in 'going' as long as he does not enter it, based on Allah's saying: 'Go to Pharaoh' (Ta-Ha 43), and the intended meaning is 'coming' (ityan), so he does not break the oath as in the forthcoming scenario. The first view's evidence: it is like going out; one says 'he went to Mecca' if he departs from his place, so it does not require arrival; hence it is valid to say 'he went to Mecca' before arriving there, just as one says 'he went out to Mecca', unlike 'coming' (ityan). Then, in going out and going, intention is required at the time of departure for the oath to be broken, while in 'coming' it is not required; rather, if he arrives, he breaks the oath whether he intended or not, because going out is varied, possibly going to it or to other places, and likewise going; so intention is necessary, unlike 'coming' because arrival is not varied. (Mukhtasar, Fath). Then he returned before arriving to Mecca, whether he intended something else or not. (T, 'Ayni) No: i.e., he does not break the oath by going out but by arriving, because 'coming' means arrival. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute (and intra-Hanafi discussion) about oaths not to go to Mecca. The author explains that if someone swears not to 'go out' to Mecca or not to 'go' to Mecca, and then starts out intending Mecca but returns before arriving, he breaks his oath according to the correct view. The condition for breaking the oath is passing the built-up area ('umran) of his city because traveling (safar) only begins after that. For funeral outings, one breaks the oath upon leaving the door because it is not travel. There is a disagreement about 'going' (dhahaba): some say it is like 'going out', requiring departure but not arrival; others say it requires entering Mecca, citing Quran 'Go to Pharaoh' meaning arrival. The Hanafi position is that 'dhahaba' is like 'khuruj'; it does not imply arrival. For 'khuruj' and 'dhahaba', intention at departure is required; for 'ityan' (coming), arrival alone breaks the oath without intention because arrival is unambiguous.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khurūj): Here, it means departing from one's place with the intention of traveling to Mecca; legal implications depend on passing the city limit.
الذهاب (al-dhahāb): In this context, it is equated with khuruj, meaning departure from one's place, not necessarily arrival.
الإتيان (al-ityān): Means arrival at the destination; when used in oaths, breaking occurs upon arrival, regardless of intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what condition is necessary for breaking an oath not to 'go out' to Mecca?
A) Entering the city of Mecca
B) Passing the built-up area of one's own city with intention
C) Leaving the door of one's house
D) Intending to pray at the Kaaba

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath not to 'go' to Mecca is broken upon departure past the city limits with intention, not upon arrival.

Oath to Come to Someone: Breaking upon Death
Explains that an oath to come to someone breaks if one party dies before fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an absolute oath to come to someone is broken due to death and explain the underlying legal reasoning.

Core Idea
An absolute oath breaks when either party dies, because fulfillment becomes impossible.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle in the Hanafi law of oaths: the effect of death on an absolute oath to come to someone. Based on a passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, we see that the oath is deemed broken at the moment of death, regardless of which party dies. This ruling follows from the fundamental condition that the sworn act must remain possible; once impossibility sets in, the oath is violated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its type
Recognize that the oath is an absolute oath (yamin mutlaqa) to perform an act (coming to someone) without a time limit.
Absolute oaths remain binding as long as fulfillment is conceivable.
2
Determine when the oath breaks
The oath breaks when either the oath-taker or the addressee dies before the act is performed.
Death makes fulfillment impossible, so the oath is violated at the last moment of life of the deceased.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The condition for breaking (hanth) is the failure of the sworn act. As long as both are alive, coming is possible. Death renders it impossible, so breakage occurs.
The pronoun in 'until he died' refers to either party.
4
Apply the ruling to similar scenarios
This principle applies to any absolute oath where the sworn act becomes impossible due to an event outside the oath-taker's control.
For example, if the person sworn about moves away permanently? Not in this text, but similar logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَم يَأتِهِ إلخ : أي مَن حَلَف لَيَأتينَّ زَيداً مَثَلاً يَعني يَأتي مَنزِلَهُ أو حانوتَهُ لَقِيَهُ أو لَم يَلقَهُ، فَلَم يَأتِهِ حَتّى ماتَ حَنِثَ. وأصلُ هذا أن الحالِفَ في اليَمينِ المُطلَقَةِ لا يَحنَثُ ما دامَ الحالِفُ والمَحلوفُ عَلَيهِ قائِمَينِ؛ لِتَصَوُّرِ البِرِّ، فإذا ماتَ أحَدُهُما فإنه يَحنَثُ. وبهذا ظَهَرَ أنَّ الضَّميرَ في قَولِهِ : "حَتّى" "ماتَ" يَعودُ إلى أحَدِهِما أَيَّهُما كانَ لا أنَّهُ خاصٌّ بِالحالِفِ. وكَذا الضَّميرُ في "آخِرِ حَياتِهِ" يَعودُ إلى أحَدِهِما. وإذا مَضَت المَدَّةُ بأَجمَعِها وَلَم يَأتِهِ حَنِثَ في آخِرِ جُزءٍ مِن جُزءٍ مِن أجزاءِ حَياتِهِ؛ لأن شَرطَ الحِنثِ فَوتُ الإتيانِ وَهُوَ لا يَتَحَقَّقُ إلّا بِما ذَكَرنا. (عيني، فتح، مُحَشّي) ثم ذُكِرَت فَوائِدُ: لَو حَلَفَ "لَيَأتينَّه إن استَطاعَ" ... إلخ.
So he did not come to him, etc.: That is, whoever swore to surely come to Zayd, for example, meaning to come to his house or his shop, whether he meets him or not, and he did not come to him until he died – he breaks the oath. The basis of this is that the oath-taker in an absolute oath does not break it as long as both the oath-taker and the one sworn about are alive, because fulfillment is conceivable. When one of them dies, then he breaks it. From this it is clear that the pronoun in 'until' 'he died' refers to either of them, whichever it may be, not specifically the oath-taker. Likewise, the pronoun in 'at the end of his life' refers to either of them. And if the entire period passes and he does not come to him, he breaks the oath at the last moment of his life, because the condition for breaking is the failure of 'coming', which is only realized by what we mentioned. ('Ayni, Fath, Muhashi) Then benefits are mentioned: if he swore 'I will surely come to him if I am able' ... etc.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding an oath to come to someone. The author explains that if someone swears, 'I will surely come to Zayd,' and then never comes until one of them dies, the oath is broken. The key principle is that an absolute oath (without time limit) remains binding as long as both parties are alive because it is possible to fulfill. Death makes fulfillment impossible, so the oath breaks at the last moment of life of the deceased. The pronoun in 'until he died' refers to either the oath-taker or the addressee, and the 'end of his life' also refers to whichever died. The breaking occurs in the final instant of life because only then does failure become certain.

Key Terms
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamīn al-muṭlaqah): An oath that is not restricted by a time limit; it remains in effect as long as both parties are alive.
الحنث (al-ḥinth): Breaking the oath, which occurs when the sworn action fails to be performed under the conditions; here at death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an oath to come to someone, when does the oath break according to this passage?
A) As soon as the oath is made
B) When the oath-taker dies
C) When either the oath-taker or the addressee dies
D) When the addressee changes his location

Key Takeaway
In an absolute oath, death of either party breaks the oath because fulfillment becomes impossible, and expiation is required.

Interpreting 'Ability' in Oaths: Customary vs. Theological Meaning
Defines istita'ah in oaths as health and means

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two meanings of 'istita'ah' and apply the Hanafi rule to determine which meaning governs in an oath context.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'istata'a' defaults to physical ability, not theological power.

When someone swears 'if I am able', what does 'able' legally mean? Does it refer to the theological power created by Allah at the moment of action, or simply to having sound health and means? The Hanafi school clarifies that the customary meaning prevails, and this affects whether the oath-taker is believed in court or only before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of istita'ah
Read the passage and identify the two distinct meanings: 1) theological power (qudrah) created by Allah, and 2) customary ability (sihhat al-asbab) – soundness of body and means.
The passage states the term is mushtarak (shared).
2
Determine the default meaning in oaths
Note that in the absence of a contextual clue (qarinah), the customary meaning is intended. This is a Hanafi rule: when a word has both a literal and customary sense, the customary governs in oaths.
The text says 'al-murad fi al-hilf al-ma'na al-muta'araf'.
3
Apply the diyanah vs. qada' distinction
If the oath-taker intends the theological meaning, he is believed religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because the customary meaning is dominant and the judge rules by apparent meanings.
The passage refutes the view that he should also be believed legally.
4
Recognize the implication for oath fulfillment
The oath is only considered broken if the person is physically able (healthy and not prevented). A sick or prevented person is not considered 'able' and thus does not break the oath.
Example: If someone swore to visit Zayd tomorrow 'if I am able', and becomes ill, the oath is not violated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهِيَ استِطاعَةُ الصِّحَّةِ. (ط) أي مَن حَلَف لَيَأتينَّ زَيداً غَداً إن استَطاعَ، فَهَذِهِ الاستِطاعَةُ تَكونُ استِطاعَةَ الصِّحَّةِ أي صِحَّةِ أَسبابِ الإتيانِ يَعني تَهِئَّتَهُ وَسَلامَةَ الجَوارِحِ، فَالمَمنوعُ وَالمَريضُ لَيسَ بِمُستَطيعٍ. والحاصِلُ أنَّ الاستِطاعَةَ مُشتَرَكَةٌ بَيْنَ مَعنَيَينِ: أحَدُهُما: القُدرَةُ يَعني الحَقيقَةَ الَّتي يُحدِثُها اللهُ سُبحانَهُ لِلعَبدِ حالَةَ الفِعلِ مُقارِنَةً لِلفِعلِ عِندَ أهلِ السُّنَّةِ وَالجَماعَةِ، وَالثاني: صِحَّةُ أَسبابِ الإتيانِ وَسَلامَةُ الآلاتِ وَارتفِاعُ المَوانِعِ. وَهَذا المَعنى هُوَ المُتَعارَفُ، فَالمُرادُ في الحِلفِ المَعنى المُتَعارَفُ لَكِنَّهُ إن نَوى بِالاستِطاعَةِ القُدرَةَ يُصَدَّقُ دِيانَةً، وَقيلَ: يُصَدَّقُ قَضاءً أيضاً؛ لِأنَّهُ نَوى حَقيقَتَهُ، وَرُدَّ بِأنَّهُ وإن كانَ مُشتَرَكاً إلّا أنَّهُ تَعَوَّفَ استِعمالُهُ عِندَ عَدَمِ القَرينَةِ لِلمَعنى المُتَعارَفِ، فَصارَ ظاهِراً فيهِ، فَلا يُصَدِّقُهُ القاضي في خِلافِهِ. (مُستَخلَص، فَتح)
So it is the ability of health. (T) That is, whoever swore to surely come to Zayd tomorrow if he is able, then this ability is the ability of health, i.e., the soundness of the means of coming, meaning his preparedness and soundness of limbs. So the one prevented and the sick are not able. The conclusion is that 'ability' (istita'ah) is shared between two meanings: one is power (qudrah), meaning the reality that Allah, glorified be He, creates for the servant at the moment of action concomitant with the action according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah; and the second is soundness of the means of coming and safety of instruments and absence of obstacles. This second meaning is the customary one. So the intended meaning in oaths is the customary meaning. However, if he intends by 'ability' the (theological) power, he is believed religiously (diyanatan). It is said: he is also believed legally (qada'an) because he intended its reality. But it is refuted because although it is shared, its usage in the absence of a contextual clue has become customary for the customary meaning, so it became apparent in that, so the judge does not believe him contrary to it. (Mukhtasar, Fath)
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling that clarifies the term 'ability' (istita'ah) in oaths. When someone swears 'if I am able' (in istata'tu), the default meaning is physical health and availability of means ('istita'at al-sihhah'). This includes sound limbs and freedom from hindrances. A sick or prevented person is not considered 'able'. The term is homonymous (mushtarak): it can mean the theological power created by Allah at the time of action (qudrah) or the customary ability of health. In oaths, the customary meaning is intended. If the oath-taker intends the theological meaning, he is believed religiously (before Allah), but not legally (in court) because the customary usage is dominant. This follows the Hanafi principle that when a word is used without context, the customary meaning prevails.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة (al-istiṭāʿah): In this oath context, it means soundness of body and means (sihhat al-asbab), i.e., being physically able and free of obstacles.
التصديق ديانة (al-taṣdīq diyānatan): The oath-taker is believed in his intention before Allah (religiously) but not in a court of law unless evidence supports it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the default meaning of 'istata'a' in an oath?
A) The theological power created by Allah at the time of action
B) Soundness of health and means to perform the act
C) The ability to do something without any physical effort
D) The permission of the guardian

Key Takeaway
In oaths, 'ability' is interpreted as physical health and availability of means, not theological power; the judge does not accept a contrary religious intention.

Oath on Wife's Exit: Permission Required for Each Occasion
Ruling on oath 'except with my permission' requiring permission for each exit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of nakirah fi siyaq al-nafi to determine when an oath is broken, and distinguish between the rulings of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the legal stratagem (hila).

Core Idea
An oath's exception in a negative context makes each occurrence require separate permission.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on an oath where a husband says 'Do not go out except with my permission.' Using the usul principle of generality in negative contexts, we will see that each exit needs its own permission, except in emergencies such as flood or fire. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effectiveness of a legal stratagem (hila) and which view is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formula and its legal effect
The oath is 'La takhruj illa bi-idhni' – 'Do not go out except with my permission.' The exception is attached to the permission. Determine the ruling: does one permission cover all exits or only one?
The key is the preposition 'bi-' (with) indicating attachment.
2
Apply the usul principle of nakirah fi siyaq al-nafi
The excepted-from (khuruj) is an indefinite noun in a negative context, indicating generality (umum). Thus, every exit must have its own permission.
This is a foundational Hanafi usul principle for interpreting oaths and negations.
3
Note exceptions to the oath
If the wife exits due to a genuine emergency (e.g., flood or fire), the oath is not broken. The permission requirement is waived by necessity.
This is a condition for breaking the oath: the exit must not be for a predominantly life-threatening situation.
4
Determine when the oath dissolves
Once the wife exits without permission on one occasion, the oath is broken (hanith) and dissolves. Subsequent exits do not break the oath again.
The oath is a single vow; its violation completes the sin and requires expiation (kaffara).
5
Analyze the legal stratagem and the intra-Hanafi dispute
The husband can say: 'Whenever you intend to go out, I have given you permission.' Then if he later forbids her, Abu Yusuf holds the prohibition is ineffective, while Muhammad holds it overrides. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view.
This shows the importance of knowing which position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني) شَرطٌ [حَتّى لو أذِنَ بها مَرَّةً فَخَرَجَت ثُم خَرَجَت مَرَّةً أُخرى بِلا إذنٍ، حَنِثَ.] أي مَن حَلَف لا تَخرُج زَوجَتُهُ إلّا بِإذنِهِ، شَرطُ الإذنِ لِكُلِّ خُروجٍ؛ لأنَّ الباءَ لِلإلصاقِ فَمَعنى الحِلفِ لا تَخرُجي إلّا خُروجاً مُلصَقاً بِإذني، فَالمُستَثنى مِنهُ نَكِرَةٌ في سِياقِ النَّفي فَأفادَ العُمومَ، فَكُلُّ خُروجٍ لا يَكونُ بِالإذنِ كان داخِلاً في اليَمينِ، وَصارَ شَرطاً لِلحِنثِ. ويُشتَرَطُ أن لا يَكونَ الخُروجُ لِوُقوعِ غَرَقٍ أو حَرَقٍ غالِباً، فَإن كانَ لَم يَحنَث. ثُم إذا حَنِثَ بِخُروجِها مَرَّةً بِغَيْرِ إذنٍ لا يَحنَثُ بِخُروجِها مَرَّةً أُخرى؛ لِعَدَمِ ما يُوجِبُ التَّكرارَ وَانحَلَّت اليَمينُ بِالأوَّلِ. وَالحيلَةُ في ذلكَ أن يَقولَ لَها: كُلَّما أرَدتِ الخُروجَ فَقَد أذِنتُ لَكِ، فإن قالَ ذلكَ ثُم نَهاها لَم يَعمَل نَهيُهُ عِندَ أبي يُوسُفَ خِلافاً لِمُحَمَّدٍ. فَلَو قالَ: عَنيتُ الإذنَ مَرَّةً يُصَدَّقُ دِيانَةً وَقَضاءً عِندَ الطَّرَفَينِ، وَعَن أبي يُوسُفَ: أنَّهُ لا يُصَدَّقُ في القَضاءِ، وَعَلَيهِ الفُتوى. (فَتْح، عيني) بِخِلافِ إلّا أن إلخ أي بِخِلافِ مَا إذا حَلَف لا تَخرُجي إلّا أن آذَنَ لَكِ، فَأذِنَ لَها مَرَّةً وَحَتّى فَخَرَجَت ثُم خَرَجَت بَعدَهُ بِلا إذنٍ لَم يَحنَث؛ لِأنَّها بِالإذنِ مَرَّةً يَنتَهي اليَمينُ؛ لِأنَّ كَلِمَةَ "حَتّى" لِلغايَةِ فَيَنتَهي اليَمينُ بِها، وَكَلِمَةُ "إلّا أن" مَحمولَةٌ عَلَيها. فَإن قُلتَ: يَرِدُ عَلَيهِ قَولُهُ تَعالى: ﴿ولا تَدخُلوا بُيوتَ النَّبِيِّ إلّا أن يُؤذَنَ لَكُم﴾ (الأحزاب:53)، فَتكرارُ الإذنِ شَرطٌ لِجَوازِ الدُّخولِ. قُلتُ: تِكرارُ الإذنِ عُرِفَ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ مِن خارِجٍ، وَهُوَ أنَّ دُخولَ دارِ إنسانٍ بِغَيرِ إذنِهِ حَرامٌ. وَلَو نَوى التَّعَدُّدَ بِقَولِهِ: إلّا أن آذَنَ لَكِ صَدَّقَ قَضاءً؛ لِأنَّهُ مُحتَمَلُ كَلامِهِ. (عيني، فَتح)
She does not go out: That is, if he said in the oath regarding his wife: 'She does not go out' to his wife. ('Ayni) Condition [so that if he permitted her once and she went out, then she went out another time without permission, he breaks the oath.] That is, whoever swore that his wife does not go out except with his permission, the condition is permission for every exit, because the ba' (preposition) is for attachment, so the meaning of the oath is 'do not go out except an exit attached to my permission.' The excepted-from is indefinite (nakirah) in the context of negation, so it indicates generality (umum). Thus every exit that is not with permission is included in the oath, and it becomes a condition for breaking. It is required that the exit not be due to a flood or fire predominantly; if so, he does not break. Then, if he breaks the oath by her going out once without permission, he does not break again by another exit, because there is nothing requiring repetition and the oath is dissolved by the first. The legal trick (hila) for this is that he says to her: 'Whenever you intend to go out, I have given you permission.' If he says that then forbids her, his prohibition does not take effect according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. If he says: 'I meant permission one time,' he is believed religiously and legally according to both sides; from Abu Yusuf: he is not believed legally, and upon this is the fatwa. (Fath, 'Ayni) Contrary to 'except that' etc.: That is, contrary to when he swore 'Do not go out except that I give you permission,' and he gave permission once, and she went out, then she went out after that without permission – he does not break the oath, because by the permission once the oath ends, because the word 'hatta' (until) indicates a limit, so the oath ends with it, and the word 'illa an' is treated like it. If you say: this is contradicted by Allah's statement: 'Do not enter the houses of the Prophet except that you are given permission' (Al-Ahzab 53), so repetition of permission is a condition for entering. I say: repetition of permission is known from another external evidence, which is that entering someone's house without permission is forbidden. And if he intends multiplicity by his statement 'except that I give you permission,' he is believed legally because it is a possible meaning of his words. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about an oath concerning a wife's exit. If a man swears to his wife 'Do not go out except with my permission,' each separate exit requires its own permission because the negative generality (nakirah fi siyaq al-nafi) makes the exception apply to every instance. However, if an emergency like flooding or fire threatens, the oath is not broken. Once the wife goes out once without permission and the oath is broken, subsequent exits do not break it again because the oath is dissolved. A legal stratagem (hila) is: he says 'Whenever you intend to go out, I have given you permission.' Then if he later forbids her, according to Abu Yusuf the prohibition is ineffective, but Muhammad disagrees. The fatwa is that he is not believed in court if he claims he meant only one permission. The text also contrasts the formula 'illa an' (except that) with 'illa bi-idhni' (except with my permission). In 'illa an', a single permission suffices for all future exits because 'hatta' indicates an end, and 'illa an' is similar. The example of Surah al-Ahzab is addressed: the repetition of permission there is from external proof, not from the wording itself.

Key Terms
النكرة في سياق النفي (al-nakirah fī siyāq al-nafy): An indefinite noun in a negative context indicates generality (umum), making the oath cover every instance of the excepted act.
الحيلة (al-ḥīlah): A legal stratagem (hila) to avoid sin or break the oath without penalty; here, a general permission to exempt future exits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He breaks the oath for both exits
B) He breaks the oath for the first exit only
C) He does not break the oath at all because she went out
D) He breaks the oath only if she dies

Key Takeaway
An oath's exception in a negative context requires permission for each instance; once broken, the oath dissolves, and an emergency is an exception.

Meaning of Istita'ah in Oaths
Defining the term 'istita'ah' in the context of oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the customary meaning of istita'ah (sihhat al-asbab wa salamat al-alat) and the theological meaning (qudra) and state which meaning governs in Hanafi oaths for judicial versus conscientious purposes.

Core Idea
In oaths, istita'ah means physical capability, not divine power — custom prevails unless intention is made explicit.

This lesson explores the definition of 'istita'ah' (ability) in the context of Hanafi oaths. When a person swears 'if I am able,' what is the legally presumed meaning? The passage distinguishes between the customary meaning (soundness of means and limbs) and the theological meaning (power created by Allah at the moment of action). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the validity and consequences of conditional oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of istita'ah
The passage states that istita'ah is shared (mushtarak) between two meanings: (1) customary ability: soundness of causes and instruments (sihhat al-asbab wa salamat al-alat) and removal of physical obstacles; (2) divine power (qudra): the reality created by Allah at the moment of action concurrent with the act, as per Ahl al-Sunnah.
2
Determine the default meaning in oaths
The passage declares that in oaths, the intended meaning is the customary one because custom (urf) has made it the prevalent usage. When someone swears 'if I am able,' it is understood as physical ability, not the theological power.
This is based on the Hanafi usul principle that when a word is shared but one meaning is dominant in urf, that meaning is the apparent (zahir).
3
Analyze the effect of contrary intention
If the swearer intends the theological meaning (qudra) when uttering the oath, he is believed in conscience (diyanatan) because intention matters between him and Allah. However, in a judicial setting (qada'an), the court does not accept his claim because the customary meaning is the apparent one.
There is a minority view that he is believed judicially as well, but the passage rejects it because the word's usage without a contextual clue has become established for the customary meaning.
4
Understand the rationale
The ruling is based on the Hanafi principle that 'al-haqiqah al-urfiyyah tuqaddamu ala al-haqiqah al-lughawiyyah' (customary reality is preferred over linguistic reality) when no contextual clue indicates otherwise. The theological meaning is the linguistic reality but not the customary one.
5
Note the difference of opinion
The passage mentions that some hold the swearer is believed judicially because he intended the true (linguistic) meaning. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that he is not, because the customary meaning has become the apparent meaning, overriding the shared nature of the word.
This dispute is intra-Hanafi; the majority view is that the judge decides based on the apparent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي استطاعة الصحة وإن نوى القدرة دين. أي الاستطاعة. أي الحالف بالاستطاعة. أو بأمري أو رضائي. حلف فهي استطاعة الصحة لأنها في العرف سلامة الأسباب والآلات وارتفاع الموانع الحسية. أي من ليأتين زيداً غداً إن استطاع، فهذه الاستطاعة تكون استطاعة الصحة أي صحة أسباب الإتيان يعني تهيئه وسلامة الجوارح، فالممنوع والمريض ليس بمستطيع، والحاصل أن الاستطاعة مشتركة بين معنيين: أحدهما: القدرة يعني الحقيقة التي يحدثها الله سبحانه للعبد حالة الفعل مقارنة للفعل عند أهل السنة والجماعة، والثاني: صحة أسباب الإتيان وسلامة الآلات وارتفاع الموانع، وهذا المعنى هو المتعارف، فالمراد في الحلف المعنى المتعارف لكنه إن نوى بالاستطاعة القدرة يصدق ديانة، وقيل: يصدق قضاء أيضاً؛ لأنه نوى حقيقته وردّ بأنه وإن كان مشتركاً إلا أنه تعورف استعماله عند عدم القرينة للمعنى المتعارف، فصار ظاهراً فيه، فلا يصدقه القاضي في خلافه.
It is the ability of soundness, and if he intends the power, it is accepted in conscience. (That is, the ability. That is, the one swearing by ability. Or by my command or my pleasure. He swore: it is the ability of soundness because in custom it is safety of causes and instruments and removal of physical obstacles. That is, for one who says, 'I will certainly come to Zaid tomorrow if I am able,' this ability is the ability of soundness, meaning soundness of the means of coming, i.e., readiness and soundness of limbs, so the prevented and the sick are not able. The conclusion is that 'ability' is shared between two meanings: one is power, which is the reality that Allah creates for the servant at the moment of action concurrent with the action according to Ahl al-Sunnah; the second is soundness of means of performance and safety of instruments and removal of obstacles, and this meaning is the customary one. So in the oath, the intended meaning is the customary one, but if he intends ability as power, he is believed in conscience. It is said he is believed judicially as well because he intended its reality; but it is rejected that although it is shared, its usage without a contextual clue has become customary for the customary meaning, so it has become apparent in that, so the judge does not believe him in opposition to that.)
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'istita'ah' (ability) in the context of oaths. The ruling is that when a person swears 'if I am able' or similar, the intended meaning is the customary ability (istita'at al-sihha), i.e., physical readiness and absence of obstacles, not the theological ability (qudra) which is the power created by Allah at the moment of action. The oath is understood according to common usage. However, if the swearer intended the theological power, he is believed in conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an) because the customary meaning dominates. There is a difference of opinion: some say he is believed judicially too, but the relied-upon position is that he is not. This is based on the principle that when a word has both a technical and a customary meaning, the customary meaning prevails when no contextual clue indicates otherwise.

Key Terms
استطاعة (istita'ah): In this passage, istita'ah has two meanings: (1) customary ability (sihhat al-asbab wa salamat al-alat) – soundness of means and limbs, and (2) divine power (qudra) created by Allah at the moment of action. In oaths, the customary meaning is intended unless the swearer specifically intends the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, istita'ah is understood by its customary meaning (physical ability) unless the swearer specifically intends the theological power, but the court does not accept such intention.

Oath 'La Takhruj Illa Bi-Idhni' and Its Conditions
Ruling on a husband's oath restricting his wife's exit to his permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two oath formulas (illa bi-idhni vs illa an adhana) and apply the Hanafi ruling on breach and the legal stratagem.

Core Idea
The letter 'ba' in 'illa bi-idhni' demands permission for each exit; 'illa an adhana' ends the oath after one permission.

This passage examines two similar yet legally different oath formulas a husband might use to restrict his wife's exit. The Hanafi school employs linguistic analysis—specifically the function of the letter 'ba' for adhesion—to determine when a breach occurs. Additionally, the passage introduces a legal stratagem (hila) to dissolve the oath and reveals an intra-madhhab difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding its effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath formulas
Recognize 'illa bi-idhni' (except with my permission) and 'illa an adhana' (unless I permit). Note their distinct Arabic structures.
The difference hinges on the particle after 'illa'.
2
Apply the 'ba' of adhesion rule to 'illa bi-idhni'
The 'ba' in 'bi-idhni' indicates adhesion, meaning each exit must be 'attached to' a specific permission. Therefore, every exit requires permission; otherwise, the oath is breached.
The indefinite noun in a negative context (la takhruj) reinforces generality.
3
Distinguish the effect of 'illa an adhana' and rebut the Quranic objection
Here 'illa an' functions like 'hatta' (until), so once permission is given, the oath ends. The verse (33:53) is distinguished because repetition there is proven by external evidence.
The husband's intention may be considered if he intended multiplicity.
4
Analyze the legal stratagem and the intra-madhhab dispute
The husband can say 'whenever you want to go out, I have permitted you.' According to Abu Yusuf, this prevents subsequent prohibition from being effective; Muhammad disagrees. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf on judicial belief.
This shows how Hanafi law provides mechanisms to avoid unintended oath violations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تخرج: أي لو قال في الحلف على امرأته: 'لا تخرج' إلى امرأته. شرط [حتى لو أذن بها مرة فخرجت ثم خرجت مرة أخري بلا إذن، حنث.] أي من حلف لا تخرج زوجته إلا بإذنه، شرط الإذن لكل خروج ؛ لأن الباء للإلصاق فمعنى الحلف لا تخرجي إلا خروجاً ملصقاً بإذني، فالمستثنى منه نكرة في سياق النفي، فأفاد العموم، فكل خروج لا يكون بالإذن كان داخلاً في اليمين، وصار شرطاً للحنث، ويشترط أن لا يكون الخروج لوقوع غرق أو حرق غالباً، فإن كان لم يحنث، ثم إذا حنث بخروجها مرة بغير إذن لا يحنث بخروجها مرة أخرى؛ لعدم ما يوجب التكرار وانحلت اليمين بالأول، والحيلة في ذلك أن يقول لها: كلما أردت الخروج فقد أذنت لك، فإن قال ذلك ثم نهاها لم يعمل نهيه عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد، فلو قال: عنيت الإذن مرة يصدق ديانة وقضاء عند الطرفين، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يصدق في القضاء، وعليه الفتوى. (فتح، عيني) بخلاف إلا أن إلخ أي بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك، فأذن لها مرة وحتى فخرجت ثم خرجت بعده بلا إذن لم يحنث؛ لأنه بالإذن مرة ينتهي اليمين؛ لأن كلمة 'حتى' للغاية فينتهي اليمين بها، وكلمة 'إلا أن' محمولة عليها، فإن قلت يرد عليه قوله تعالى: {ولا تَدْخُلُوا بُيُوتَ النَّبِيِّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُؤْذَنَ لَكُمْ} (الأحزاب: ٥٣)، فتكرار الإذن شرط لجواز الدخول، قلت: تكرار الإذن عرف بدليل آخر من خارج، وهو أن دخول دار إنسان بغير إذنه حرام، ولو نوى التعدد بقوله: إلا أن آذن لك صدق قضاء؛ لأنه محتمل كلامه والرضا والأمر كالإذن فيما ذكرنا. (عيني، فتح)
'Do not go out': That is, if he said in the oath concerning his wife: 'Do not go out' to his wife. Condition: Even if he permitted her once and she went out, then she went out another time without permission, he incurs a breach. That is, whoever swears, 'My wife shall not go out except with my permission' requires permission for every exit; because the letter 'ba' is for adhesion, so the meaning of the oath is 'Do not go out except with an exit adhering to my permission.' The excluded item is indefinite in a negative context, so it indicates generality, thus every exit not with permission is included in the oath and becomes a condition for breach. It is conditioned that the exit not be due to a prevailing flood or fire; if so, he does not breach. Then, if he breaches by her exiting once without permission, he does not breach by her exiting another time, because there is nothing requiring repetition and the oath is dissolved by the first. The stratagem is that he says to her: 'Whenever you want to go out, I have permitted you.' If he says that and then forbids her, his prohibition is not effective according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. If he says, 'I meant permission once,' he is believed in conscience and judicially according to both; from Abu Yusuf, he is not believed in court, and the fatwa is upon that. Contrary to 'except that I permit' etc.: That is, contrary to if he swears, 'Do not go out unless I permit you,' and he permitted her once and she went out, then she went out afterward without permission, he does not breach; because with permission once the oath ends; because the word 'hatta' (until) indicates a limit, so the oath ends with it, and the word 'illa an' is carried on it. If you say, it is refuted by His saying, {Do not enter the houses of the Prophet unless you are permitted} (al-Ahzab 33:53), so repetition of permission is a condition for entry. I say: Repetition of permission is established by another external evidence, namely that entering someone's house without his permission is forbidden. If he intended multiplicity by saying 'except that I permit you,' he is believed judicially because his speech is probable, and consent and command are like permission in what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage discusses two types of oaths concerning a wife's exit. First, if a husband swears 'la takhruj illa bi-idhni' (do not go out except with my permission), the letter 'ba' implies adhesion, requiring permission for each and every exit. The condition for breach is that she exits without permission; once she does so, the oath is dissolved and further exits do not cause additional breaches. Exceptions exist for emergencies like flood or fire. A legal stratagem (hila) is for the husband to say 'Whenever you want to go out, I have permitted you,' which precludes future breaches. A difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad notes that if the husband later forbids her, Abu Yusuf says the prohibition is ineffective, while Muhammad disagrees. Second, if the husband uses the phrase 'illa an adhana' (unless I permit), once he permits her once, the oath ends and subsequent exits without permission do not cause a breach. This is because 'hatta' and 'illa an' indicate a limit. The verse about entering the Prophet's house is distinguished because the repetition there is established by another evidence (the general prohibition of entering without permission). If the husband intends multiplicity with 'illa an adhana', he is believed judicially as his words are ambiguous.

Key Terms
باء للإلصاق (ba' lil-ilṣāq): The letter 'ba' indicating adhesion or accompaniment, used to argue that each exit must be attached to permission.
حنث (ḥinth): Violation of an oath (causing the oath to be broken and requiring expiation in oaths by Allah, or divorce/emancipation in oaths by divorce/freeing).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband swears 'My wife shall not go out unless I permit her (illa an adhana).' She goes out once with his permission, then goes out again without permission. Does he breach the oath?
A) Yes, because each exit requires permission.
B) No, because the oath ended with the first permission.
C) Yes, because 'illa an' implies repetition.
D) No, because the wife's action is not considered.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between oath formulas based on linguistic precision, allowing legal stratagems and recognizing intra-madhhab differences.

Yamin al-Faur: The Immediate Oath
Explaining the concept of an oath of immediacy (yamin al-faur) with examples and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a yamin al-faur scenario, apply its ruling to given facts, and distinguish it from general oaths using Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
An oath limited to the immediate moment by custom and context is a uniquely Hanafi category.

This lesson explores a distinctive Hanafi contribution to oath jurisprudence: the immediate oath (yamin al-faur). When an oath is made in direct response to an imminent action, the school holds that it applies only to that specific instance, not to future occurrences. We will examine the evidence from hadith, the reasoning based on custom (urf), and the practical application through concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Define yamin al-faur
Understand the definition: an oath triggered by an immediate situation, applying only to that moment, derived from custom and context.
Derived from 'fara al-qidr' (pot boiling) metaphor for speed.
2
Review the examples
Analyze the three examples: wife about to go out, hitting a slave, and meal invitation response. Note the common element: the oath is a direct response to an imminent act.
Response to invitation ('sit and eat with me') also qualifies.
3
Examine the hadith proof
Read the hadith of Jabir and his son: they swore not to help a man, then helped later without breach. This shows the oath was contextually limited.
Hadith is ahad, but accepted as sound basis for the category.
4
Contrast with other schools
Note that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) treat general oaths as general regardless of context. Understand the basis of difference: literal vs. contextual interpretation.
Hanafi position is mufta bihi and accepted by later scholars.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Practice identifying yamin al-faur in novel situations: look for immediacy, context of prevention, and response to an imminent action.
Key test: Would the speaker reasonably intend only the present instance?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ضرب العبد فقال: إن ضربت تقيد به كاجلس فتغد عندي، فقال: إن تغديت فعبدي حر تقید به أي إذا أرادت المرأة أن تخرج فقال له الزوج إن خرجت فأنت طالق أو عبيده أحرار، أو أرادت بضرب عبدها فقال: إن ضربت فأنت طالق أو عبدي حر، تقيد اليمين بهذه المرة؛ لأن مراد المتكلم المنع عن هذا الخروج وعن هذا الضرب عرفاً، والأيمان مبناه على العرف حتى لو مكثت ساعة ثم خرجت أو ضربت لا يحنث، وهذه يمين الفور مأخوذة من فارت القدر، إذا غلت فاستعيرت للسرعة ثم سميت به الحالة التي لا ريث فيها ولا مكث ويقال جاء فلان من فوره أي من ساعته، ويقدر الفور بساعة. وتفرد أبو حنيفة بإظهاره، وكان العلماء قبله يقولون اليمين إما مؤبدة كلا يفعل، أو مؤقتة كلا يفعل اليوم، فخرج أبو حنيفة قسماً ثالثاً وهو المؤقتة معنى المعلقة لفظاً، وإنما أخذه من حديث جابر الله وابنه حين دعيا إلى نصرة رجل، فحلفا أن لا ينصراه ثم نصراه بعد ذلك، ولم يحنثا، و لم يسبق أبا حنيفة أحد إليها لا في تسميتها ولا في حكمها، ولم يخالفه أحد، فاس كلهم عيال أبي حنيفة، ثم تصوير المسألة الثانية بأن أرادت المرأة ضرب عبدها إلخ موافق لشرح ملا مسكين وفي 'العيني' و'المستخلص' تصويرها أو أراد رجل ضرب العبد فقال الآخر: إن ضربت العبد فعبده حر، والمآل واحد. (فتح محشي) ركب إن تغديت أي من قال لآخر: اجلس فتغد معي فقال: 'إن تغديت فعبدي حر' تقيدت اليمين بهذه المرة، حتى لو تغدى في بيته، لم يحنث؛ لأن كونه مجيباً أخرج الكلام عن العموم؛ إذ الجواب يقتضي إعادة ما في السؤال، فكأنه قال: إن تغديت معك هذه المرة، وإنما شبه المصنف هذه المسألة بالتي تقدمها؛ لأن أصلهما واحد، وهو ترك الحقيقة بدلالة من جهة المتكلم. (مستخلص، فتح)
Or hitting the slave: he said, 'If you hit, it is restricted' like 'Sit and have lunch with me,' and he said, 'If I have lunch, my slave is free.' It is restricted to this time; meaning, if the woman intended to go out and the husband said, 'If you go out, you are divorced,' or 'my slaves are free,' or she intended to hit her slave and he said, 'If you hit, you are divorced,' or 'my slave is free,' the oath is restricted to this instance; because the speaker's intent customarily is to prevent this particular exit or this particular hit, and oaths are based on custom. Even if she waited an hour and then went out or hit, she does not breach. This is the 'yamin al-faur' (immediate oath), derived from 'fara al-qidr' (the pot boiled) metaphor for speed; it is named for the state with no delay. It is said, 'He came in his fur' meaning immediately. The immediacy is estimated as one hour. Abu Hanifa uniquely brought it forth. Before him, scholars said oaths are either perpetual like 'he will never do,' or temporary like 'he will not do today.' Abu Hanifa extracted a third type: temporary in meaning, general in wording. He took it from the hadith of Jabir and his son when they were called to help a man, and they swore not to help him, then later helped him and did not breach. No one preceded Abu Hanifa in naming or ruling, and no one opposed him; all are dependent on Abu Hanifa. Then the depiction of the second issue—that the woman intended to hit her slave etc.—agrees with the commentary of Mulla Miskin. In 'al-Ayni' and 'al-Mustakhlas', it is depicted as a man intending to hit a slave and another said, 'If you hit the slave, then my slave is free,' and the outcome is the same. (Fath Mahshi) 'Rakiba': 'In taghaddayt': Someone said to another, 'Sit and have lunch with me,' and he replied, 'If I have lunch, my slave is free.' The oath is restricted to this instance; even if he eats lunch in his own house, he does not breach. Because being a respondent removes the statement from generality; the answer necessitates repeating what is in the question, so it is as if he said, 'If I have lunch with you this time.' The author likens this issue to the preceding one because both rely on abandoning the literal meaning due to an indication from the speaker.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'yamin al-faur' (immediate oath), which is a unique contribution of Imam Abu Hanifa. The idea is that when an oath is made in response to a specific imminent action—like a wife about to go out, or a slave about to be hit—the oath applies only to that immediate instance, not to future actions. This is based on custom ('urf) because the speaker's intent is to prevent that particular act. If the wife waits even an hour before going out, the oath is not breached. Abu Hanifa inferred this from the hadith of Jabir and his son who swore not to help a man but later helped him without breach. Thus, he recognized a third category of oath: temporary in meaning but general in wording (muwaqqata ma'nan mu'allaqa lafzan). This is a distinct Hanafi position not found in other schools. The passage gives three examples: (1) a wife about to go out, (2) hitting a slave, and (3) a response to an invitation to eat. In the last case, if someone is invited and replies 'if I eat, my slave is free,' the oath is limited to that meal, even if he eats elsewhere. All these cases share the principle of leaving the literal meaning due to an indication from the speaker's context.

Key Terms
يمين الفور (yamīn al-fawr): An oath that is triggered by an imminent action and applies only to that immediate instance, not to future similar actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband sees his wife about to leave the house and says, 'If you go out, you are divorced.' She instead leaves an hour later. Is the divorce valid?
A) Yes, because the oath is general.
B) No, because it is a yamin al-faur limited to the immediate instance.
C) Yes, because divorce is effective regardless of timing.
D) No, because she did not go out at all.

Key Takeaway
Yamin al-faur is a unique Hanafi doctrine where an oath made in response to an imminent action is limited to that moment, based on custom and the hadith of Jabir.
