Riding the Slave's Mount: Intra-Hanafi Differences in Oath Breach
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath breach by riding slave's mount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding when riding a slave's mount breaches an oath not to ride the master's mount, and apply the ruling to given facts.

Core Idea
An oath not to ride the master's mount is breached by riding the slave's mount only if intention aligns — but the three Hanafi masters disagree on the role of debt and intent.

This passage examines a common scenario in oaths: someone swears not to ride Zaid's mount, then rides the mount of Zaid's slave. The ruling is not uniform even within the Hanafi school. Understanding the nuances of intention and debt is essential for applying the correct fatwa. We will walk through the four-case analysis of Abu Hanifa and compare it with the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, noting which position later authorities preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the act
The swearer says: 'I will not ride Zaid's mount.' He then rides the mount of Zaid's authorized slave. The question is whether this act breaches the oath.
The slave's mount is treated differently because it is customarily attributed to the slave, not to the master.
2
Identify the variables
Two key variables: (1) whether the slave has a debt that consumes his entire capital and earnings (dayn mustaghriq), and (2) whether the swearer intended the slave's mount when he swore.
These variables create four scenarios in Abu Hanifa's analysis.
3
Map Abu Hanifa's four-case analysis
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: (a) Debt consuming + any intention → no breach (mount not master's property). (b) No consuming debt + intended master's own mount → no breach (specific intention excludes slave's mount). (c) No consuming debt + no specific intention → no breach (custom attributes mount to slave). (d) No consuming debt + intended slave's mount → breach. Only case (d) results in breach.
Intention is crucial; without it, custom overrides legal ownership.
4
Contrast Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that if the swearer intends the slave's mount, he breaches regardless of debt. Debt does not prevent ownership; only the master's ownership matters. If he does not intend, there is no breach (similar to Abu Hanifa's case c).
Abu Yusuf reduces the variables: intention alone triggers breach, debt is irrelevant.
5
Contrast Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that the swearer breaches in all cases, even without intention and regardless of debt. The slave's property is in the master's ownership; therefore riding any mount of the slave is riding the master's mount for oath purposes.
This is the strictest view, emphasizing legal reality over custom.
6
Identify the mufta bihi (operative position)
Later commentators (Fath, Ayni) note that the three (meaning Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agreed with Muhammad's view 'considering the reality' (i'tibaran li al-haqiqah). This indicates that the operative fatwa leans toward Muhammad's position, though Abu Hanifa's detailed analysis remains the theoretical framework.
In practice, many later Hanafi fatwas adopt Muhammad's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومركب عبده كمركبه في الحنث إن نوى ولا دين به. أي عبد شخص مثل مركب الشخص فيتناوله اللفظ ويدخل فيها المخاطب. صورته لو حلف أن لا يركب دابة زيد فركب دابة عبده المأذون له، وههنا صور أربع: الأولى: أن يكون على العبد دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه، فلا يحنث لأن هذا المركب ليس له الثانية: أن لا يكون عليه دين مستغرق، ولكن نوى مركب زيد مركبه الخاص به، فلا يحنث أيضاً، الثالثة: أن لا يكون عليه دين ولم ينو مرتك العبد، فلا يحنث؛ لأن الملك وإن كان لفلان، لكن يضاف إلى العبد عرفاً فاختلت الإضافة إلى المولى، فبدون النية لا يتناوله اللفظ، الرابعة: أن لا يكون على العبد دين مستغرق ونوى مركب العبد، يحنث بركوبه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف: يحنث إن نوى، سواء كان عليه دين أو لا؛ لأن استغراق كسبه بالدين لا يمنع ثبوت الملك للمولى، وعند محمد: يحنث بكل حال وإن لم ينو؛ إذ العبد وما في يده ملك لسيده والدين غير مانع من ثبوت الملك، وإنما قال: مركب عبده؛ لأنه إن ركب مركب مكاتبه لا يحنث عندهم، وقالت الثلاثة على وفق قول محمد: يحنث في الوجوه كلها اعتباراً للحقيقة. (فتح، عيني) ولا دين: أي والحال أنه لا دين على العبد أصلاً أو كان ولكن لم يستغرق. (ط، عيني)
And the mount of his slave is like his own mount in causing breach if he intends and there is no debt on the slave. That is, a person's slave is like the person himself, so the wording includes it and the addressee enters into it. Its scenario: if he swore that he would not ride Zaid's mount, and he rode the mount of Zaid's authorized slave. Here are four scenarios: First: if the slave has debt consuming his capital and earnings, then he does not breach because this mount is not his. Second: if he does not have consuming debt, but he intended Zaid's own mount, he also does not breach. Third: if there is no debt and he did not intend the slave's mount, he does not breach; because even though ownership is for someone, the mount is customarily attributed to the slave, so the attribution to the master is disrupted; thus without intention the wording does not include it. Fourth: if there is no consuming debt and he intended the slave's mount, he breaches by riding it. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf: he breaches if he intends, whether there is debt or not; because the consumption of his earnings by debt does not prevent the establishment of ownership for the master. According to Muhammad: he breaches in all cases even if he did not intend; since the slave and what is in his hand are owned by his master, and debt does not prevent ownership. He (the author) said 'the mount of his slave' because if he rides the mount of his mukatib, he does not breach according to them. The three (imams besides Abu Hanifa) according to Muhammad's saying: he breaches in all cases, considering the reality. (Fath, Ayni) 'And no debt': that is, while there is no debt on the slave at all, or it exists but does not consume (the value). (Tah, Ayni)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath like 'I will not ride Zaid's mount' and the swearer rides the mount of Zaid's slave. The ruling depends on the slave's debt situation and intention. Abu Hanifa distinguishes four cases: (1) if the slave's debt consumes his entire value and earnings, the mount is not considered the master's, so no breach; (2) if no consuming debt but the swearer intended specifically the master's own mount, no breach; (3) if no debt and no specific intention, still no breach because custom attributes the mount to the slave; (4) if no debt and he intended the slave's mount, then he breaches. Abu Yusuf says he breaches if he intends the slave's mount, regardless of debt. Muhammad says he breaches in all cases, even without intention, because the slave's property is owned by the master. The author notes that the mukatib (contractually freeing) is excluded. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) are said to follow Muhammad's view in all cases, considering the reality of ownership—meaning the fatwa aligns with the stricter view that the slave's mount is effectively the master's. However, the primary position (Abu Hanifa) distinguishes based on debt and intention.

Key Terms
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that consumes the slave's entire capital and earnings, such that nothing is left for the master.
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments; his property is not considered his master's in this context.
حنث (hanth): Breach of an oath, requiring expiation (kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario does Abu Hanifa say riding a slave's mount breaches the oath?
A) When the slave has a consuming debt and the swearer intended the slave's mount.
B) When there is no consuming debt and the swearer intended the slave's mount.
C) When the swearer intended the master's mount.
D) In all cases, regardless of debt or intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's primary analysis (Abu Hanifa) distinguishes four cases on debt and intention, but later fatwa adopts Muhammad's view that riding a slave's mount always breaches the oath.

The Oath of Immediacy (Yamīn al-Fawr) and Its Proof
Defines yamīn al-fawr with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define yamīn al-fawr and explain its proof from the hadith of Jabir and his son.

Core Idea
An oath to prevent an action may be restricted to immediate performance based on custom, not perpetual.

This lesson introduces a unique Hanafi classification of oaths: yamīn al-fawr (oath of immediacy). Imam Abu Hanifa distinguished it from perpetual and time-bound oaths, grounding it in custom and a hadith. Understanding this category clarifies when an oath is broken and when it is not, based on the speaker's intent and customary context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of oaths
Before Abu Hanifa, scholars only recognized perpetual oaths (e.g., 'I will never do X') and time-bound oaths (e.g., 'I will not do X today'). Abu Hanifa added a third: the oath of immediacy (yamīn al-fawr), which is conditional in wording but time-bound in meaning.
This third category is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the definition of al-fawr
The term al-fawr is derived from the bubbling of a pot (fārat al-qidr), metaphorically meaning speed or immediacy. It is estimated as approximately one hour, based on custom and the speaker's intent.
The temporal estimate is based on common practice, not a fixed textual limit.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith of Jabir and his son: They were called to help a man and swore they would not help him, but later they helped him and were not considered oath-breakers. This shows their oath was understood to be for that specific immediate occasion, not a permanent prohibition.
The hadith is cited as evidence for the concept of immediacy in oaths.
4
Apply the rule to examples
A husband says to his wife, 'If you go out, you are divorced,' when she intends to go out at that moment. This is an oath of immediacy: if she goes out immediately, she breaks the oath; if she delays an hour, she does not. The same applies to hitting a slave.
The ruling is based on custom and the speaker's apparent intent to prevent the specific instance.
5
Note the authority
Abu Hanifa alone brought this classification to light, and no scholar before him had articulated it. The Hanafi school unanimously accepts it, and it is the mufta bihi position.
Other madhhabs do not recognize this third category; they treat all oaths as either perpetual or time-bound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الدخول والسكنى.... أو ضرب العبد فقال: إن ضربت تقيد به كاجلس فتغد عندي، فقال: إن تغديت الزوج فعبدي حر ومركب عبده كمركبه في الحنث إن نوى ولا دين به. أي عبد شخص مثل مركب الشخص فيتناوله اللفظ ويدخل فيها المخاطب تقید به أي إذا أرادت المرأة أن تخرج فقال له الزوج إن خرجت فأنت طالق أو عبيده أحرار، أو أرادت بضرب عبدها فقال: إن ضربت فأنت طالق أو عبدي، حر، تقيد اليمين بهذه المرة؛ لأن مراد المتكلم المنع عن هذا الخروج وعن هذا الضرب عرفاً، والأيمان مبناه على العرف حتى لو مكثت ساعة ثم خرجت أو ضربت لا يحنث، وهذه يمين الفور مأخوذة من فارت القدر إذا غلت فاستعيرت للسرعة ثم سميت به الحالة التي لا ريث فيها ولا مكث ويقال جاء فلان من فوره أي من ساعته، ويقدر الفور بساعة. وتفرد أبو حنيفة بإظهاره، وكان العلماء قبله يقولون اليمين إما مؤبدة كلا يفعل، أو مؤقتة كلا يفعل اليوم، فخرج أبو حنيفة قسماً ثالثاً وهو المؤقتة معنى المعلقة لفظاً، وإنما أخذه من حديث جابر رضي الله عنه وابنه حين دعيا إلى نصرة رجل، فحلفا أن لا ينصراه ثم نصراه بعد ذلك، ولم يحنثا، ولم يسبق أبا حنيفة أحد إليها لا في تسميتها ولا في حكمها، ولم يخالفه أحد، فالناس كلهم عيال أبي حنيفة، ثم تصوير المسألة الثانية بأن أرادت المرأة ضرب عبدها إلخ موافق لشرح ملا مسكين وفي "العيني" و"المستخلص" تصويرها أو أراد رجل ضرب العبد فقال الآخر: إن ضربت العبد فعبده حر، والمآل واحد. (فتح محشي)
Chapter of oath regarding entering and dwelling... or hitting the slave, he said: 'If I hit, it is restricted' like 'sit and eat at my place', he said: 'If I eat, my slave is free' and the mount of his slave is like his mount in breaking the oath if he intends, and there is no debt on it. That is, any slave person is like the person's mount, so the word includes it and includes the addressee. It is restricted, meaning if the woman wanted to go out and the husband said to her: 'If you go out, you are divorced' or 'his slaves are free', or she intended to hit her slave, he said: 'If you hit, you are divorced' or 'my slave is free', the oath is restricted to this time; because the speaker's intent is to prevent this specific going out and this specific hitting according to custom, and oaths are based on custom, so if she stayed an hour and then went out or hit, she does not break the oath. This is yamīn al-fawr, derived from 'fārat al-qidr' (when the pot boils), metaphorically used for speed, then called the state in which there is no delay or stay, and it is said: he came from his fawr, i.e., immediately, and fawr is estimated as one hour. Abu Hanifa alone brought it to light. Scholars before him said the oath is either perpetual like 'he will never do' or time-bound like 'he will not do today'. Abu Hanifa introduced a third category: time-bound in meaning but conditional in wording. He took it from the hadith of Jabir (may Allah be pleased with him) and his son when they were called to help a man, and they swore they would not help him, then later they helped him and did not break their oath. No one preceded Abu Hanifa in this, neither in its name nor its ruling, and no one opposed him; all people are dependent on Abu Hanifa. Then the depiction of the second issue that the woman intended to hit her slave etc. is in agreement with the commentary of Mulla Miskin, and in al-‘Aynī and al-Mustakhlas it is depicted as a man intending to hit the slave and the other said: 'If you hit the slave, his slave is free', and the outcome is one. (Fath Mahshi)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of yamīn al-fawr (oath of immediacy) and presents it as a distinct category introduced by Imam Abu Hanifa. It begins by illustrating the ruling with examples: a husband prohibiting his wife from going out or hitting a slave, where the oath is tied to a specific instance. The key principle is that such oaths are based on custom ('urf); if the action is delayed beyond approximately one hour, the oath is not broken. The derivation of the term al-fawr from boiling pot metaphor is explained. The passage then highlights Abu Hanifa's unique contribution: before him, scholars only recognized perpetual or time-bound oaths; Abu Hanifa added a third type where the oath is conditional in wording but time-bound in meaning. The proof is the hadith of Jabir, where the Companions swore not to help a man but later helped him without breaking the oath, indicating that the oath was for that specific occasion. The text emphasizes that no one opposed Abu Hanifa's classification, and it provides a secondary illustration to harmonize different wordings of the same principle.

Key Terms
يمين الفور (yamīn al-fawr): An oath that is restricted to immediate performance, typically based on custom and the speaker's intent to prevent a specific action at a specific time.
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy, estimated as approximately one hour, derived from the bubbling of a pot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a woman subject to an oath of immediacy stays an hour and then goes out?
A) She breaks the oath and must expiate.
B) She does not break the oath because the oath was tied to immediate action.
C) She only breaks the oath if she intended to go out at the exact moment.
D) She must consult a scholar to determine if the oath is broken.

Key Takeaway
Yamīn al-fawr is a uniquely Hanafi category that restricts an oath's effect to immediate performance based on custom and the speaker's intent, supported by the hadith of Jabir.

Riding a Slave's Mount: Four Scenarios and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute with four scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios regarding riding a slave's mount in an oath and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
In oaths, custom ('urf) can override literal ownership; Abu Hanifa considers intention and debt, while Sahibayn rely on strict ownership.

This lesson explores a nuanced issue in Hanafi fiqh: if a person swears not to ride Zaid's mount, does riding a mount owned by Zaid's slave break the oath? The answer depends on whether the slave has a covering debt and the oath-taker's intention. The Hanafi masters differed on the weight of these factors, and their disagreement reveals deeper principles about the role of custom and literal meaning in legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the oath context and base text
The passage begins with an analogy: an oath about eating with someone is restricted to that specific meal because of the context. Similarly, the main issue concerns an oath not to ride Zaid's mount, but the mount belongs to Zaid's slave. Understand that the base matn is just 'مركب عبده' and the commentary expands it into four scenarios.
The prior example establishes that context can narrow an oath's scope.
2
List the four scenarios
The commentary presents four scenarios based on two variables: whether the slave has a debt that covers his entire value and earnings (mustaghraq) and whether the oath-taker intended the slave's mount. Write them out clearly: (1) debt + no intention, (2) no debt + intended master's mount, (3) no debt + no intention, (4) no debt + intended slave's mount.
Only scenario (4) breaks the oath according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Compare the positions of the three imams
For each scenario, state the ruling according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Abu Hanifa: only scenario (4) breaks. Abu Yusuf: breaks if intention present, regardless of debt. Muhammad: always breaks regardless of debt or intention.
The 'three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and the later school) adopt Muhammad's view in all cases.
4
Analyze the underlying principles
Abu Hanifa relies on 'urf (custom): the mount is customarily attributed to the slave, so the oath's wording does not include it unless intention specifies. Muhammad relies on literal ownership: the slave's property is actually the master's, so any riding breaks the oath. Abu Yusuf splits the difference, focusing on intention.
This reflects a broader methodological difference between qiyas and istihsan.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical effect
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi madhhab is Abu Hanifa's, as confirmed by later authorities. The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, one can avoid breaking the oath by not intending the slave's mount, whereas under Muhammad, any riding requires expiation.
This shows how 'urf can mitigate strict ownership in oath contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(مستخلص، فتح) ومركب عبده إلخ صورته لو حلف أن لا يركب دابة زيد فركب دابة عبده المأذون له، وههنا صور أربع: الأولى: أن يكون على العبد دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه، فلا يحنث لأن هذا المركب ليس له الثانية: أن لا يكون عليه دين مستغرق، ولكن نوى مركب زيد مركبه الخاص به، فلا يحنث أيضاً، الثالثة: أن لا يكون عليه دين ولم ينو مرتك العبد، فلا يحنث؛ لأن الملك وإن كان لفلان، لكن يضاف إلى العبد عرفاً فاختلت الإضافة إلى المولى، فبدون النية لا يتناوله اللفظ، الرابعة: أن لا يكون على العبد دين مستغرق ونوى مركب العبد، يحنث بركوبه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف: يحنث إن نوى، سواء كان عليه دين أو لا؛ لأن استغراق كسبه بالدين لا يمنع ثبوت الملك للمولى، وعند محمد: يحنث بكل حال وإن لم ينو؛ إذ العبد وما في يده ملك لسيده والدين غير مانع من ثبوت الملك، وإنما قال: مركب عبده؛ لأنه إن ركب مركب مكاتبه لا يحنث عندهم، وقالت الثلاثة على وفق قول محمد: يحنث في الوجوه كلها اعتباراً للحقيقة. (فتح، عيني) ولا دين: أي والحال أنه لا دين على العبد أصلاً أو كان ولكن لم يستغرق. (ط، عيني)
Riding: 'If I eat' that is, someone said to another: 'Sit and eat with me', and he said: 'If I eat, my slave is free', the oath is restricted to this time, such that even if he ate at his home, he would not break the oath; because being a respondent removed the generality of the speech; since the answer implies repeating what was in the question, so it is as if he said: 'If I eat with you this time'. The author likened this issue to the one preceding it because their basis is one: abandoning the literal meaning due to an indication from the speaker. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'And the mount of his slave' etc.: its form is: if someone swore not to ride Zaid's mount and then rode the mount of Zaid's authorized slave. There are four scenarios here: First: that the slave has a debt covering his value and earnings, then he does not break the oath because this mount is not his. Second: that there is no debt covering, but he intended Zaid's own mount, so he also does not break. Third: that there is no debt and he did not intend the slave's mount, so he does not break; because ownership is for Zaid, but it is customarily attributed to the slave, so the attribution to the master is weakened; without intention, the word does not include it. Fourth: that there is no covering debt and he intended the slave's mount, then he breaks by riding it. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf: he breaks if he intends, whether there is debt or not, because the debt covering earnings does not negate ownership for the master. According to Muhammad: he breaks in all cases even without intention, because the slave and what he possesses are the master's property, and debt does not prevent ownership. And he only said 'mount of his slave' because if he rode the mount of his mukatab, he does not break according to them. The three (i.e., Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and the opposing school) said according to Muhammad's statement: he breaks in all ways, considering the literal meaning. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'And there is no debt': meaning the state that there is no debt on the slave initially, or if there is, it is not covering. (T, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses two related issues: the first is an oath about eating with someone, where being a respondent restricts the oath to that specific meal (like the earlier discussion of immediacy). The main focus is the second issue: an oath not to ride Zaid's mount. The commentator presents four scenarios based on whether the slave has a debt covering his value, and whether the oath-taker intended the slave's mount. Abu Hanifa's position is that only the fourth scenario (no covering debt and intention) breaks the oath. Abu Yusuf considers intention sufficient regardless of debt, while Muhammad holds that any riding of the slave's mount breaks the oath. The text notes that the three (presumably Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and the opposing school) adopt Muhammad's view in all cases, sticking to the literal meaning. The note clarifies that 'no debt' means either no debt or a debt that does not cover the slave's entire value.

Key Terms
مركب العبد (markab al-'abd): A mount that belongs to a slave (owned by a master).
مستغرق (mustaghraq): A debt that covers the entire value of the slave and his earnings, making the slave effectively owned by creditors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the slave has a covering debt and the oath-taker intended the slave's mount.
B) When the slave has no covering debt and the oath-taker intended the slave's mount.
C) When the oath-taker did not intend any specific mount.
D) In all scenarios except when the slave has a debt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the interplay of custom, ownership, and intention determines whether riding a slave's mount violates an oath not to ride the master's mount.

Oaths on Eating: Definitions, Trees, and Loaves
Definitions and rulings on oaths regarding consumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of eating and drinking, identify when an oath on an inedible tree extends to its fruit by consensus, and apply the principle of partial consumption to oaths involving loaves.

Core Idea
An oath on an inedible tree figuratively covers its fruit by consensus; each substance's mode of consumption defines the oath's scope.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq clarifies foundational definitions and key rulings in the chapter on oaths. We first learn how Hanafi fiqh distinguishes eating, drinking, tasting, swallowing, and sucking. Then we see how these definitions apply when a person swears not to eat from a specific source like a palm tree, and how consensus (ijma) binds the ruling. Finally, the passage introduces the critical principle of partial consumption in oaths involving whole loaves, a scenario common in conditional divorce or manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the Arabic terms for eating (akl), drinking (shurb), tasting (dhawq), swallowing (ibtila'), and sucking (mass). Note the specific characteristics: akl requires chewability; shurb involves non-crushable liquid; dhawq uses only the lips; ibtila' uses only the throat; mass uses the palate.
These definitions are fundamental for determining what act fulfills an oath.
2
Analyze the palm tree ruling
The text states: 'this palm tree, he breaks the oath by its fruit.' Understand that because the tree itself is inedible, the oath is figuratively extended to its produce (fruit) by consensus. Read the commentary: all types of dates, pollen, etc. are included, but processed products like wine or vinegar are not.
The consensus proof (ijma) gives the ruling certainty.
3
Distinguish edible vs. inedible objects
Contrast the palm tree with an edible object like a sheep. For an edible object, the oath stays on the meat, not the byproducts (milk, butter). For an inedible tree with no fruit (e.g., willow), the oath turns to its monetary value.
This distinction hinges on whether the object itself is consumed.
4
Apply the loaf principle
Examine the sub-case (far') about a conditional oath on a loaf. If a person swears to eat the whole loaf or face divorce/freeing, eating half does not break the oath because the condition requires total consumption. But if someone says 'this loaf is haram for me,' eating half breaks it. The general rule: if the item is normally consumed entirely in one session, the oath covers all; otherwise, only part.
This principle is used in fatwa; the text says 'الفتوى على ذلك'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب. باب اليمين أي في بيان أحكامها في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام هذه النخلة حنث بثمرها، ولو عين البسر أو الكرم بأن حلف لأكل هذا البسر هذه لا يأكل من باب اليمين إلخ ذكر الأكل والشرب بعد الخروج؛ لأنه إنما يراد منه غالباً تحصيل ما به إبقاء البينة . المأكول من والمشروب، ولا خفاء في احتياجه إلى اللبس والكلام فلا جرم إن ذكرهما بعده ثم الأكل: إيصال ما يتأتى فيه المضغ إلى الجوف ممضوغاً كان أو غير ممضوغ والشرب: إيصال ما لا يتأتى فيه الهشم إلى الجوف، والذوق: إيصال الشيء إلى فمه لاستبانة طعمه، وقيل: الأكل عمل الشفاه والحلق والذوق عمل الشفاه دون الحلق، والابتلاع: عمل الحلق دون الشفاه والمص : عمل اللهات، وكل أكل ذوق ولا عكس رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٤/١] (فتح) لا يأكل: أي لو حلف رجل لا يأكل إلخ. (عيني) حنث بثمرها : [ أي بأكل ثمرها؛ لانصراف يمينه إليه مجازاً بالإجماع (عيني)] أي لو حلف لا يأكل من النخلة يحنث بجميع ما يخرج منها من جمار أو بسر أو رطب أو طلع أو دبس، وكذا إذا قال: من هذا الكرم ينصرف إلى ما يخرج منه من عنب وحصرم؛ لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى النخلة وهي لا توكل، فتنصرف إلى ما يخرج منها؛ لأنها سبب للتمر فجازت الاستعارة، والثمر بالمثلثة ما يخرج منها حيث لم يتغير بصنعة جديدة، فلا يحنث بالنبيذ والخل والناطف والدبس المطبوخ، وقيد بما لا تؤكل عينه أي النخلة؛ لأنه لو حلف على ما تؤكل عينه كهذه الشاة انصرفت اليمين إلى اللحم دون اللبن والزبد؛ لأنها مأكولة فينعقد اليمين عليها. ولو لم يكن للشجرة ثمر كالصفصاف ونحوه ينصرف اليمين إلى ثمنها، وإن أكل من عين النخلة لا يحنث وإن نواها؛ لأن الحقيقة مهجورة بدلالة محل الكلام، وأشار بثمرها إلى أنه لو قطع غصناً منها فوصله بشجرة أخرى فأكل من ثمر هذا الغصن لا يحنث، وقال بعضهم يحنث. فرع: قال: إن أكلت هذا الرغيف اليوم فامرأته طالق ثلاثاً وإن لم يأكله اليوم فأمته حرة فأكل النصف لم يحنث؛ لانعدام شرط الحنث في اليمينين، وهو أكل الكل أو ترك الكل، قال: هذا الرغيف علي حرام فأكل بعضه حنث، بخلاف لا اكل هذا الرغيف إذا كان كله مما يؤكل في مجلس واحد والفتوى على ذلك، والأصل أنه إذا حلف لا يأكل معيناً فأكل بعضه فإن كان مما يوكل كله عادة فالحلف على كله، وإلا فعلى بعضه. (عيني، فتح) ولو عين البسر إلخ الأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أن اليمين إذا عقدت على شيء يوصف، فإن صلح داعياً إلى اليمين يتقيد به، سواء كان معرفاً أو منكراً، وإن لم يصلح داعياً فإن كان منكراً تتقيد أيضاً، والبسر: بضم الموحدة وسكون المهملة جمع بسرة يقال لها بالفارسية: غوره خرما. (عيني، فتح)
Book of Oaths. Chapter on oath regarding eating and drinking. Chapter on oath, i.e., in explaining its rulings regarding eating, drinking, clothing, and speech. 'This palm tree, he breaks the oath by its fruit.' And if he specified unripe dates or the grapevine, by swearing to eat this unripe date... 'He will not eat' from the chapter of oath etc. He mentioned eating and drinking after going out because through them one usually preserves life from food and drink, and there is no doubt of his need for clothing and speech, so indeed he mentioned them after. Then eating: delivering what can be chewed to the stomach, chewed or not. Drinking: delivering what cannot be crushed to the stomach. Tasting: delivering something to the mouth to discern its taste. It is said: eating involves the lips and throat; tasting involves only the lips; swallowing involves only the throat; sucking involves the palate. And every eat is a taste but not vice versa. (Fath) 'He will not eat': i.e., if a man swore not to eat, etc. (Ayni) 'He breaks the oath by its fruit': i.e., by eating its fruit, because his oath is figuratively extended to it by consensus (Ayni). That is, if he swore not to eat from the palm tree, he breaks the oath by all that comes out of it: palm heart, unripe dates, ripe dates, pollen, or molasses. Similarly, if he said: from this grapevine, it extends to what comes out of it: grapes and unripe grapes; because he attached the oath to the palm tree which is not eaten, so it is turned to what comes out of it; because it is the cause of dates, so metaphor is permissible. 'Al-thamar' (with thā) is what comes out of it as long as it has not changed by a new process, so he does not break with wine, vinegar, nāṭif, or cooked molasses. He restricted it to what is not eaten itself, like the palm tree; because if he swore on something that is eaten itself, like this sheep, the oath is directed to the meat, not the milk or butter, because it is eaten, so the oath is valid on it. If the tree has no fruit, like willow and similar, the oath is directed to its price. And if he ate from the palm tree itself, he does not break, even if he intended it, because the literal meaning is abandoned due to the context. He indicated by 'its fruit' that if he cut a branch and attached it to another tree and ate from that branch's fruit, he does not break, and some said he does. Branch: He said: 'If I eat this loaf today, his wife is thrice divorced, and if he does not eat it today, his slave woman is free.' He ate half; he does not break, because the condition for breaking is absent in both oaths: eating all or leaving all. He said: 'This loaf is haram for me', he ate some: he breaks, unlike 'I will not eat this loaf' if it is something normally eaten in one session. The fatwa is on that. The principle: if he swore not to eat a specific thing and ate part of it, if it is normally eaten entirely, the oath is on all of it; otherwise, on part. (Ayni, Fath) 'And if he specified unripe dates' etc. The principle for these issues: when the oath is made on something described, if it is suitable as a motive for the oath, it is restricted, whether definite or indefinite; if not suitable, if indefinite, it is also restricted. Al-busr (with dammat al-bā and sukūn al-sīn) is the plural of busra, called in Persian: ghūra khurmā. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage covers the chapter on oaths regarding eating and drinking, beginning with definitions. Eating is defined as delivering chewable substance to the stomach; drinking as delivering non-chewable liquid; tasting, swallowing, and sucking are distinguished. The main ruling: an oath not to eat from a palm tree is broken by eating any of its fruit (dates, etc.) because the tree itself is inedible, so the oath extends to its produce by figurative interpretation (majāz), accepted by consensus. But if the substance is processed (e.g., wine, vinegar), the oath is not broken. If the object is edible (e.g., a sheep), the oath pertains to its meat, not milk/butter. If the tree has no fruit, the oath refers to its value. Eating the tree itself does not break the oath. A branch: if one cuts a branch and grafts it to another tree, the oath does not extend to its fruit. The next issue: oaths involving eating a whole loaf: if one swears an oath conditioned on eating the whole loaf, eating half does not break the oath; but if one says 'this loaf is haram for me', eating half breaks it. The principle: if the item is normally consumed entirely in one session, the oath covers all; otherwise, only part. Finally, the phrase 'specified unripe dates' introduces the rule that an oath on a described thing is restricted by that description if it serves as a motive, even if indefinite; al-busr is defined as unripe dates.

Key Terms
الأكل (al-akl): Delivering what can be chewed (mā yata'atā fīhi al-madgh) to the stomach, whether chewed or not.
الشرب (al-shurb): Delivering what cannot be crushed (mā lā yata'atā fīhi al-hashm) to the stomach.
الثمر (al-thamar): Anything that comes out of a tree (fruit, dates, etc.) that has not been processed into a new substance.
الذوق (al-dhawq): Bringing a substance to the mouth to discern its taste, involving only the lips.
الابتلاع (al-ibtila'): Swallowing, involving only the throat.
المص (al-mass): Sucking, involving the palate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because any consumption breaks the haram declaration.
B) No, because he ate only half, not the whole.
C) It depends on whether the loaf is normally eaten in one session.
D) The passage does not address this scenario.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines eating and drinking based on the substance's chewability, and an oath on an inedible source extends to its unprocessed produce by consensus; partial consumption of a loaf triggers an oath only if the whole is normally consumed in one session, but a haram declaration is broken by any amount.

Legal Definitions of Eating, Drinking, and Tasting for Oaths
Defines five actions for oath rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of eating, drinking, tasting, swallowing, and sucking, and apply them to determine whether an oath concerning eating or drinking is broken.

Core Idea
For oaths, eating requires reaching the stomach; tasting alone does not break an oath not to eat.

When someone takes an oath about eating or drinking, the Hanafi school defines these actions precisely to determine if the oath is broken. This lesson presents the technical definitions from Kanz al-Daqaiq, distinguishing between actions that reach the stomach and those that only involve the mouth. Understanding these definitions is essential for correctly applying oath-related rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Terms
List the five actions defined: eating (akl), drinking (shurb), tasting (dhawq), swallowing (ibtila'), and sucking (mass). Memorize their Arabic names.
2
Learn Each Definition
For each term, note the specific conveyance (to the stomach, mouth, etc.) and the anatomical parts involved (lips, throat, palate).
3
Understand the Hierarchy: Akl includes Dhawq
The text states 'every eating is a tasting, but not every tasting is an eating.' This means tasting is a broader category that includes eating, but eating adds the element of reaching the stomach.
This is the key legal distinction for oaths.
4
Apply to an Oath Scenario
Consider a person who swears 'I will not eat' but only tastes food without swallowing. According to the definition, tasting alone is not eating, so the oath is not broken. However, if they swallow even a small morsel, it becomes eating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأكل: إيصال ما يتأتى فيه المضغ إلى الجوف ممضوغاً كان أو غير ممضوغ والشرب: إيصال ما لا يتأتى فيه الهشم إلى الجوف، والذوق: إيصال الشيء إلى فمه لاستبانة طعمه، وقيل: الأكل عمل الشفاه والحلق والذوق عمل الشفاه دون الحلق، والابتلاع: عمل الحلق دون الشفاه والمص : عمل اللهات، وكل أكل ذوق ولا عكس
Eating: conveying something that can be chewed to the stomach, whether chewed or not. Drinking: conveying something that cannot be crushed to the stomach. Tasting: conveying something to the mouth to discern its taste. It is said: eating is an action of the lips and throat, tasting is an action of the lips without the throat, swallowing is an action of the throat without the lips, and sucking is an action of the palate. Every eating is a tasting, but not vice versa.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definitions used in the Hanafi school for determining whether an oath concerning eating or drinking is broken. The definitions distinguish between eating (akl: involves chewing and reaching the stomach), drinking (shurb: liquids that cannot be crushed), tasting (dhawq: only to the mouth), swallowing (ibtila': only throat), and sucking (mass: palate). The key point is that every act of eating includes tasting, but tasting alone does not count as eating. This is crucial for oaths because if someone swears not to eat but only tastes, the oath is not broken. The definitions set the boundaries for legal liability in oaths.

Key Terms
الأكل (al-akl): Conveying something that can be chewed to the stomach, whether chewed or not.
الشرب (al-shurb): Conveying something that cannot be crushed (liquids) to the stomach.
الذوق (al-dhawq): Conveying something to the mouth to discern its taste, not necessarily swallowing.
الابتلاع (al-ibtila'): The action of the throat alone, i.e., swallowing.
المص (al-mass): The action of the palate, i.e., sucking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definitions in this passage, which of the following is true?
A) Every tasting is an eating.
B) Every eating is a tasting.
C) Swallowing is a type of tasting.
D) Sucking is not a type of eating.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, eating for oath purposes requires food to reach the stomach; tasting alone does not break an oath not to eat.

Oath on a Non-Edible Tree: Metaphorical Extension to Produce
Ruling on when an oath not to eat from a palm tree is broken

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath on a non-edible object (e.g., palm tree) extends metaphorically to its produce, and identify exceptions for manufactured items and partial consumption.

Core Idea
An oath on a non-edible tree applies to its fruit by metaphor, but not to manufactured products.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common scenario: a person swears not to eat from a palm tree. Since the tree itself is inedible, the oath is understood metaphorically to refer to its fruit. The Hanafi school uses consensus (ijma) and analogical reasoning (qiyas) to extend this principle to other trees and to define limits when the produce is manufactured or partially eaten. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly determining when an oath is broken and requires expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is swearing not to eat from a palm tree. The ruling is that the oath is broken by eating any unprocessed fruit (dates, fresh or dry). The text clarifies that the oath applies metaphorically to the fruit because the tree itself is not eaten.
The ruling is based on consensus (ijma) that the phrase 'from the palm tree' is metaphorical for its fruit.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The author uses qiyas (analogy) and ijma. The proof structure: the tree is the cause (sabab) of the fruit, and since the tree is inedible, the oath must refer to its produce. This is supported by the principle of isti'ara (metaphor) and the consensus of scholars.
The argument type is dalil aqli (rational proof) because it relies on metaphorical extension and cause-effect reasoning.
3
Apply the principle to different scenarios
Apply the same rule to grapevines (oath applies to grapes/unripe grapes). Then distinguish: manufactured items (vinegar, cooked dibs) do not break the oath because they are changed by manufacture. Also, if the tree has no fruit (e.g., willow), the oath applies to its sale value.
The condition is that the produce must be unaltered (lam yataghayyar bi sina'atin jadida).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the grafted branch
If a branch is cut from the oath-tree and grafted to another tree, the text says the swearer does not break the oath by eating its fruit. But some say he does. This disagreement is based on whether the fruit is still considered 'from' the original tree.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the oath is not broken, as indicated by the phrase 'la yahnathu'.
5
Examine the sub-case of partial eating and conditional oaths
If someone swears to eat a whole loaf, eating half does not break the oath. But if he declares the loaf 'haram' (like a prohibition oath), eating any part breaks it. The principle depends on custom: if the whole is normally eaten in one sitting, the oath is on the whole; otherwise, on the part.
This shows that the ruling is tied to 'urf (custom) and the intention implied by the wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يأكل: أي لو حلف رجل لا يأكل إلخ. (عيني) حنث بثمرها : أي بأكل ثمرها؛ لانصراف يمينه إليه مجازاً بالإجماع (عيني) أي لو حلف لا يأكل من النخلة يحنث بجميع ما يخرج منها من جمار أو بسر أو رطب أو طلع أو دبس، وكذا إذا قال: من هذا الكرم ينصرف إلى ما يخرج منه من عنب وحصرم؛ لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى النخلة وهي لا توكل، فتنصرف إلى ما يخرج منها؛ لأنها سبب للتمر فجازت الاستعارة، والثمر بالمثلثة ما يخرج منها حيث لم يتغير بصنعة جديدة، فلا يحنث بالنبيذ والخل والناطف والدبس المطبوخ، وقيد بما لا تؤكل عينه أي النخلة؛ لأنه لو حلف على ما تؤكل عينه كهذه الشاة انصرفت اليمين إلى اللحم دون اللبن والزبد؛ لأنها مأكولة فينعقد اليمين عليها. ولو لم يكن للشجرة ثمر كالصفصاف ونحوه ينصرف اليمين إلى ثمنها، وإن أكل من عين النخلة لا يحنث وإن نواها؛ لأن الحقيقة مهجورة بدلالة محل الكلام، وأشار بثمرها إلى أنه لو قطع غصناً منها فوصله بشجرة أخرى فأكل من ثمر هذا الغصن لا يحنث، وقال بعضهم يحنث. فرع: قال: إن أكلت هذا الرغيف اليوم فامرأته طالق ثلاثاً وإن لم يأكله اليوم فأمته حرة فأكل النصف لم يحنث؛ لانعدام شرط الحنث في اليمينين، وهو أكل الكل أو ترك الكل، قال: هذا الرغيف علي حرام فأكل بعضه حنث، بخلاف لا اكل هذا الرغيف إذا كان كله مما يؤكل في مجلس واحد والفتوى على ذلك، والأصل أنه إذا حلف لا يأكل معيناً فأكل بعضه فإن كان مما يوكل كله عادة فالحلف على كله، وإلا فعلى بعضه. (عيني، فتح)
He said: 'He does not eat': i.e., if a man swore that he would not eat, etc. (Ayni) He breaks the oath by its fruit: i.e., by eating its fruit, because his oath is metaphorically applied to it by consensus (Ayni). That is, if he swore not to eat from the palm tree, he breaks the oath by all that comes out of it: jumar, unripe dates, ripe dates, pollen, or dibs. Likewise, if he said: 'from this grapevine,' it applies to what comes out of it: grapes and unripe grapes, because he attached the oath to the palm tree, which itself is not eaten, so it applies to what comes out of it because it is the cause of the dates, so metaphor is permissible. And 'fruit' (thamr) with three dots means what comes out of it as long as it has not been changed by a new manufacture, so he does not break the oath by nabidh, vinegar, natif, or cooked dibs. It is restricted to that whose substance is not eaten, i.e., the palm tree, because if he swore on something whose substance is eaten, like this sheep, the oath applies to the meat, not to milk or butter, because it is eaten so the oath is valid on it. And if the tree has no fruit, like willow and the like, the oath applies to its price. And if he eats from the substance of the palm tree itself, he does not break the oath even if he intended it, because the literal meaning is abandoned due to the context of the speech. And he indicated by 'its fruit' that if he cut a branch from it and grafted it to another tree, and then ate from the fruit of that branch, he does not break the oath; but some say he does. A sub-issue: He said: 'If I eat this loaf today, my wife is thrice divorced, and if I do not eat it today, my slave-girl is free.' Then he ate half of it: he does not break the oath because the condition for breaking the oath in both oaths is absent, which is eating all or leaving all. He said: 'This loaf is haram for me,' then he ate some of it: he breaks the oath. Unlike 'I will not eat this loaf' if the whole loaf is normally eaten in one sitting, and the fatwa is upon that. The principle is that if he swore not to eat a specific thing, and then ate part of it, if the whole is normally eaten, the oath is upon the whole; otherwise, upon part of it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage discusses oaths related to things that are not themselves eaten but produce edible items. When a person swears not to eat from a palm tree, he breaks the oath by eating any of its produce (fresh dates, dried dates, etc.) because the word 'palm tree' is used metaphorically for its fruit by consensus. However, if the produce is changed by manufacturing (like vinegar, cooked syrup), the oath is not broken because it is no longer considered 'fruit' in the unprocessed sense. The ruling is different for an animal that is eaten: if sworn on a sheep, the oath applies to the meat, not milk or butter, because the animal itself is edible. If the tree has no fruit, the oath refers to its sale value. Eating the wood of the tree itself does not break the oath. A sub-ruling addresses when someone swears conditional oaths on eating a loaf: if he swears to eat the whole loaf in a day, eating half does not break the oath because the condition (eating all) is not met. But if he declares the loaf haram, eating any part breaks the oath because it is like a general prohibition. The principle: for an oath not to eat a specific thing, the ruling depends on whether the whole thing is customarily eaten at once or not.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath swearing not to do something or to do something.
حنث (hanatha): The act of breaking an oath.
ثمر (thamr): The produce of the tree before manufacturing.
استعارة (isti'ara): Metaphor; using a word for something other than its literal meaning due to a connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears not to eat from a specific apple tree. He cooks the apples into applesauce and eats it. According to the passage, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because applesauce is made from apples.
B) No, because cooking is a manufacturing change.
C) Yes, if he intended the applesauce.
D) No, because the tree is not edible.

Key Takeaway
An oath on a non-edible tree metaphorically applies to its unprocessed fruit; any manufacturing or grafting breaks the connection, and partial consumption depends on custom.

The Dāʿī Principle: Oaths on Food and Milk
Ruling with reasoning on oaths and change of state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of dāʿīyah to determine whether an oath is broken when the named food item changes its state.

Core Idea
An oath is not broken when the motivating quality (dāʿī) disappears with the change.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of an oath upon a specific object depends on whether the object's description was the reason for the oath. This passage from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq explains that if the description (like unripe dates) is a genuine cause (dāʿī), then when the object changes to a different state (ripe dates), the oath is not violated. We will examine this principle as applied to dates and milk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and its description
Look at what the person swore to avoid: is it a specific instance (e.g., 'this busr') or a general category? Note the description given.
2
Determine if the description is a valid dāʿī
Ask: would this quality reasonably motivate someone to take an oath? For example, if ripe dates cause harm, the quality 'rutab' is a dāʿī.
3
Check if the description changed
Did the object's state change from the one sworn upon? If busr became rutab, the description is gone.
4
Apply the ruling
If the description was a valid dāʿī and it changed, the oath is not broken. If not, the oath remains effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، فتح) والرطب واللبن لا يحنث برطبه وتمره ،وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً لم يحنث، وفي لا يأكل رطباً أو بسراً، أي لو حلف رجل ... (عيني) والرطب بأن حلف لا يأكل هذا الرطب . (عيني) واللبن بأن حلف لا يأكل هذا اللبن. (عيني) لا يحنث برطبه إلخ فيه لف ونشر مرتب أي من حلف لا يأكل هذا البسر ، فأكل بعد ما صار رطباً، أو حلف لا يأكل . هذا الرطب، فأكل بعد ما صار تمراً، أو حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللبن، فأكل بعد ما صار شيرازاً، من عنه لا يحنث؛ لأن صفة الرطوبة والبسورة داعية إلى اليمين؛ إذ ربما يضره الرطب دون البسر، وكذا في الصورتين الأخريين، والتمر بالمثناة، هو ما ليس من الرطب، والشيراز: بكسر الشين المعجمة لبن يغلى فيثخن جداً واستخرج ماءه حتى صار صفراطاً معرب جفرات، وكذا لا يحنث إذا أكل منها في هذه الصور، وأما إذا لم يكن للنخلة ثمر فيحنث بثمنها . (مستخلص، فتح وتمره: أي ولا يحنث بأكل ثمره فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الرطب. (عيني) وشيرازه: أي ولا يحنث بأكل شيرازه وهو اللبن الرائب فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل . من هذا اللبن. (عيني) بخلاف هذا الصبي إلخ أي لو حلف أن لا يكلم هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب فكلمهما بعد ما شاخا حنث؛ لأن صفة الصبي والشاب وإن كانت داعية إلى اليمين، لكن هجرانهما لأجل الصبا والشباب منهي . شرعاً؛ لأنه أمرنا بتحمل أخلاق الفتيان ومرحمة الصبيان فكان مهجوراً شرعاً، والمهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عادةً، فلم يعتبر الداعي، وقيد بالإشارة؛ لأنه لو حلف لا يكلم صبياً أو شاباً تقيد بزمان صباه أو شبابه، فلو كلم بعد ما بلغ وشاخ لم يحنث؛ لتبديل الاسم، فإن الصبي بعد البلوغ يدعى شاباً وفتى، والشاب يدعى شيخاً، والشاب شرعاً: من حد البلوغ إلى ثلاثين ما لم يغلب عليه الشيب والشيخ ما زاد على الخمسين، وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث في هذا أيضاً. (فتح، عيني) وهذا الحمل: [ أي وبخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل هذا الحمل فأكله بعد ما صار كبشاً، فيحنث. (عيني)] الحمل: بفتحتين ولد الشاة في السنة الأولى، أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل لحم هذا الحمل، فأكل بعد ما صار كبشاً يحنث؛ لأنه ليس في الحمل صفة داعية إلى اليمين، فإن الممتنع عنه يكون أشد امتناعاً من لحم الكبش، وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث أيضاً؛ لأن لحم الحمل لكثرة رطوباته ليس محموداً، فيكون داعياً إلى اليمين بخلاف لحم الكبش، فإنه أكثر قوة للبدن؛ لقلة الرطوبات. (مستخلص، عيني) لم يحنث لأنه لم يأكل المحلوف عليه، وكذا إذا حلف لا يأكل عنباً فأكل زبيباً. (ط، عيني) وفي لا يأكل رطبا : أي لو حلف لا يأكل رطباً أو حلف أن لا يأكل بسراً، والجزاء يأتي من بعد، أي يحنث بأكل المذنب عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يحنث، وقيل: محمد مع أبي حنيفة وسيأتي مفصلاً. (محشي)
And if he specifies unripe dates (busr), etc. The principle in this kind of issue is that when an oath is made on something described, if the description is a valid cause (dāʿī) for the oath, it is restricted by it, whether the object is definite or indefinite; and if it is not a valid cause, then if it is indefinite, it is also restricted. Busr: with a damma on the bāʾ and a sukūn on the sīn, plural of busra; in Persian it is called gūrah khurmā. (Ayni, Fath) And [the oath on] ripe dates (rutab) and milk: he does not break the oath by its ripe dates or its dried dates or its shīrāz. Unlike this child, this youth, and this lamb. [From the matn:] 'He did not swear not to eat busr and then ate rutab: he does not break the oath. And regarding 'he does not eat rutab or busr'...' Ayni: 'And ripe dates (rutab) means if he swore not to eat this rutab; and milk means if he swore not to eat this milk.' He does not break the oath by its ripe dates etc. There is a chiasmus (laff wa nashr) order: that is, whoever swore not to eat this busr, then ate it after it became rutab, or swore not to eat this rutab, then ate it after it became tamr (dried dates), or swore not to eat from this milk, then ate it after it became shīrāz, he does not break the oath because the quality of ripeness or unripeness is a cause (dāʿī) for the oath; perhaps ripe dates harm him while unripe do not, and similarly in the other two cases. Tamr (with tathnīyah) is what is not rutab. Shīrāz: with a kasra on the shīn, milk that is boiled until very thick and its water is extracted until it becomes a yellowish substance, Persianized from jūfrāt. Thus, he does not break the oath if he eats from these in these cases. But if the palm tree has no fruit, he breaks the oath by its price. (Mustakhlis, Fath) 'And its tamr': meaning he does not break the oath by eating its dried dates when he swore not to eat from this rutab (Ayni). 'And its shīrāz': meaning he does not break the oath by eating shīrāz, which is sour milk, when he swore not to eat from this milk (Ayni). Unlike this child, i.e., if he swore not to speak to this child and this youth, and then he spoke to them after they grew old, he breaks the oath; because the quality of childhood and youth, even though it is a cause (dāʿī) for the oath, nevertheless avoiding them for the sake of childhood and youth is prohibited by Shariʿah, because we are commanded to bear the manners of the young and have mercy on children, so it is abandoned by Shariʿah. And what is abandoned by Shariʿah is like what is abandoned by custom, so the cause is not considered. It is restricted by the demonstrative pronoun (hādhā); because if he swore not to speak to a child or a youth (without a demonstrative), it is restricted to the time of his childhood or youth, so if he speaks after he reaches maturity and grows old, he does not break the oath, due to the change of name; for after puberty, a child is called a youth or lad, and a youth is called an old man. The youth, according to Shariʿah, is from the age of puberty to thirty, as long as grey hair does not prevail; the old man is over fifty. And according to the three (Imams), he also does not break the oath in this case. (Fath, Ayni) And 'this lamb': i.e., unlike if he swore not to eat this lamb, and then ate it after it became a ram, he breaks the oath. (Ayni) 'The lamb' (al-ḥaml) with two fatḥahs: the offspring of a sheep in its first year; i.e., if he swore not to eat the meat of this lamb, and then ate it after it became a ram, he breaks the oath, because the lamb does not have a quality that is a cause (dāʿī) for the oath; for the thing he refrains from is more abhorrent than the meat of a ram. And according to the three, he also does not break the oath; because the meat of a lamb, due to its many moistures, is not praiseworthy, so it would be a cause for the oath, unlike the meat of a ram, which is stronger for the body due to few moistures. (Mustakhlis, Ayni) He does not break the oath because he did not eat the thing sworn upon; likewise, if he swore not to eat grapes and then ate raisins. (T, Ayni) And regarding 'he does not eat rutab': i.e., if he swore not to eat rutab or swore not to eat busr, the penalty comes afterward; i.e., he breaks the oath by eating the opposite according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he does not break the oath, and it is said that Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa. The detail will come later. (Mahashi)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This extensive passage deals with the effect of change in the state of the object of an oath. The key principle: if the description (e.g., unripe vs. ripe) is a genuine cause (dāʿī) for the oath — meaning the person swore because of that specific quality — then when the object changes, the oath is not broken because the original quality is gone. For example, if someone swears not to eat unripe dates (busr) because they harm him, and then eats ripe dates (rutab), the oath is not broken. Similarly for ripe dates turning into dried dates (tamr), or milk turning into shīrāz (thickened milk). However, if the quality is not a valid cause in Shariʿah — such as avoiding speaking to a child (since Islam encourages mercy to children) — then even if the child grows up, the oath remains and is broken if he speaks to him, because the prohibition on avoiding children makes the cause invalid. Likewise for a lamb: there is no natural aversion to eating lamb meat (it is actually more desirable than ram?), so the oath is not nullified by growth. The passage also notes an intra-madhab dispute: if someone swears not to eat ripe dates (rutab) and then eats unripe dates (busr), or vice versa, Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken because the opposite type still falls under the general category of dates, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, with a report that Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa) hold that it is not broken because the names are different and the cause may differ. The detail is promised later.

Key Terms
داعية (dāʿīyah): The effective cause or motive for taking the oath; a quality that reasonably prompts someone to swear.
البسر (al-busr): Unripe dates, still green and hard.
الرطب (al-rutab): Ripe, fresh dates.
التمر (al-tamr): Dried dates, fully ripe and dried.
شيراز (shīrāz): Thickened or concentrated milk, possibly like yogurt or cheese.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears not to eat a specific unripe date (busr) and then eats it after it becomes ripe (rutab), what is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because he ate dates.
B) He does not break the oath because the quality that motivated the oath (unripeness) is gone.
C) He breaks the oath because the name changed.
D) He does not break the oath only if he explicitly intended unripe dates.

Key Takeaway
The dāʿī principle: an oath is restricted by the motivating quality; if that quality changes, the oath is not broken.

Exceptions: Oaths on Children, Youth, and Lambs
Rulings on exceptions where the dāʿī is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid causes (dāʿī) in oaths, and apply the exception when the cause is prohibited by Shariʿah or non-existent.

Core Idea
An oath is broken when the motivating quality is invalid in Shariʿah or not actually motivating.

Not all descriptions that might motivate an oath are considered valid in Hanafi law. This lesson examines cases where the dāʿī is rejected: swearing not to speak to a child or youth (because avoiding them is discouraged) and swearing not to eat a lamb (because its meat is actually desirable). In these cases, the oath is broken even though the object changes state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and its description
Is it a child (in age) or a lamb (in age)? Note the specific description used.
2
Determine if the description would normally be a dāʿī
For a child, avoiding children might be a cause; for a lamb, the meat quality might be a cause. But check Shariʿah validity.
3
Check if the description is valid or invalid in Shariʿah
If Shariʿah prohibits avoiding the description (e.g., mercy to children), then the dāʿī is invalid. If the description is not actually harmful (lamb meat is good), then no dāʿī exists.
4
Apply the exceptional ruling
If the dāʿī is invalid or non-existent, the oath is not restricted by it, so the oath is broken when the object changes (or even if it doesn't?). Actually, in these cases, the oath is broken because the intended avoidance is not justified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو عين البسر إلخ الأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أن اليمين إذا عقدت على شيء يوصف، فإن صلح داعياً إلى اليمين يتقيد به، سواء كان معرفاً أو منكراً، وإن لم يصلح داعياً فإن كان منكراً تتقيد أيضاً، والبسر: بضم الموحدة وسكون المهملة جمع بسرة يقال لها بالفارسية: غوره خرما. (عيني، فتح) والرطب واللبن لا يحنث برطبه وتمره ،وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً لم يحنث، وفي لا يأكل رطباً أو بسراً، أي لو حلف رجل ... (عيني) والرطب بأن حلف لا يأكل هذا الرطب . (عيني) واللبن بأن حلف لا يأكل هذا اللبن. (عيني) لا يحنث برطبه إلخ فيه لف ونشر مرتب أي من حلف لا يأكل هذا البسر ، فأكل بعد ما صار رطباً، أو حلف لا يأكل . هذا الرطب، فأكل بعد ما صار تمراً، أو حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللبن، فأكل بعد ما صار شيرازاً، من عنه لا يحنث؛ لأن صفة الرطوبة والبسورة داعية إلى اليمين؛ إذ ربما يضره الرطب دون البسر، وكذا في الصورتين الأخريين، والتمر بالمثناة، هو ما ليس من الرطب، والشيراز: بكسر الشين المعجمة لبن يغلى فيثخن جداً واستخرج ماءه حتى صار صفراطاً معرب جفرات، وكذا لا يحنث إذا أكل منها في هذه الصور، وأما إذا لم يكن للنخلة ثمر فيحنث بثمنها . (مستخلص، فتح وتمره: أي ولا يحنث بأكل ثمره فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الرطب. (عيني) وشيرازه: أي ولا يحنث بأكل شيرازه وهو اللبن الرائب فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل . من هذا اللبن. (عيني) بخلاف هذا الصبي إلخ أي لو حلف أن لا يكلم هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب فكلمهما بعد ما شاخا حنث؛ لأن صفة الصبي والشاب وإن كانت داعية إلى اليمين، لكن هجرانهما لأجل الصبا والشباب منهي . شرعاً؛ لأنه أمرنا بتحمل أخلاق الفتيان ومرحمة الصبيان فكان مهجوراً شرعاً، والمهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عادةً، فلم يعتبر الداعي، وقيد بالإشارة؛ لأنه لو حلف لا يكلم صبياً أو شاباً تقيد بزمان صباه أو شبابه، فلو كلم بعد ما بلغ وشاخ لم يحنث؛ لتبديل الاسم، فإن الصبي بعد البلوغ يدعى شاباً وفتى، والشاب يدعى شيخاً، والشاب شرعاً: من حد البلوغ إلى ثلاثين ما لم يغلب عليه الشيب والشيخ ما زاد على الخمسين، وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث في هذا أيضاً. (فتح، عيني) وهذا الحمل: [ أي وبخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل هذا الحمل فأكله بعد ما صار كبشاً، فيحنث. (عيني)] الحمل: بفتحتين ولد الشاة في السنة الأولى، أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل لحم هذا الحمل، فأكل بعد ما صار كبشاً يحنث؛ لأنه ليس في الحمل صفة داعية إلى اليمين، فإن الممتنع عنه يكون أشد امتناعاً من لحم الكبش، وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث أيضاً؛ لأن لحم الحمل لكثرة رطوباته ليس محموداً، فيكون داعياً إلى اليمين بخلاف لحم الكبش، فإنه أكثر قوة للبدن؛ لقلة الرطوبات. (مستخلص، عيني) لم يحنث لأنه لم يأكل المحلوف عليه، وكذا إذا حلف لا يأكل عنباً فأكل زبيباً. (ط، عيني) وفي لا يأكل رطبا : أي لو حلف لا يأكل رطباً أو حلف أن لا يأكل بسراً، والجزاء يأتي من بعد، أي يحنث بأكل المذنب عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يحنث، وقيل: محمد مع أبي حنيفة وسيأتي مفصلاً. (محشي)
And if he specifies unripe dates (busr), etc. The principle in this kind of issue is that when an oath is made on something described, if the description is a valid cause (dāʿī) for the oath, it is restricted by it, whether the object is definite or indefinite; and if it is not a valid cause, then if it is indefinite, it is also restricted. Busr: with a damma on the bāʾ and a sukūn on the sīn, plural of busra; in Persian it is called gūrah khurmā. (Ayni, Fath) And [the oath on] ripe dates (rutab) and milk: he does not break the oath by its ripe dates or its dried dates or its shīrāz. Unlike this child, this youth, and this lamb. [From the matn:] 'He did not swear not to eat busr and then ate rutab: he does not break the oath. And regarding 'he does not eat rutab or busr'...' Ayni: 'And ripe dates (rutab) means if he swore not to eat this rutab; and milk means if he swore not to eat this milk.' He does not break the oath by its ripe dates etc. There is a chiasmus (laff wa nashr) order: that is, whoever swore not to eat this busr, then ate it after it became rutab, or swore not to eat this rutab, then ate it after it became tamr (dried dates), or swore not to eat from this milk, then ate it after it became shīrāz, he does not break the oath because the quality of ripeness or unripeness is a cause (dāʿī) for the oath; perhaps ripe dates harm him while unripe do not, and similarly in the other two cases. Tamr (with tathnīyah) is what is not rutab. Shīrāz: with a kasra on the shīn, milk that is boiled until very thick and its water is extracted until it becomes a yellowish substance, Persianized from jūfrāt. Thus, he does not break the oath if he eats from these in these cases. But if the palm tree has no fruit, he breaks the oath by its price. (Mustakhlis, Fath) 'And its tamr': meaning he does not break the oath by eating its dried dates when he swore not to eat from this rutab (Ayni). 'And its shīrāz': meaning he does not break the oath by eating shīrāz, which is sour milk, when he swore not to eat from this milk (Ayni). Unlike this child, i.e., if he swore not to speak to this child and this youth, and then he spoke to them after they grew old, he breaks the oath; because the quality of childhood and youth, even though it is a cause (dāʿī) for the oath, nevertheless avoiding them for the sake of childhood and youth is prohibited by Shariʿah, because we are commanded to bear the manners of the young and have mercy on children, so it is abandoned by Shariʿah. And what is abandoned by Shariʿah is like what is abandoned by custom, so the cause is not considered. It is restricted by the demonstrative pronoun (hādhā); because if he swore not to speak to a child or a youth (without a demonstrative), it is restricted to the time of his childhood or youth, so if he speaks after he reaches maturity and grows old, he does not break the oath, due to the change of name; for after puberty, a child is called a youth or lad, and a youth is called an old man. The youth, according to Shariʿah, is from the age of puberty to thirty, as long as grey hair does not prevail; the old man is over fifty. And according to the three (Imams), he also does not break the oath in this case. (Fath, Ayni) And 'this lamb': i.e., unlike if he swore not to eat this lamb, and then ate it after it became a ram, he breaks the oath. (Ayni) 'The lamb' (al-ḥaml) with two fatḥahs: the offspring of a sheep in its first year; i.e., if he swore not to eat the meat of this lamb, and then ate it after it became a ram, he breaks the oath, because the lamb does not have a quality that is a cause (dāʿī) for the oath; for the thing he refrains from is more abhorrent than the meat of a ram. And according to the three, he also does not break the oath; because the meat of a lamb, due to its many moistures, is not praiseworthy, so it would be a cause for the oath, unlike the meat of a ram, which is stronger for the body due to few moistures. (Mustakhlis, Ayni) He does not break the oath because he did not eat the thing sworn upon; likewise, if he swore not to eat grapes and then ate raisins. (T, Ayni) And regarding 'he does not eat rutab': i.e., if he swore not to eat rutab or swore not to eat busr, the penalty comes afterward; i.e., he breaks the oath by eating the opposite according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he does not break the oath, and it is said that Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa. The detail will come later. (Mahashi)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This extensive passage deals with the effect of change in the state of the object of an oath. The key principle: if the description (e.g., unripe vs. ripe) is a genuine cause (dāʿī) for the oath — meaning the person swore because of that specific quality — then when the object changes, the oath is not broken because the original quality is gone. For example, if someone swears not to eat unripe dates (busr) because they harm him, and then eats ripe dates (rutab), the oath is not broken. Similarly for ripe dates turning into dried dates (tamr), or milk turning into shīrāz (thickened milk). However, if the quality is not a valid cause in Shariʿah — such as avoiding speaking to a child (since Islam encourages mercy to children) — then even if the child grows up, the oath remains and is broken if he speaks to him, because the prohibition on avoiding children makes the cause invalid. Likewise for a lamb: there is no natural aversion to eating lamb meat (it is actually more desirable than ram?), so the oath is not nullified by growth. The passage also notes an intra-madhab dispute: if someone swears not to eat ripe dates (rutab) and then eats unripe dates (busr), or vice versa, Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken because the opposite type still falls under the general category of dates, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, with a report that Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa) hold that it is not broken because the names are different and the cause may differ. The detail is promised later.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-ṣabī): A child, before puberty.
الشاب (al-shāb): A youth, from puberty to forty? Here from puberty to thirty.
الحمل (al-ḥaml): A lamb, the offspring of a sheep in its first year.
الكبش (al-kabsh): A ram, an adult male sheep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears not to speak to a specific youth (shāb) and then speaks to him after he becomes an old man, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break the oath because the name changed.
B) He breaks the oath because avoiding youth is prohibited in Shariʿah.
C) He breaks the oath only if he intended the youth as a youth.
D) He does not break the oath because the oath was indefinite.

Key Takeaway
If the motivating cause (dāʿī) is invalid in Shariʿah or non-existent, the oath is not restricted by it, and the oath is broken when the object changes.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Swearing on Opposite Types of Dates
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths regarding opposite date types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether swearing not to eat one type of date (e.g., rutab) includes the opposite type (e.g., busr), and explain the practical difference.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: the oath is broken by the opposite type; the Sahibayn: it is not.

When someone swears not to eat ripe dates (rutab) and then eats unripe dates (busr), or vice versa, the Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This lesson focuses on that specific ikhtilaf, which the previous passage says will be detailed later. We examine the two views and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact oath
Is the oath on 'rutab' (ripe dates) or 'busr' (unripe dates)? The dispute arises when the opposite type is eaten.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
He says the oath is broken (yahnath) because both are types of dates, and the oath covers the category.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the oath is not broken (la yahnath) because the names are distinct and the motivating cause may differ.
4
Note the variation on Muhammad
There is a report that Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa, so the dispute might be between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf alone.
5
Understand the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the muftī follows Abu Hanifa, he will rule the oath is broken; if he follows the Sahibayn, he will rule it is not.

However, if the quality is not a valid cause in Shariʿah — such as avoiding speaking to a child (since Islam encourages mercy to children) — then even if the child grows up, the oath remains and is broken if he speaks to him, because the prohibition on avoiding children makes the cause invalid. Likewise for a lamb: there is no natural aversion to eating lamb meat (it is actually more desirable than ram?), so the oath is not nullified by growth. The passage also notes an intra-madhab dispute: if someone swears not to eat ripe dates (rutab) and then eats unripe dates (busr), or vice versa, Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken because the opposite type still falls under the general category of dates, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, with a report that Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa) hold that it is not broken because the names are different and the cause may differ. The detail is promised later.

Key Terms
المذنب (al-mudhnib): The opposite type; what is sworn against (e.g., eating busr when sworn on rutab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding an oath on one type of date (e.g., rutab) when the opposite type (busr) is eaten?
A) The oath is broken.
B) The oath is not broken.
C) It depends on the intention.
D) They differ; Abu Yusuf says broken, Muhammad says not.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree on whether the oath on one type of date covers the opposite type; the text promises more detail later, so this is not the final word.

Oaths and Change in Quality: Dates and Milk
Ruling on oaths for dates and milk with quality change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an oath is not broken when the object's quality changes, and apply the rationale to distinguish when the oath is binding.

Core Idea
An oath is tied to the specific quality that prompted it; when that quality changes, the oath is not broken.

When a person swears not to consume a specific item, does the oath still bind them if that item transforms into a different form? In this passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the Hanafi school clarifies that the oath is not broken because the reason for the oath—the specific quality causing aversion—no longer exists. The passage illustrates this with dates at various stages of ripeness and milk turned into whey. Understanding this principle helps avoid unintended oath-breaking and ensures accurate application of the rules of oaths (ayman).

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Read the passage and note the three examples: swearing not to eat unripe dates then eating ripe ones; swearing not to eat fresh dates then eating dried ones; swearing not to drink milk then drinking whey. Also note the exception: if the palm has no fruit, the oath is broken by consuming its value.
2
Recognize the quality change
In each case, the consumed item is derived from the original but has undergone a transformation: unripe to ripe, fresh to dried, liquid milk to thickened whey. Identify that the transformation changes the essential quality (sifah) of the object.
Key terms: busr → rutab, rutab → tamr, laban → shīrāz.
3
Apply the rationale (ta'lil)
The passage states that the oath is prompted by the specific quality (e.g., raṭābah, buṣūrah). When that quality is gone, the underlying cause of the oath disappears. Therefore, the oath is not broken. Confirm that the new form lacks the original aversive quality.
The 'illa is the specific quality that motivated the oath.
4
Distinguish the exception
If the palm tree has no fruit (i.e., the date does not exist at all at the time of oath), then the oath is not tied to a specific fruit but to its price (thaman). In that case, eating the fruit that later appears would break the oath if the person consumes its value. Understand the difference: the quality change rule applies only when the original object exists and transforms; if the object is nonexistent, the oath attaches to the value.
The exception highlights that the ruling depends on the existence of the object at the time of the oath.
5
Formulate the general rule
From the examples, derive the principle: An oath concerning a specific object is not broken by consuming that object after its essential quality has changed. The change removes the 'illa that made the oath meaningful. The ruling is based on the Hanafi method of considering the effective cause (ʿilla) and the context of the oath.
This rule applies to any similar situation where the subject of an oath undergoes a change in its defining characteristic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرطب واللبن لا يحنث برطبه وتمره وشيرازه، أي لو حلف رجل لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً لم يحنث، أو حلف لا يأكل هذا الرطب فأكل بعد ما صار تمراً، أو حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللبن فأكل بعد ما صار شيرازاً، لا يحنث؛ لأن صفة الرطوبة والبسورة داعية إلى اليمين؛ إذ ربما يضره الرطب دون البسر، وكذا في الصورتين الأخريين، والتمر بالمثناة هو ما ليس من الرطب، والشيراز بكسر الشين المعجمة لبن يغلى فيثخن جداً واستخرج ماءه حتى صار صفراطاً معرب جفرات، وكذا لا يحنث إذا أكل منها في هذه الصور، وأما إذا لم يكن للنخلة ثمر فيحنث بثمنه. (مستخلص، فتح وتمره: أي ولا يحنث بأكل ثمره فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الرطب. (عيني) وشيرازه: أي ولا يحنث بأكل شيرازه وهو اللبن الرائب فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللبن. (عيني)
And fresh dates and milk: he does not break his oath by their ripe dates and their dried dates and their whey. [Explanation]: So if a man swore, 'I will not eat unripe dates,' then he ate ripe dates, he does not break his oath; or if he swore, 'I will not eat this fresh dates,' then he ate after it became dried dates; or if he swore, 'I will not eat from this milk,' then he ate after it became whey, he does not break his oath because the quality of freshness and unripeness is what prompts the oath since perhaps fresh dates harm him while unripe dates do not, and similarly in the other two cases. Tamr (dried dates) is what is not fresh, and shīrāz is milk boiled until thickened and its water extracted until it becomes a kind of curd. Likewise, he does not break his oath if he eats from them in these cases. But if the palm tree has no fruit, he breaks his oath by its price.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the ruling on oaths concerning dates and milk when their condition changes. The author explains that if a person swears not to eat a specific type (e.g., unripe dates, fresh dates, or milk) and then consumes it after it has transformed into a different form (ripe dates, dried dates, whey), the oath is not broken. The reason is that the oath is driven by the specific quality that caused the aversion (such as the freshness causing harm). When that quality is gone, the underlying reason for the oath no longer applies. The commentary also clarifies the terminology: tamr is dried dates, and shīrāz is a thick milk product. An exception is made when the palm tree has no fruit at all — in that case, the oath is broken by consuming its value (thaman). This ruling is from the Hanafi school as per al-Kanz and its commentaries.

Key Terms
بسر (busr): Unripe dates, the stage before rutab.
رطب (rutab): Fresh, ripe dates, moist and soft.
تمر (tamr): Dried dates, the final stage of date maturity.
شيراز (shīrāz): A thick milk product made by boiling milk until thickened, similar to whey or curd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears not to eat from 'this milk.' The milk is later made into yogurt. According to the Hanafi ruling, does he break his oath if he eats the yogurt?
A) Yes, because yogurt is still a dairy product.
B) No, because the milk changed into a different substance (yogurt).
C) Yes, because the oath was on the milk specifically.
D) No, unless he intended to include yogurt.

Key Takeaway
An oath is broken only if the consumed item retains the specific quality that prompted the oath; a change in that quality means the oath remains intact.
