Oath to Speak to a Boy or Young Man: Effect of Aging
Expansion of the rule on oaths: change in the object of the oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a pointed and a general oath regarding a boy/young man, state the Hanafi ruling for each case, identify the rationale, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
An oath to avoid speaking to a specific young person is broken when they age because Sharia encourages kindness to youth, but a general oath lapses with the change of name.

In the previous lesson, we saw that an oath regarding objects (like dates or milk) is not broken when the object changes. Here, the ruling differs for people: the very quality that prompted the oath (youth) is something we are commanded to respect, so avoidance is prohibited. However, the wording of the oath determines the outcome—whether it is a specific individual or a general category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of the oath
Determine whether the oath refers to a specific individual (by pointing or clear indication) or a general type (e.g., 'any boy' or 'any young man').
The text explicitly contrasts 'this boy' (pointed) with 'a boy' (general).
2
Apply the pointed case ruling
If the oath was on a specific person (pointed), and that person ages (boy becomes young man, young man becomes elder), then speaking to them breaks the oath. This is because avoiding someone due to youth is prohibited in Sharia, so the prompting cause is invalid.
The rationale: 'المهمور شرعاً كالمهمور عادةً' – what is legally avoided is like what is customarily avoided, so the cause is not considered.
3
Apply the general case ruling
If the oath was general (e.g., 'I will not speak to a boy'), it is limited to the time when the person is a boy. After he reaches puberty and becomes a young man, the oath is no longer binding because the name has changed.
The change of name (ṣabī to shābb) lifts the restriction.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute
Note that the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar) hold that the oath is not broken even in the pointed case, possibly analogizing to the previous lesson's objects case.
The text says: 'وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث في هذا أيضاً.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف هذا الصبي إلخ أي لو حلف أن لا يكلم هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب فكلمهما بعد ما شاخا حنث؛ لأن صفة الصبي والشاب وإن كانت داعية إلى اليمين، لكن هجرانهما لأجل الصبا والشباب منهي شرعاً؛ لأنه أمرنا بتحمل أخلاق الفتيان ومرحمة الصبيان فكان مهجوراً شرعاً، والمهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عادةً، فلم يعتبر الداعي، وقيد بالإشارة؛ لأنه لو حلف لا يكلم صبياً أو شاباً تقيد بزمان صباه أو شبابه، فلو كلم بعد ما بلغ وشاخ لم يحنث؛ لتبديل الاسم، فإن الصبي بعد البلوغ يدعى شاباً وفتى، والشاب يدعى شيخاً، والشاب شرعاً: من حد البلوغ إلى ثلاثين ما لم يغلب عليه الشيب والشيخ ما زاد على الخمسين، وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث في هذا أيضاً. (فتح، عيني)
Contrary to this boy and this young man: i.e., if he swore that he will not speak to this boy and this young man, then he spoke to them after they have aged, he breaks his oath because the quality of boyhood and youth, although it prompts the oath, their avoidance due to their youth is prohibited in Sharia because we are commanded to bear the manners of youths and have mercy on children, so it is considered a legally prohibited avoidance, and what is legally prohibited is like what is customarily prohibited, so the prompting characteristic is not considered. And it is restricted by pointing because if he swore not to speak to a boy or a young man without pointing, it is restricted to the time of his boyhood or youth, so if he spoke after he reached adulthood and aged, he does not break the oath due to the change of name. For the boy after maturity is called a young man and a youth, and the young man is called an elder. The young man legally is from the age of maturity to thirty unless gray hair prevails, and an elder is above fifty. According to the three, he also does not break the oath in this case.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage contrasts with the previous ruling on dates and milk. Here, the oath is broken because the reason for avoiding the person (youth) is itself something encouraged by Sharia—to be kind to the young. So the oath stands. The commentary also notes that if the oath was general without pointing, it is limited to the period of youth. There is a difference with the three imams (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) who say no hanth.

Key Terms
صبي (ṣabī): A boy, from birth until puberty.
شاب (shābb): A young man from puberty to thirty, unless gray hair prevails.
شاخ (shākha): To age or become old.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to speak to a specific boy, and the boy later becomes a young man (shābb), what happens if he speaks to him?
A) He does not break his oath because the name changed.
B) He breaks his oath because avoiding due to youth is prohibited.
C) He does not break his oath because the quality changed.
D) He breaks his oath only if the oath was by pointing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath to avoid speaking to a specific young person is broken when they age due to Sharia's prohibition of avoiding youth, but a general oath is limited to the period of youth.

Oath on Lamb Meat after the Lamb Becomes a Ram
States that oath is broken if eating the meat after the animal has grown, unlike the dates case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on an oath regarding lamb meat after the animal grows and analyze the dispute with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An oath not to eat lamb meat is broken if the lamb becomes a ram because lamb lacks a quality justifying the oath.

This passage addresses a specific case of oaths: a person swears not to eat the meat of a particular lamb, then eats it after it has grown into a ram. The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, holds that the oath is broken and expiation is required, while the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar) argue the opposite. The difference turns on whether the lamb meat has an intrinsic quality (like harm or disgust) that would motivate the oath. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze the legal cause (ʿilla) behind oath rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is swearing not to eat the meat of a specific lamb, then eating it after it becomes a ram. The legal question: does this break the oath (hanth)? Abu Hanifa says yes; the three say no.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Position
Abu Hanifa argues that lamb meat does not have a quality that would prompt the oath; in fact, it is often more desirable than ram meat. Therefore, the oath was not based on a specific harmful quality, so the change does not preserve the oath's object.
The key concept is dā'iyya (motivating cause) for the oath.
3
Understand the Opposing View
The three imams hold that lamb meat has more moisture and is less praised, making it a valid reason for the oath. Since ram meat is drier and more praised, the oath's object has changed, so the oath is not broken.
They focus on the meat's physical quality differences.
4
Contrast with the Dates Case
The text implies this differs from an oath about dates that turn into vinegar. In that case, the oath is not broken because dates have a quality (potential to become vinegar) that is central to the oath. Lamb meat lacks such a dynamic quality.
This contrast illustrates the Hanafi method of looking for the motivating circumstance.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If one follows Abu Hanifa, eating the ram meat breaks the oath and requires kaffarah. If following the three, the oath remains intact and no expiation is needed. The text does not specify the mufta bihi, but in Hanafi practice, the majority view (the three) may be preferred.
Check later sources for tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الحمل: [ أي وبخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل هذا الحمل فأكله بعد ما صار كبشاً، فيحنث. (عيني)] الحمل: بفتحتين ولد الشاة في السنة الأولى، أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل لحم هذا الحمل، فأكل بعد ما صار كبشاً يحنث؛ لأنه ليس في الحمل صفة داعية إلى اليمين، فإن الممتنع عنه يكون أشد امتناعاً من لحم الكبش، وعند الثلاثة: لا يحنث أيضاً؛ لأن لحم الحمل لكثرة رطوباته ليس محموداً، فيكون داعياً إلى اليمين بخلاف لحم الكبش، فإنه أكثر قوة للبدن؛ لقلة الرطوبات. (مستخلص، عيني)
And this lamb: i.e., in contrast to the case where he swore not to eat this lamb, then he ate it after it became a ram, he breaks his oath. Lamb (hamal) with two fathas is the offspring of a sheep in its first year. That is, if he swore not to eat the meat of this lamb, then he ate after it became a ram, he breaks his oath because there is no quality in the lamb that prompts the oath, since the abstained meat is more avoidable than ram meat. According to the three, he also does not break his oath because the meat of the lamb due to its many moisture is not praised, so it is a prompt to the oath, unlike ram meat which is stronger for the body due to fewer moisture.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage addresses an oath regarding the meat of a lamb (hamal). If one swears not to eat the meat of a specific lamb and later eats the meat after it has grown into a ram, the oath is broken according to Abu Hanifa. The reason is that lamb meat does not have a quality that would cause aversion (such as being harmful); in fact, lamb meat is more tender and often preferred, so there is no specific reason for the oath. The three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) disagree, saying that lamb meat is less desirable due to its moisture, so the quality change does prompt the oath, and thus the oath is not broken. This again shows the Hanafi focus on the underlying cause (dā'iyya) of the oath.

Key Terms
حمل (ḥamal): A lamb, the young of a sheep in its first year.
كبش (kabsh): A ram, an adult male sheep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if one swears not to eat the meat of a specific lamb and later eats it after it becomes a ram, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break his oath because the animal changed.
B) He breaks his oath because lamb meat is not a cause for avoidance.
C) He breaks his oath only if the meat is still tender.
D) He does not break his oath because the meat quality improved.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to eat lamb meat is broken when the lamb becomes a ram, because lamb meat lacks a quality that would justify the oath, unlike the dates case where a dynamic quality preserves the oath.

Majority Rule in Purchase Oaths: The Case of Mixed Dates
States that oath on not buying rutab is not broken by buying a bunch with some rutab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that in purchase transactions, the majority determines the category for oath violations.

Core Idea
In purchase oaths, minority is subordinate to majority; in consumption, each piece matters.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling when a person swears not to buy fresh dates (rutab) but buys a bunch that is mostly unripe (busr) with a small amount of rutab. The distinction between buying and eating is key: in a single transaction, the whole is judged by its predominant part, while in eating, each morsel is considered individually.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the action
The oath is 'not to buy rutab' and the action is buying a kibāsa (bunch) that is predominantly busr with some rutab.
The bunch is a single unit in a purchase.
2
Recognize the two contexts
The passage contrasts buying with eating. In buying, the object is the whole bunch; in eating, the object is each individual date.
This distinction is crucial for the ruling.
3
Apply the majority principle
Since the majority of the bunch is busr, the bunch is considered busr for the purpose of the oath. The minor rutab is 'tabi' (follower) of the majority.
The principle: al-maghlub tabi' lil-ghalib.
4
Understand the 'illa
The reason ('illa) is that buying encounters the whole (al-jumla), so the minority is absorbed into the majority.
If the oath were on eating, the minority would be encountered alone and break the oath.
5
Distinguish the eating scenario
If the oath were 'not to eat rutab', eating even a single rutab would break the oath because eating is by individual pieces.
The passage explicitly states this exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يحنث بشراء كباسة إلخ الكباسة: بكسر الكاف والباء الموحدة وهي العقر والعرجون وهي بمنزلة العنقود من العنب يقال بالفارسية: "خوشه" أي من حلف لا يشتري رطبا فاشتري كباسة بسر فيها رطب قليل، لم يحنث؛ لأن الشراء يصادف الجملة والمغلوب تابع للغالب، وقيد بالشراء؛ لأنه إذا كان اليمين على الأكل يحنث؛ لما ذكرنا أن الأكل يصادفه وحده [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٥/١] (مستخلص) رطباً لأن القليل تابع للكثير ، ولو كان اليمين على الأكل يحنث. (ط، عيني)
And he does not break his oath by buying a bunch (kibāsa) etc. Kibāsa: with a kasra on the kaf and ba, it is the palm branch with dates, like a cluster of grapes, called in Persian: khūshah. That is, if someone swore not to buy rutab, then he bought a bunch of busr in which there is a little rutab, he does not break his oath because buying encounters the whole and the subordinate is a follower of the dominant. And it is restricted to buying because if the oath was on eating, he would break it, as we mentioned that eating encounters it alone. [Ramz al-Haqā'iq: 1/355] (Mukhtasar) Rutaban because the little is a follower of the much, but if the oath was on eating, he breaks it.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This short passage clarifies that in oaths regarding buying, the consideration is the majority of the item. If one swears not to buy fresh dates (rutab) and buys a bunch that is mostly unripe (busr) with only a small amount of rutab, the oath is not broken. This is because buying is a single transaction that encompasses the whole, and the minority is considered a follower of the majority. However, if the oath had been on eating, the ruling would differ: since eating is done piece by piece, one might encounter the forbidden piece alone, so the oath would be broken. This distinction between sale and consumption is consistent with the earlier discussion on mudhnab.

Key Terms
كباسة (kibāsa): A bunch of dates on a branch, similar to a grape cluster.
رطب (rutab): Fresh, ripe dates.
بسر (busr): Unripe dates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to buy rutab and then buys a bunch of dates that is mostly busr with a few rutab, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because rutab is present.
B) No, because the majority is busr.
C) Yes, because buying is like eating.
D) No, because he didn't intend that bunch.

Key Takeaway
In purchase oaths, the category of the majority determines the ruling; the minority is treated as a follower.

Fish and Oaths: Istihsan Overriding Qiyas
Preference for istihsan over apparent qiyas in oath rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating fish when sworn not to eat meat and explain the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
Fish is not 'meat' for oaths: istihsan overrides the literal qiyas from the Quran.

An oath not to eat meat seems straightforward until fish enters the picture. The Quran calls fish 'fresh meat', yet the Hanafi school rules that eating fish does not break such an oath. Why? Because the school gives weight to customary understanding and the underlying nature of meat, using the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) over strict analogy (qiyas). This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
State the Hanafi rule: a person who swears not to eat meat and then eats fish does not break the oath — unless he specifically intended to include fish.
Note the role of intention (niyyah): it can change the outcome.
2
Recognize the qiyas argument
Understand why analogical reasoning would find the person guilty: the Quran calls fish 'lahman' (meat), so fish should be included.
This qiyas relies on the apparent linguistic meaning of the text.
3
Understand the istihsan counter-argument
Learn the Hanafi response: the Quranic usage is metaphorical (majaz); true meat comes from blood, and fish lack blood. Also, fish does not require ritual slaughter (dhabah), unlike land animals.
Istihsan here is based on custom (urf) and the underlying rationale (illah) of the oath.
4
Examine the inter-madhhab disagreement
Note that the majority of schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) and even Abu Yusuf (according to one report) hold the opposite view: fish is meat.
This shows how different methodological choices lead to different rulings.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
Confirm the mufta bihi: the Hanafi fatwa follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad — fish is not meat unless intended.
The practical consequence is that no expiation (kaffarah) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبسمك في لا يأكل لحماً : أي لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً فأكل السمك لم يحنث إذا لم يكن له نية، فأما إذا نواه حنث طرياً أو غير طري، والقياس أن يحنث، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وأحمد وفي "المجمع": وهو قول أبي يوسف أيضاً؛ لأنه سمي في القرآن لحماً، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْ كُلِّ تَأْكُلُونَ لَحْماً طَرِيّا (فاطر: ١٢)، والمراد لحم السمك بالإجماع، وجه الاستحسان أن التسمية مجازية؛ لأن اللحم ينشأ من الدم ولا دم في السمك؛ إذ هو من سواكن الماء؛ إذ بين طبيعة الماء والدم مخالفة؛ لأن الماء بارد والدم حار؛ ولهذا أحل أكل السمك من غير ذكاة، =
And by fish in 'he does not eat meat': i.e., if he swore not to eat meat and then ate fish, he does not break his oath if he had no intention (to include fish). But if he intended it, he breaks it, whether fresh or not. Analogy (qiyas) dictates that he breaks it, and it is the view of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, and in al-Majma', it is also the view of Abu Yusuf. Because it is called meat in the Quran. Allah says: 'And from each you eat fresh meat' (35:12), and by consensus it means fish meat. The basis of istihsan is that the designation is metaphorical because meat originates from blood, and there is no blood in fish, as it is of the still waters, because the nature of water and blood are opposite: water is cold and blood is hot. For this reason, the eating of fish is permitted without ritual slaughter.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath not to eat meat (lahm). If someone swears not to eat meat and then eats fish, the Hanafi position (based on istihsan) is that he does not break the oath unless he specifically intended to include fish. The literal analogy (qiyas) would dictate that fish is meat because the Quran calls it 'lahman tariyyan' (fresh meat) in Surah Fatir, and this is agreed to refer to fish. However, the Hanafis argue that the Quranic usage is metaphorical (majaz), not literal, because true meat comes from blood, and fish have no blood. Hence, fish is not included in the common understanding of 'meat'. This is strengthened by the fact that fish does not require ritual slaughter (dhabah), which is necessary for land animals. The majority of scholars (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, and even Abu Yusuf apparently) disagree and hold that fish is included. The relied-upon Hanafi view is that the oath is not broken without intention.

Key Terms
لحم (lahm): Meat, specifically from land animals with blood. Fish is not included in the common understanding.
السمك (al-samak): Fish, aquatic creatures without blood that do not require ritual slaughter.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that gives weight to custom, necessity, or underlying meaning over strict analogy.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage; a word used in a non-literal sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears not to eat meat (lahm) and then eats fish without intending it, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because fish is called meat in the Quran.
B) He does not break his oath because fish is not considered meat customarily.
C) He breaks his oath only if the fish is fresh.
D) He does not break his oath regardless of intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan can override qiyas based on customary understanding, as seen in the ruling that fish is not meat for oath purposes unless specifically intended.

Oaths and Custom: Hanafi Ruling on Eating Pork, Liver, and Tripe
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on whether pork, human flesh, liver, and tripe break an oath not to eat meat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether eating pork, human flesh, liver, and tripe breaks an oath not to eat meat, and explain the role of custom (urf) in interpreting oaths.

Core Idea
Oaths are interpreted by custom, not literal definitions — pork and liver may not break an oath if not considered meat by custom.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq tackles a nuanced fiqh question: if someone swears not to eat meat, does eating pork, human flesh, liver, or tripe break the oath? The answer depends on whether these are considered 'meat' in common usage. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reliance on custom (urf) and how it leads to different rulings compared to other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruled Act and its Hukm
Recognize that the act in question is eating specific items after an oath not to eat meat. The hukm is whether hanth (oath-breaking) occurs.
The hukm is not a standard wajib/haram; it determines if the oath is broken.
2
State the Hanafi Position (Original and Corrected)
The original Zahir al-Riwayah view says all four break the oath. The corrected (sahih) view says pork and human flesh do not break the oath due to custom; liver and tripe also do not break according to later custom.
The text explicitly says 'al-sahih' and 'al-fatawa 'ala al-urf'.
3
Identify the Opposing School and Their Position
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold that liver and tripe do not break the oath either, but for them it is because these are not called meat in common parlance, not specifically based on custom.
The text mentions: 'عند الشافعي وأحمد: لا يحنث في كبد وكرش'.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: In the original Hanafi view, liver/tripe break the oath; in the later custom-based Hanafi view, they do not. The Shafi'i/Hanbali view also says no. For pork, the Hanafi mufta bihi says no, while Shafi'i might say yes if he considers it meat.
Understanding the thamarah helps in real-life fatwa: the outcome changes based on which position is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولحم الخنزير إلخ أي . من حلف لا يأكل لحماً، فأكل لحم الخنزير أو لحم الآدمي أو أكل الكبد أو الكرش يحنث؛ لأنها لحم حقيقة، والصحيح أنه لا يحنث بلحم الخنزير والآدمي؛ لأن أكلهما ليس بمتعارف، الأيمان ومبنى على العرف وعليه الفتوى، وبه قال بعض المالكية، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: لا يحنث في كبد وكرش، وكذا الرئة والطحال عندنا، خلافاً لهما في الرئة، وفي 'المحيط': لا يحنث بأكل الكبد والكرش في عرفنا؛ لأنهما لا يعدان لحما، ويحنث عند أهل الكوفة. (مستخلص) والإنسان بالجر عطف على الخنزير أي ولحم الإنسان والكبد والكرش مرفوعان عطفا على اللحم. (عيني) والكرش الكرش لكل محتر بمنزلة المعدة للإنسان. [رد المحتار ٣٩٣/١] لحم خبر المبتدأ أي لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً يحنث بأكل هذه الأشياء. (عيني)
And regarding pork, human flesh, liver, and tripe: They are meat. That is, whoever swears not to eat meat, and eats pork or human flesh or eats liver or tripe, breaks his oath because they are meat in reality. However, the correct position is that he does not break his oath by eating pork or human flesh because eating them is not customary; oaths are based on custom, and that is the fatwa. Some Malikis said the same. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: He does not break his oath by liver and tripe. Likewise, lung and spleen according to us, contrary to them in lung. In al-Muhit: He does not break his oath by eating liver and tripe in our custom because they are not considered meat, and he breaks his oath according to the people of Kufa. (Mustakhlas) 'Human' is in the genitive as a conjunction to 'pig', meaning human flesh; 'liver' and 'tripe' are nominative conjoined to 'meat'. (Ayni) 'Tripe' is the tripe for every ruminant animal comparable to the stomach of humans. [Radd al-Muhtar 1/393] 'Meat' is the predicate of the subject, meaning if he swears not to eat meat, he breaks his oath by eating these things. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the scope of an oath not to eat meat. The text first states the matn that pork, human flesh, liver, and tripe are considered meat, so eating them would break the oath. However, the commentator then corrects this: the correct position (al-sahih) is that pork and human flesh do NOT break the oath because they are not commonly eaten, and oaths are based on custom (urf). This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The text then notes that some Malikis agreed. Then it contrasts with the positions of Shafi'i and Ahmad, who do not break the oath with liver and tripe either (unlike the Hanafi ruling that they do break it, at least in the original view). The commentary also quotes from al-Muhit that in our custom (later Hanafi region), liver and tripe are not considered meat, so an oath is not broken. This shows the evolution of the ruling based on regional custom. The passage ends with grammatical analysis.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath invoking Allah's name or an attribute, binding the swearer to do or refrain from an act.
عرف (urf): Common custom or practice that determines the interpretation of an oath's subject matter.
حنث (hanth): The act of breaking an oath, incurring sin and requirement of expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, does eating pork break an oath not to eat meat?
A) Yes, because pork is meat in reality.
B) No, because eating pork is not customary.
C) Yes, because pork is explicitly prohibited.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted based on local custom (urf); therefore, foods not commonly considered meat do not break an oath to avoid meat, even if they are technically meat.

Back Fat in Oaths: A Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether back fat is considered fat for oath purposes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on back fat in oaths and explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, using the concept of istithna munqati'.

Core Idea
Back fat is not fat for oath purposes according to Abu Hanifa; it is fatty meat.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning oaths about 'fat' (shahm). The key question: if someone swears not to eat fat and then eats back fat (shahm al-zahr), is the oath broken? Abu Hanifa says no, while his two companions (Sahibayn) say yes. Understanding their proofs and the underlying usul principle of disconnected exception (istithna munqati') is essential for accurate fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds regarding back fat in oaths. Note that he considers back fat to be fatty meat (lahm samin), not true fat, so the oath is not broken.
This is the basis of the dispute.
2
Identify the dissenting view
State that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that back fat is considered fat and thus breaks the oath. Also note that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad in one narration agree with them.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Analyze the Sahibayn's dalil: they cite Quran 6:146 (the exception clause) and argue that the exception must be from the same genus, and that back fat has the property of fat (it melts).
They use a linguistic argument based on the nature of exception.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's counter-argument: the exception in the Quran is disconnected (munqati'), meaning it excludes something not of the same genus. Also, custom treats back fat as meat.
This relies on usul al-fiqh concept of istithna munqati'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, the oath is not broken; according to the Sahibayn, it is. The mufta bihi (operative view) is Abu Hanifa's, especially since Muhammad reportedly agreed with him.
The later note about Persian oaths adds a nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشحم الظهر في 'شحماً' : [ أي لو حلف لا يأكل شحماً فأكل شحم الظهر لا يحنث عند أبي حنيفة. واعلم أن الشحوم أربعة شحم الظهر، وشحم مختلط بالعظم، وشحم على ظاهر الأمعاء، وشحم البطن، ولا خلاف في الحنث في شحم البطن وما على الأمعاء كما لا خلاف في عدم الحنث بما في العظم وشحم الظهر، وقالا يحنث بشحم الظهر أيضاً، قال الشافعي في وجه، وأحمد في رواية؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَّا مَا حَمَلَتْ و به ظُهُورُهُمَا (الأنعام: ١٤٦)، والأصل في الاستثناء أن يكون من الجنس، ولأنه شحم حقيقة وفيه خاصيته، ألا ترى أنه مذاب كشحم البطن، ولأبي حنيفة: أن شحم الظهر هو اللحم السمين، ويستعمل استعمال اللحوم، وما ذكر في النص استثناء منقطع، وذكر الطحاوي قول محمد مع أبي حنيفة، وقيل: هذا إذا حلف بالعربية، أما لو قال بالفارسية: 'بي' لا يقع على شحم الظهر أصلا. (فتح، عيني)
And regarding back fat in the oath 'fat': Meaning, if he swears not to eat fat and he eats back fat, he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa. Know that fats are four: back fat, fat mixed with bone, fat on the outer intestines, and belly fat. There is no disagreement in breaking the oath by belly fat and what is on the intestines, just as there is no disagreement in not breaking it by what is in bone and back fat. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He also breaks the oath by back fat. Al-Shafi'i said in one opinion, and Ahmad in one narration: Because of Allah's statement: 'Except what is carried on their backs' (al-An'am 6:146), and the principle in exception is that it is from the same genus, and because it is fat in reality and has its specific property; do you not see that it melts like belly fat? For Abu Hanifa: Back fat is fatty meat, and it is used like meat, and what is mentioned in the text is a disconnected exception. Al-Tahawi mentioned that Muhammad agreed with Abu Hanifa. It is said: This if he swears in Arabic; but if he says in Persian 'bi' it does not apply to back fat at all. (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage discusses an oath regarding 'fat' (shahm). It enumerates four types of fat. The ruling: There is consensus that belly fat and intestinal fat break the oath, while fat inside bones and back fat do not break the oath according to Abu Hanifa. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that back fat does break the oath because it is fat in reality, citing the Quranic exception (Quran 6:146) which implies that back fat is of the same genus as prohibited fat. They also argue it has the property of melting like belly fat. Abu Hanifa's counterargument: Back fat is actually fatty meat, used as meat, and the Quranic exception is a disconnected exception (istithna munqati'), meaning it excludes something not of the same genus. The passage notes that Muhammad's position is reported to agree with Abu Hanifa, and that if the oath is in Persian, the word 'bi' (fat) does not cover back fat at all.

Key Terms
شحم (shahm): Fat; in this context, the subject of an oath not to eat fat.
استثناء منقطع (istithna munqati'): A disconnected exception where the excepted item is not of the same genus as the general term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, does eating back fat (shahm al-zahr) break an oath not to eat fat?
A) Yes, because it is fat in reality.
B) No, because it is considered fatty meat.
C) Yes, because it melts like belly fat.
D) No, because the oath is null.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes back fat from true fat for oath purposes, relying on custom and the concept of disconnected exception.

The Tail in Oaths: Not Meat, Not Fat
Ruling that tail is neither meat nor fat, so oaths are not broken

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating tail (aliyah) in an oath about meat or fat and explain that the tail is excluded based on literal meaning and custom.

Core Idea
Tail is neither meat nor fat; oaths about them remain unbroken.

This short passage addresses a specific case in oaths: if someone swears not to eat meat or fat, does eating the sheep's tail break the oath? The Hanafi school makes a precise distinction: the tail is a third type, not meat or fat. This ruling highlights how oaths are interpreted according to common custom (urf) and literal meaning, not biological category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The scenario: a person swears not to eat or buy meat or fat, then eats or buys the tail (aliyah) of a sheep. Does this break the oath?
The issue hinges on whether the tail is considered meat or fat.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the Hanafi school, the oath is not broken. The tail is neither meat nor fat, but a separate entity.
This is the clear position presented in the text.
3
Examine the Hanafi rationale
The Hanafi position relies on literal meaning and custom: the word 'meat' or 'fat' does not include the tail in common usage. It is a third type.
The commentary emphasizes 'ma'nan wa urfan' – meaning and custom.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
Imam Ahmad and some Shafi'is hold that the tail is covered, so the oath would be broken. Their proof is not given in the text but presumably considers the tail as part of the animal or fatty tissue.
This is an inter-madhhab difference.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
A Hanafi following this view would not need to expiate (kaffarah) for eating the tail, while a Shafi'i or Hanbali would consider the oath broken and require expiation.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the need for kaffarah or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبألية في 'لحماً أو شحماً ' : [ أي لا يحنث أيضاً بأكل ألية أو شرائها في حلفه 'لا يأكل أو لا يشتري لحما أو شحماً'. (عيني)] الألية بفتح الهمزة وسكون اللام ذنب كما في الصراح أي لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً أو شحماً فأكل ألية لا يحنث؛ لأنها ليس بلحم ولا شحم، وإنما هو نوع ثالث، فلا يتناولها اللفظ معنى ولا عرفاً، وعند أحمد وبعض الشافعية: يحنث بأكلها وشرائها إذا حلف أن لا يأكل ولا يشتري لحماً ولا شحماً. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٦/١] (فتح)
And regarding the tail (aliyah) in the oath 'meat or fat': Meaning, he also does not break his oath by eating or buying the tail when he swears 'not to eat or buy meat or fat'. (Ayni) 'Aliyah' with a fatha on the hamza and sukun on the lam is the tail, as in al-Sirah. That is, if he swears not to eat meat or fat and he eats the tail, he does not break his oath because it is not meat nor fat; rather it is a third type, so the word does not include it in meaning nor custom. According to Ahmad and some Shafi'is: He breaks his oath by eating it or buying it if he swears not to eat or buy meat or fat. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:356] (Fath)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This short passage addresses the ruling on the tail (aliyah) in an oath not to eat or buy meat or fat. The Hanafi position is that the tail is neither meat nor fat but a third category, so an oath regarding meat or fat does not cover it. The text contrasts this with Ahmad and some Shafi'is who hold that the tail is included. The commentary relies on the principle that oaths are interpreted by common custom (urf) and literal meaning, and since the tail is not normally referred to as meat or fat, it falls outside the oath.

Key Terms
ألية (aliyah): The tail of a sheep, considered a separate entity from meat and fat.

Oath on 'This Wheat': Does It Include Bread?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of an oath on wheat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether an oath on 'this wheat' covers its processed forms, and explain the underlying usul principle of haqiqah vs majaz muta'arif.

Core Idea
An oath on 'this wheat' binds only the grain for Abu Hanifa, but bread too for the Sahibayn — the dispute turns on whether literal or common metaphorical meaning governs.

When a person swears not to eat from a specific batch of wheat, does eating bread made from it break the oath? This question reveals a key usul al-fiqh debate within the Hanafi school: should we follow the literal meaning (haqiqah) or the commonly used metaphorical meaning (majaz muta'arif)? The answer differs between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, and the practical consequence affects whether kaffarah becomes due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the actions
The oath is 'I will not eat from this wheat' (لا آكل من هذا البر). The actions under dispute are: eating bread from that wheat, and eating sawiq (parched barley) from it.
Also included: biting the whole grain — consensus that this breaks the oath.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that only the whole grain breaks the oath; bread and sawiq do not. His reasoning: the literal meaning (haqiqah) of 'wheat' is the grain itself, and it should be preferred over any metaphorical usage, even if the metaphor is common.
The exception is when the literal meaning has become obsolete (like the date palm case where 'date' is no longer used for the whole tree).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that bread does break the oath. Abu Yusuf includes bread but not sawiq; Muhammad includes both bread and sawiq. Their reasoning: when a word has both a literal and a common metaphorical meaning, the common metaphor (majaz muta'arif) should be preferred, because that is how people actually use the language.
The 'three' (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and another) agree with Abu Yusuf on bread but differ on sawiq.
4
Understand the usul principle: haqiqah vs majaz muta'arif
This dispute is based on a fundamental principle of Hanafi usul: when a word has a valid literal meaning and also a common metaphorical usage, which takes priority? Abu Hanifa says the literal (even if rare), the Sahibayn say the common metaphorical. This principle applies to many other cases beyond oaths.
For example, the word 'finding' (wujid) in zakat: does it mean finding physically or having the ability? Different rulings follow.
5
Note the role of intention (niyyah)
If the person swearing has a specific intention (e.g., he only meant the grain), then that intention controls by consensus. The dispute only arises when no specific intention is present.
Intention can refer to either the literal or metaphorical meaning, as long as it is plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالخبز في 'هذا البر': أي من حلف لا يأكل من هذا البر، فأكل الخبز منه لا يحنث بل يحنث بقضم الحنطة عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: إن أكل من خبزها يحنث أيضاً، وهذا مبني على أصل مختلف بينهم، وهو أن اللفظ إذا كانت لا يحنث حقيقة مستعملة ومجاز متعارف فالمجاز المتعارف أولى عندهما، وعند أبي حنيفة: الحقيقة أولى وإن كانت نادرة، إلا إذا كانت مهجورة كما في مسألة النخلة، وعلى هذا الأصل يبتني كثير من المسائل، وكذا إذا أكل من سويقه لا يحنث عند أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف يحنث بأكل الخبز لا بالسويق وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقال محمد: يحنث في السويق أيضاً، وإن قضم الحنطة حنث بالإجماع، والخلاف فيما إذا لم تكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى فهو كما نوى بالإجماع؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه أو محتمله وهو مجاز. (عيني، فتح)
And regarding bread in the oath 'this wheat': Meaning, if he swears not to eat from this wheat, and he eats bread made from it, he does not break his oath; rather he breaks it by biting the wheat grain according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions said: If he eats from its bread, he also breaks his oath. This is based on a foundational difference between them: when a word has both a used reality and a common metaphor, the common metaphor is preferred according to them, while according to Abu Hanifa the reality is preferred even if rare, unless it has become obsolete as in the date palm case. Many issues are built on this principle. Likewise, if he eats from its sawiq (parched barley), he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He breaks his oath by eating bread, not by sawiq, and the three (other imams) said the same. Muhammad said: He also breaks his oath by sawiq. If he bites the wheat, he breaks the oath by consensus. The disagreement is when he has no specific intention. If he intended something, then it is as he intended by consensus, because he intended the reality of his speech or its probable metaphorical meaning. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage discusses an oath concerning 'this wheat' (hadha al-barr). Abu Hanifa holds that the oath applies only to the wheat grain itself, not to its processed forms like bread or sawiq. Thus, eating bread from that wheat does not break the oath; only biting the whole grain does. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that bread is included because it is a common metaphorical extension of the word. This reflects a usul al-fiqh principle: when a word has both a literal meaning (haqiqah) and a common metaphorical usage (majaz muta'arif), Abu Hanifa prefers the literal even if rare, while the Sahibayn prefer the common metaphor. The exception is when the literal meaning has become obsolete. The passage also notes Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's differences on sawiq, and that there is consensus that biting the grain breaks the oath. If the swearer had a specific intention (niyyah), it controls the interpretation by consensus.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word as originally coined.
مجاز متعارف (majaz muta'arif): A metaphor that is commonly used and conventionally understood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the foundational principle underlying the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding oaths on wheat?
A) Whether the word 'wheat' has a clear literal meaning
B) Whether the common metaphorical meaning or the literal meaning should be preferred
C) Whether the oath was sworn in Arabic or another language
D) Whether the person intended to include bread or not

Key Takeaway
The scope of an oath on 'this wheat' depends on whether you prioritize literal or common metaphorical meaning, leading to different rulings on bread and sawiq within the Hanafi school.

Oath on Flour: Bread vs Raw Flour (Suff)
Ruling that bread from flour breaks the oath, but eating raw flour (suff) does not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of custom (urf) to determine whether an oath on flour is broken by eating raw flour (suff).

Core Idea
In oaths, custom (urf) determines the true subject; flour is not normally eaten raw, so eating dry flour does not break the oath.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on oaths involving flour. When someone swears not to eat from a specific flour, does eating that flour in its raw dry form (suff) constitute breaking the oath? The Hanafi school, relying on custom, distinguishes between eating bread (which breaks the oath) and eating raw flour (which does not). We will explore the proof, the Shafi'i opposition, and the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath subject
The oath is 'I will not eat from this flour.' The subject is the flour itself, but its customary use is to make bread.
Focus on the object: the flour, not its derivatives.
2
Apply the custom principle
Hanafi fiqh uses custom (urf) to interpret oaths. Since flour is not normally eaten raw, the oath is understood to refer to its customary form: bread.
Custom overrides literal meaning in ambiguous oaths.
3
Distinguish between bread and suff
Eating bread made from the flour breaks the oath because bread is the customary product. Eating the flour dry (suff) does not break the oath because that is not the normal way to consume flour.
The text states 'al-sahih' for the Hanafi view.
4
Compare with Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i argues that eating raw flour is still eating flour literally, so the oath is broken. The Hanafi response is that custom restricts the literal meaning.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul.
5
Note the role of intention
If the swearer intended to eat the flour itself (not its product), then the intention overrides custom, and eating bread would not break the oath.
Intention is decisive when expressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي 'هذا الدقيق' حنث بخبزه لا بسفه، والخبز ما اعتاده بلده، والشواء والطبيخ على اللحم والرأس ما يباع في مصره. نادرة، إلا إذا كانت مهجورة كما في مسألة النخلة، وعلى هذا الأصل يبتني كثير من المسائل، وكذا إذا أكل من سويقه لا يحنث عند أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف يحنث بأكل الخبز لا بالسويق وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقال محمد: يحنث في السويق أيضاً، وإن قضم الحنطة حنث بالإجماع، والخلاف فيما إذا لم تكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى فهو كما نوى بالإجماع؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه أو محتمله وهو مجاز. (عيني، فتح) حنث بخبزه فانصرفت اليمين إلى ما يتخذ منه. (عيني) أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق يحنث بأكل خبزه؛ لأن عين الدقيق لا توكل عادة، وقوله: بسفه أي لا يحنث بأكل الدقيق سفوفاً بأن يوضع على الكف ويبتلع من غير مضغ وهو الصحيح، وعند الشافعي: يحنث بسفه أيضاً؛ لأنه أكل الدقيق حقيقة، ولو نوى أكل الدقيق بعينه لم يحنث بأكل الخبز ؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه. (مستخلص، عيني)
And in the oath 'this flour', he breaks his oath by its bread, not by eating it raw (suff). Bread is what is customary in his country. Roast and cooked food are restricted to meat, and head is what is sold in his town. ... Rare, unless it is obsolete as in the date palm case, and on this principle many issues are built. Likewise, if he eats from its sawiq, he does not break the oath according to Abu Hanifa; Abu Yusuf said he breaks by eating bread but not by sawiq, and the three said the same; Muhammad said he also breaks by sawiq. If he bites the wheat, he breaks the oath by consensus. The disagreement is when he has no intention. If he intended, it is as he intended by consensus, because he intended the reality of his speech or its probable metaphorical meaning. (Ayni, Fath) He breaks by its bread, so the oath refers to what is made from it. (Ayni) Meaning, if he swears not to eat from this flour, he breaks by eating its bread because the flour itself is not normally eaten. His statement 'not by its raw form' means he does not break by eating flour dry (suff) by placing it on the palm and swallowing without chewing; that is the correct view. According to al-Shafi'i, he also breaks by eating it dry because he actually ate flour. If he intended to eat the flour itself, he does not break by eating bread because he intended the reality of his speech. (Mustakhlas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage continues with an oath regarding 'this flour' (hadha al-daqiq). The ruling: eating bread made from that flour breaks the oath, but eating the flour dry (suff) does not, because flour is not customarily eaten alone. The correct view (al-sahih) is that he does not break the oath by eating it dry. Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that eating flour is literally eating flour. If the swearer intended specifically to eat the flour itself, then eating bread does not break the oath. The passage also reiterates the principle that intention overrides. This lesson combines two brief discussions: first on flour, then a reiteration of earlier principles.

Key Terms
دقيق (daqiq): Flour; the substance ground from grain.
سفوف (suff): Eating dry flour by placing it in the palm and swallowing without chewing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position (al-sahih), does eating dry flour (suff) break an oath not to eat from that flour?
A) Yes, because flour is the substance sworn on.
B) No, because flour is not normally eaten alone.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custom (urf) determines the scope of oaths, so an oath on flour is only broken by eating its customary product (bread), not by eating it raw.

Custom Defines Bread in Oaths
Ruling on interpreting oath by custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath on 'bread' is interpreted by local custom, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i/Maliki literalist view.

Core Idea
An oath on 'bread' is bound by custom, not by universal definition.

When someone swears not to eat bread, does that include all baked flour products, or only what people in his town consider bread? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq gives the Hanafi answer: custom is decisive. Understanding this principle prevents unnecessary oath-breaking and demonstrates how 'urf (custom) operates as a source of law in the Hanafi school, especially in matters of oaths and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly that the oath refers only to bread customary in the swearer's country. The default is wheat and barley bread in most lands.
2
Note the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i and Malik hold that any type of bread breaks the oath, based on the literal meaning of the word.
3
Understand the custom principle
The Hanafi school prioritizes customary usage ('urf) over literal definitions in oaths, because the swearer's intention and common understanding determine the meaning.
This is a branch of the broader rule 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.
4
Apply to scenarios
Consider: rice bread, qata'if (pancakes), or other forms. If not customary, the oath is not broken unless the swearer specifically intended them.
5
Identify the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: a Hanafi can eat non-customary bread without kaffarah; a Shafi'i cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخبز ما اعتاده بلده [ أي أهل بلد الحالف حتى لو أكل الحالف خلاف ما عندهم من الخبز لا يحنث. (عيني)] حلف لا يأكل خبزاً فيمينه ينصرف على خبز يعتاده أهل بلده كخبز الحنطة والشعير في عامة الديار؛ لأنه المتبادر الغالب المعتاد، فلو أكل خبز القطائف أي بالفارسية 'نان' لو 'زينة' لا يحنث؛ لأنه ليس بخبز مطلقاً . إلا إذا نواه؛ لأنه محتمل كلامه ولا يحنث بخبز الأرز في بلد لا يعتادونه ولو كان طعامهم أو أكثر طعامهم خبز الأرز يحنث به، وعند الشافعي ومالك يحنث بأي خبز كان. (مستخلص، عيني)
And bread is what is customary in his country [i.e., of the people of the swearer's country, so if he eats bread contrary to their custom, he does not break his oath. (Ayni)] If he swears not to eat bread, his oath refers to bread that his people customarily eat, such as wheat bread and barley bread in most lands, because that is the immediate, prevalent, customary meaning. So if he eats 'khubz al-qata'if' (a type of pancake) i.e., in Persian 'nan' or 'zina', he does not break his oath because it is not bread at all, unless he intended it, because it is a possible meaning of his words. He does not break his oath by rice bread in a country where they do not customarily eat it, but if their food or most of their food is rice bread, he does break by it. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, he breaks by any bread. (Mustakhlas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage clarifies that an oath not to eat 'bread' is interpreted according to the custom of the swearer's locality. The default types are wheat and barley bread. Novel forms like qata'if (pancakes) or rice bread are not included unless customarily eaten or intended. This contrasts with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions, which include any type of bread.

Key Terms
خبز (khubz): Bread; in oaths, it is defined by local custom, not literally all baked flour products.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In Hanafi fiqh, an oath on 'bread' is limited to the bread types customary in the swearer's locality, not all types of bread.

Oath on Roast or Cooked Food: Custom Determines the Meaning
Ruling on oath interpretation based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh restricts oaths on 'roast' and 'cooked food' to meat dishes based on custom, and distinguish the Shafi'i/Maliki position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the meaning of 'roast' and 'cooked food' in oaths is determined by custom, not literal definition.

This passage addresses a common legal question: if a person swears not to eat 'roast' or 'cooked food', what exactly is included? The answer depends heavily on the custom of the time and place. The early Hanafi custom in Kufa restricted these terms to meat dishes, but later custom expanded 'cooked food' to include anything cooked in water. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools, by contrast, adhere to the literal meaning. Understanding how custom (urf) operates in Hanafi fiqh is essential for correctly applying oath rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
In the early Hanafi custom, 'roast' (shawa) and 'cooked food' (tabikh) in oaths refer only to meat-based dishes. So an oath not to eat roast or cooked food only applies to roasted or cooked meat, not vegetables.
This is based on the custom of the people of Kufa.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik hold that these terms apply to all roasts and all cooked foods, regardless of meat content, because the literal meaning covers everything.
They prioritize linguistic generality over custom.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's proof is the literal and unrestricted meaning of the words 'roast' and 'cooked food'.
They argue that custom cannot restrict the clear linguistic meaning in oaths.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the meaning of such terms in oaths follows the custom of the people swearing the oath. Since language is shaped by usage, custom is the arbiter.
This is reinforced by the note that later Hanafi custom expanded tabikh to include anything cooked in water.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
A Hanafi following early custom would not break his oath by eating roasted eggplant, while a Shafi'i would. Later Hanafis might consider roasted eggplant as tabikh if local custom so defines it.
Thus, the fatwa depends on the time and place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشواء والطبيخ على اللحم أي لو حلف لا يأكل الشواء أو الطبيخ ولا نية له تقع يمينه على اللحم المشوي وعلى المطبوخ من اللحم دون الباذنجان والجزر المشويين ونحوهما، وهذا في عرفهم؛ لأن الطبيخ عندهم ما طبخ بلحم أو شحم لا ما طبخ بسمن أو زيت، وأما في عرفنا فاسم الطبيخ يقع على كل مطبوخ بالماء، فالقياس فيه أن يحنث فيما هو مطبوخ وعند الشافعي ومالك: يقع على كل شواء بلا نية وإذا أكل المقلية اليابسة لا يحنث؛ لأنه لا . طبيخاً، وإن أكل الخبز بالمرقة يحنث؛ لأنه يسمى طبيخاً وفيها أجزاء اللحم أيضاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٦/١] (فتح)
And roast and cooked food refer to meat: That is, if he swears not to eat roast or cooked food and has no intention, his oath applies to roasted meat and cooked meat, not to roasted eggplant, carrots, and the like. This was in their custom (of early Hanafis) because 'cooked food' (tabikh) for them was what was cooked with meat or fat, not what was cooked with ghee or oil. In our custom, the term 'tabikh' applies to everything cooked in water, so by analogy he would break his oath by anything cooked. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, it applies to every roast without intention. If he eats dry fried food (muqliyah yabisah) he does not break his oath because it is not called tabikh. If he eats bread with broth, he breaks his oath because it is called tabikh and it contains parts of meat as well. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:356] (Fath)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses oaths regarding 'roast' (shawa) and 'cooked food' (tabikh). In the earlier custom of the Hanafi school (specifically the people of Kufa), these terms referred specifically to meat dishes. So an oath not to eat roast or cooked food only applies to roasted or cooked meat, not to vegetable dishes. However, the text notes that in the commentator's own time (later custom), 'tabikh' has a broader meaning including all food cooked in water. It also contrasts with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions that apply to any roast. Examples: dry fried foods are not called tabikh, but bread with broth is tabikh. This shows how custom evolves and affects fatwa.

Key Terms
شواء (shawa): Roasted food; in oaths, originally restricted to roasted meat.
طبيخ (tabikh): Cooked food; originally meat or fat-based, later any food cooked in water.
يمين (yamin): Oath; a solemn promise invoking God's name.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the early Hanafi custom, does an oath not to eat 'roast' cover roasted eggplant?
A) Yes
B) No

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the interpretation of terms in oaths is governed by the custom of the speaker, not the literal dictionary meaning.

Oath on 'Head': The Role of Custom in Defining Terms
Ruling that oath on 'head' means heads of animals commonly sold in the swearer's town

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school uses custom (urf) to define ambiguous terms in oaths and identify the mufta bihi position on which animal heads are included.

Core Idea
An oath on 'head' follows the custom of the market, not the dictionary.

This passage deals with a common legal scenario: someone swears not to eat 'head' (ra's). What does he mean? The Hanafi school looks to the custom of the swearer's town. We trace the evolution of the ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa's initial view to the later fatwa, and contrast it with Shafi'i and Maliki views. Understanding this shows how custom (urf) operates as a source of law in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the ambiguity of 'head'
Recognize that the word 'head' (ra's) is ambiguous. The Hanafi school does not take its literal meaning; it customizes based on what is commonly sold and eaten in the swearer's town.
This is rooted in the usul principle that ambiguous terms are interpreted by custom.
2
Trace the evolution within Abu Hanifa's view and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa initially included camel, cow, and sheep based on Kufan custom. When the custom changed (camel heads no longer sold), he retracted to cow and sheep. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) limited it to sheep only based on Baghdad custom.
Note: this is a difference of time and place, not of proof.
3
Identify the proof: custom (urf) and its adaptability
The primary evidence is the custom of the town. The text says 'because we know he does not mean every head' – this is a rational inference (dalil aqli) based on common practice.
Custom is a valid source (dalil) in Hanafi fiqh, especially when texts are ambiguous.
4
Contrast with Shafi'i and Maliki views
Imam al-Shafi'i includes camel head; Imam Malik includes every head. Their evidence is the literal meaning of the word.
This difference is not over evidence but over the weight given to custom vs. literalism.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The text explicitly states 'وعليه الفتوى' (and that is the fatwa) for sheep head. Thus the relied-upon position is the narrowest: only sheep.
This reflects the later Hanafi tendency to adopt the more cautious view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرأس ما يباع في مصره أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل رأساً فيمينه على رؤوس تكبس في التنانير وتباع في مصره؛ لأنا نعلم أنه لا يريد به رأس كل شيء، فإن رأس الجراد والعصفور لا يدخل تحته، ويدخل رأس الغنم وعليه الفتوى، وكان أبو حنيفة يقول :أولاً : يدخل فيه رأس الإبل والبقر والغنم مما رأى من عادة أهل الكوفة، ثم لما تركوها هذه العادة في الإبل ،رجع، فقال: يحنث في رأس البقر والغنم خاصة، وقالا: لا يحنث إلا في رأس الغنم خاصة لما شاهدا عادة أهل بغداد فعلم أن هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان لا اختلاف حجة وبرهان، وعند الشافعي: يحنث برأس الإبل أيضاً، وعند مالك: يحنث بكل رأس. (عيني، مسكين)
And head is what is sold in his town: That is, if he swears not to eat head, his oath applies to heads that are baked in ovens and sold in his town, because we know he does not mean every head. The head of locusts and sparrows are not included, while sheep heads are included, and this is the fatwa. Abu Hanifa initially said: The head of camels, cows, and sheep are included, based on the custom of the people of Kufa. Then when they abandoned that custom regarding camels, he retracted and said: He breaks the oath by cow and sheep heads only. The two companions said: He only breaks the oath by sheep heads because they observed the custom of the people of Baghdad. This is a difference of time and era, not a difference of proof and evidence. According to al-Shafi'i: He also breaks by camel head. According to Malik: He breaks by every head. (Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This final passage discusses an oath not to eat 'head' (ra's). The ruling depends on the custom of the swearer's town. Initially, Abu Hanifa included the heads of camels, cows, and sheep based on Kufan custom. When that custom changed (people stopped selling camel heads), he revised his view to include only cow and sheep heads. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) limited it to sheep heads only, based on Baghdad custom. The text emphasizes that this difference is due to changing customs, not a fundamental disagreement. Shafi'i and Malik have broader interpretations.

Key Terms
رأس (ra's): Head of an animal; in oaths, it refers to heads commonly sold in the market, not bird heads.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All animal heads
B) Sheep head only
C) Cow and sheep heads
D) Camel, cow, and sheep heads

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the meaning of ambiguous terms in oaths is determined by the custom of the swearer's town, and the operative ruling is that only sheep heads are included.

Custom Interprets the Oath: Flour Bread vs Raw
Ruling on oath about flour: custom determines hanth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath is interpreted according to custom (‘urf) to determine whether eating flour raw constitutes a violation (hanth).

Core Idea
An oath about flour is broken by bread, not raw consumption — custom governs.

When someone swears 'I will not eat this flour', what counts as eating it? The Hanafi school answers based on custom: flour is not customarily eaten raw, so the oath is only broken by consuming it in the customary way, such as bread. This lesson explores the ruling and the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its literal wording
State the oath: 'I will not eat this flour.' Literally, flour is the substance. Determine what the oath-taker meant.
The wording is general; intention matters if stated.
2
Classify the consumption: customary vs. literal
Eating bread made from flour is customary; eating dry flour by itself (suff) is not customary. Distinguish between customary consumption (bread) and non-customary (raw).
Custom varies by place; the ruling assumes flour is not eaten raw.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle: ‘urf governs the oath
Hanafi usul: an oath is interpreted by what is customary (urf) unless the oath-taker intended otherwise. Thus, only bread counts as eating the flour.
The matn states: 'حنث بخبزه لا بسفه'.
4
Compare with Shafi'i position
Shafi'i holds that eating raw flour breaks the oath because the literal act occurred. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi requires no expiation; Shafi'i does.
The Hanafi position is relied upon (al-sahih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي 'هذا الدقيق' حنث بخبزه لا بسُفّه، نادرة إلا إذا كانت مهجورة كما في مسألة النخلة، وعلى هذا الأصل يبتني كثير من المسائل، وكذا إذا أكل من سويقه لا يحنث عند أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف يحنث بأكل الخبز لا بالسويق وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقال محمد: يحنث في السويق أيضاً، وإن قضم الحنطة حنث بالإجماع، والخلاف فيما إذا لم تكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى فهو كما نوى بالإجماع؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه أو محتمله وهو مجاز. (عيني، فتح) حنث بخبزه فانصرفت اليمين إلى ما يتخذ منه. (عيني) أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق يحنث بأكل خبزه؛ لأن عين الدقيق لا توكل عادة، وقوله: بسفه أي لا يحنث بأكل الدقيق سفوفاً بأن يوضع على الكف ويبتلع من غير مضغ وهو الصحيح، وعند الشافعي: يحنث بسفه أيضاً؛ لأنه أكل الدقيق حقيقة، ولو نوى أكل الدقيق بعينه لم يحنث بأكل الخبز ; لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه. (مستخلص، عيني)
And regarding 'this flour', he breaks his oath by eating its bread, not by eating it raw. And bread is what is customary in his land. And roast and cooked meat pertains to meat, and the head is what is sold in his city. [Some] rare [exceptions] unless it has been abandoned, as in the case of the date palm. And upon this principle many issues are built. Similarly, if he eats from his gruel (sawiq), he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He breaks his oath by eating bread, not by gruel, and that is the saying of the three. Muhammad said: He breaks his oath by gruel as well. And if he chews wheat, he breaks his oath by consensus. The difference is when he has no intention. As for when he intends, it is as he intends by consensus, because he intended the literal meaning of his speech or its probable meaning, which is metaphorical. (Ayni, Fath) He breaks his oath by eating its bread, so the oath is directed to what is made from it. (Ayni) That is, if he swore not to eat from this flour, he breaks his oath by eating its bread, because the substance of flour is not usually eaten. And his saying: 'not by eating it raw' means he does not break his oath by eating flour dry, by placing it on the palm and swallowing it without chewing, and this is the correct [view]. According to Al-Shafi'i, he breaks his oath by eating it raw as well, because he actually ate flour. And if he intended to eat the flour itself, he does not break his oath by eating bread, because he intended the literal meaning of his speech. (Mustakhlas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on an oath regarding flour. The main point is that the oath is understood according to customary consumption. Thus, if one swears not to eat 'this flour', he is bound by eating bread made from that flour, because flour itself is not typically eaten directly. The exception is if he intends the literal flour, then his intention is followed. The passage also discusses the dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that eating gruel (sawiq) does not break the oath, while Abu Yusuf holds it does for bread but not for sawiq, and Muhammad holds it breaks for sawiq as well. Chewing wheat grains breaks the oath by consensus. The ruling is based on the principle that the oath is interpreted according to what is customary, and if the oath-taker had a specific intention, that intention is followed.

Key Terms
حِنْث (hanth): Violation of an oath, requiring expiation (kaffarah).
سَفّ (suff): Eating dry powder by placing it on the palm and swallowing without chewing.
عُرْف (urf): Customary practice that governs the interpretation of oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if someone swears not to eat 'this flour' and then eats flour by placing it on his palm and swallowing it dry, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he consumed flour.
B) No, because this is not the customary way to eat flour.
C) Yes, according to Imam Muhammad.
D) No, but only if he intended not to eat it dry.

Key Takeaway
An oath is interpreted by custom; eating flour raw does not break the oath because flour is not customarily consumed raw.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Gruel and the Role of Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sawiq and the role of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding eating gruel (sawiq) after an oath about flour, and explain how intention affects the ruling.

Core Idea
Eating gruel from flour triggers a three-way split: no hanth (Abu Hanifa), hanth by bread only (Abu Yusuf), hanth by gruel too (Muhammad).

The same oath about flour can lead to different rulings depending on whether the consumption is gruel (sawiq) and what the oath-taker intended. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute among the three imams and the consensus on chewing wheat, as well as the decisive role of explicit intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on sawiq
Recite the positions: Abu Hanifa – no hanth by sawiq; Abu Yusuf – hanth by bread, not sawiq; Muhammad – hanth by sawiq as well. Note that Abu Yusuf and the 'three' (presumably the other teachers) agree on bread ruling.
The text says 'وبه قالت الثلاثة' referring to Abu Yusuf's view being held by three.
2
Distinguish the consensus on chewing wheat
Chewing whole wheat grains breaks the oath by consensus. Contrast with raw flour (suff) and sawiq: wheat grain is a different substance.
Qard is chewing; it is considered eating.
3
Analyze the role of intention
If the oath-taker had no specific intention, the default is custom. If he intended a specific meaning (e.g., literal flour), then his intention is followed by consensus.
Intention overrides custom because it clarifies the true meaning of the oath.
4
Determine the mufta bihi for sawiq
The text does not specify a relied-upon view for sawiq. Note that some later scholars prefer Abu Hanifa's view, but it remains ambiguous.
Refer to later Hanafi works for tarjih.

Bread Custom in Oaths
Ruling that bread is defined by local custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that bread in an oath is defined by local custom and apply it to determine whether the oath is broken.

Core Idea
Bread in an oath means only what is customary in the swearer's land.

Imagine a man swears not to eat bread, then eats a type of bread not common in his region—did he break his oath? The answer depends on custom. This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa’iq clarifies that Hanafi fiqh interprets such oaths according to the customary bread of the swearer's locality, not all possible breads.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is an oath not to eat bread. The ruling concerns what constitutes 'bread' for the oath's fulfilment.
The oath is interpreted strictly; the swearer is only bound by the conventional meaning.
2
Locate the custom-based rule
The matn states 'والخبز ما اعتاده بلده'—bread is what is customary in his land. This is the Hanafi position.
Custom ('urf) operates as a specifying factor for ambiguous terms in oaths.
3
Apply the rule to examples
If a man from a wheat-bread region eats qataif bread or rice bread not customary there, he does not break his oath—unless he intended that specific type.
The exception for intention shows that the oath covers all possible meanings if the swearer had a specific intent.
4
Contrast with other schools
Shafi'i and Maliki hold that any bread–wheat, barley, rice, etc.–breaks the oath because the term 'bread' is generic.
The difference stems from the weight given to custom versus literal meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخبز ما اعتاده بلده [ أي أهل بلد الحالف حتى لو أكل الحالف خلاف ما عندهم من الخبز لا يحنث. (عيني)] حلف لا يأكل خبزاً فيمينه ينصرف على خبز يعتاده أهل بلده كخبز الحنطة والشعير في عامة الديار؛ لأنه المتبادر الغالب المعتاد، فلو أكل خبز القطائف أي بالفارسية 'نان' لو 'زينة' لا يحنث؛ لأنه ليس بخبز مطلقاً . إلا إذا نواه؛ لأنه محتمل كلامه ولا يحنث بخبز الأرز في بلد لا يعتادونه ولو كان طعامهم أو أكثر طعامهم خبز الأرز يحنث به، وعند الشافعي ومالك يحنث بأي خبز كان. (مستخلص، عيني)
And bread is what is customary in his land [i.e., the people of the swearer's land, so if he eats bread contrary to their custom, he does not break his oath. (Ayni)] If he swears not to eat bread, his oath is directed to the bread that is customary among the people of his land, like wheat and barley bread in most lands, because that is the predominant and customary [meaning]. So if he eats qataif bread, i.e., in Persian 'nan' or 'zina', he does not break his oath, because it is not [considered] bread at all, unless he intended it, because it is a possible meaning of his words. And he does not break his oath by eating rice bread in a land where it is not customary, but if their food or most of their food is rice bread, he breaks his oath by it. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, he breaks his oath by any bread. (Mustakhlas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on what constitutes 'bread' in an oath. The Hanafi position is that the oath is interpreted according to the custom of the swearer's locality. Thus, only the bread commonly consumed there is included. If the swearer eats a type of bread not customary in his land, he does not break his oath, unless he specifically intended that type. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that any bread breaks the oath, regardless of custom.

Key Terms
الخبز (al-khubz): Bread; in oaths, refers to the type customarily eaten in the swearer's land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man in a region where only wheat bread is eaten swears not to eat bread and then eats rice bread, does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because rice bread is bread.
B) No, because rice bread is not customary in his land.
C) Yes, as per Shafi'i school.
D) No, unless he intended rice bread.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, terms in oaths are interpreted according to local custom, so 'bread' means only the bread commonly eaten in the swearer's land.

Roast and Cooked Meat in Oaths — The Role of Custom
Ruling on the scope of 'roast' and 'cooked' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths involving 'roast' and 'cooked food' and explain how custom and analogy determine the scope.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, oaths about 'roast' and 'cooked food' are interpreted by custom—only meat dishes count.

When a person swears not to eat 'roast' or 'cooked food', does that include vegetables? The Hanafi school answers by turning to local custom. This ruling shows how custom shapes legal interpretation and how different schools arrive at different conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and terms
The oath is about 'shiwa' (roast) and 'tabikh' (cooked food). What do these words refer to in legal context? Without intention, custom governs.
2
Determine the default interpretation
In Hanafi fiqh, if no intention is specified, the customary meaning (urf) is used. In classical custom, these terms referred specifically to meat dishes.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Compare with other schools
Shafi'i and Maliki hold that the words apply to any roasted or cooked item, not limited to meat. Note the basis: literal meaning vs. custom.
4
Consider the effect of changing custom
The text notes that in later custom, 'tabikh' may include all water-cooked dishes, so by analogy (qiyas) the oath would be broken by any cooked food.
This shows custom is not static.
5
Apply the rulings
Distinguish cases like dry fried food (no break) vs. bread with soup containing meat (break). Apply the principle to new scenarios.
Focus on the examples given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشواء والطبيخ على اللحم أي لو حلف لا يأكل الشواء أو الطبيخ ولا نية له تقع يمينه على اللحم المشوي وعلى المطبوخ من اللحم دون الباذنجان والجزر المشويين ونحوهما، وهذا في عرفهم؛ لأن الطبيخ عندهم ما طبخ بلحم أو شحم لا ما طبخ بسمن أو زيت، وأما في عرفنا فاسم الطبيخ يقع على كل مطبوخ بالماء، فالقياس فيه أن يحنث فيما هو مطبوخ وعند الشافعي ومالك: يقع على كل شواء بلا نية وإذا أكل المقلية اليابسة لا يحنث؛ لأنه لا . طبيخاً، وإن أكل الخبز بالمرقة يحنث؛ لأنه يسمى طبيخاً وفيها أجزاء اللحم أيضاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٦/١] (فتح)
And roast and cooked meat: meaning if he swears not to eat roast or cooked food and has no intention, his oath falls on roasted meat and cooked meat, not on roasted eggplant, carrots, and the like. This is according to their custom, because 'cooked' for them is what is cooked with meat or fat, not what is cooked with ghee or oil. As for our custom, the name 'cooked' applies to everything cooked with water, so by analogy he would break his oath by what is cooked. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, it applies to every roast without intention. And if he eats dry fried food, he does not break his oath because it is not called cooked. And if he eats bread with soup, he breaks his oath because it is called cooked and it contains parts of meat as well. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/356] (Fath)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on oaths concerning 'roast' (shiwa') and 'cooked food' (tabikh). According to Hanafis, these terms are interpreted by custom. In the classical custom, they referred specifically to meat dishes. Thus, an oath not to eat roast or cooked food applies to meat, not to vegetables like eggplant or carrots. However, in later custom, 'cooked food' may include all water-cooked dishes, so by analogy, one would break the oath by any cooked item. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that it applies to any roast, regardless. The text also notes that bread with soup qualifies as 'cooked' if it contains meat.

Key Terms
الشواء (al-shiwa'): Roast; in oaths, refers to roasted meat by custom.
الطبيخ (al-tabikh): Cooked food; in oaths, refers to meat cooked with meat or fat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man swears not to eat 'cooked food' and then eats roasted eggplant, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because it is cooked.
B) No, because in their custom 'cooked food' refers to meat dishes.
C) Yes, according to Shafi'i.
D) No, but only if he intended meat.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths about common foods are interpreted by prevailing custom, not literal definitions.
