Oath About Eating Head: Local Custom
Ruling that 'head' in oaths refers to heads sold in the swearer's city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the subject of an oath is defined by local custom, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and his students regarding which animal heads are included.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'head' means whatever heads are customarily sold and eaten in the swearer's city.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common scenario: a person swears not to eat 'head' (ras). Does that include camel head? Cow? Sheep? The answer depends on local custom. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement based on different customs in Kufa and Baghdad. We will trace this reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
What does 'head' (ras) mean when used in an oath? Is it all animal heads or only those commonly eaten?
The oath is 'I will not eat head' without specifying an animal.
2
State the Hanafi principle
Interpret oaths according to custom (urf). The meaning is determined by what is commonly sold and consumed in the swearer's locality.
This is derived from the maxim: 'al-urf al-khas' (specific custom).
3
Trace Abu Hanifa's evolving view
Initially, Abu Hanifa included camel, cow, and sheep based on Kufan custom. When camel heads fell out of custom, he restricted to cow and sheep.
This shows that custom changes over time, and rulings adapt.
4
Identify the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, based on Baghdad custom, only included sheep head. They argued that only sheep heads were commonly sold there.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf due to differing local customs.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'wa alayhi al-fatwa' for sheep head. It is the relied-upon position. For cow and camel, it depends on whether they are customarily sold in the swearer's city.
The fatwa is not on camel head unless custom includes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرأس ما يباع في مصره أي لو حلف أن لا يأكل رأساً فيمينه على رؤوس تكبس في التنانير وتباع في مصره؛ لأنا نعلم أنه لا يريد به رأس كل شيء، فإن رأس الجراد والعصفور لا يدخل تحته، ويدخل رأس الغنم وعليه الفتوى، وكان أبو حنيفة يقول :أولاً : يدخل فيه رأس الإبل والبقر والغنم مما رأى من عادة أهل الكوفة، ثم لما تركوها هذه العادة في الإبل ،رجع، فقال: يحنث في رأس البقر والغنم خاصة، وقالا: لا يحنث إلا في رأس الغنم خاصة لما شاهدا عادة أهل بغداد فعلم أن هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان لا اختلاف حجة وبرهان، وعند الشافعي: يحنث برأس الإبل أيضاً، وعند مالك: يحنث بكل رأس. (عيني، مسكين)
And the head is what is sold in his city. That is, if he swears not to eat a head, his oath applies to heads that are baked in ovens and sold in his city, because we know that he does not mean the head of everything. Thus, the head of a locust or sparrow is not included, but the head of a sheep is included, and the fatwa is upon that. Abu Hanifa initially said: It includes the head of camel, cow, and sheep, based on the custom of the people of Kufa. Then, when they abandoned this custom regarding camels, he retracted and said: He breaks his oath with the head of cow and sheep specifically. The two [students] said: He only breaks his oath with the head of sheep specifically, based on the custom of the people of Baghdad. Thus it is known that this is a difference of time and era, not a difference of evidence and proof. According to al-Shafi'i, he breaks his oath with the head of camel as well. According to Malik, he breaks his oath with every head. (Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on oaths concerning 'head' (ras). The Hanafi position is that the oath applies to animal heads customarily sold and eaten in the swearer's city. Initially, Abu Hanifa included camel, cow, and sheep in Kufa. Later, when camel heads fell out of custom, he restricted to cow and sheep. His students, based on Baghdad custom, restricted it to sheep only. This reflects a difference of custom, not a fundamental legal disagreement. The fatwa is on the inclusion of sheep head. Shafi'i includes camel head; Maliki includes all heads.

Key Terms
الرأس (al-ras): Animal head sold as food; in oaths, refers to heads customarily sold in the swearer's market.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa, if a man swears not to eat 'head' and eats a sheep's head, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because sheep head is customary.
B) No, because only cow and camel are included.
C) Yes, but only if it is sold in his city.
D) No, because the two students exclude sheep.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'head' is defined by local custom; the fatwa includes sheep head, while camel and cow heads depend on custom.

The Definition of Fruit in Hanafi Oaths
Ruling on fruit classification in oaths with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi classification of fruit in oaths and explain why grapes and pomegranates are excluded according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Fruit in oaths is defined by enjoyment, not nourishment — grapes and pomegranates nourish, so they are not fruit.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the term 'fruit' (al-fakiha) in an oath is not based on common botanical classification but on customary usage and the purpose of enjoyment. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding items like grapes and pomegranates. Understanding this classification is crucial for determining whether an oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage rules on what counts as 'fruit' in an oath. First, identify the disputed items: grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates.
These are specifically mentioned as excluded by the matn.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, these items are not fruit because they are primarily used for nourishment or medicine, not purely for enjoyment (tafakkuh).
The key criterion is tafakkuh – enjoyment after a meal.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates are fruit because the element of enjoyment is present in them.
They argue that people do eat these for pleasure as well.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: if someone swears not to eat fruit and eats a grape, does he break his oath? According to Abu Hanifa, no; according to the Sahibayn, yes.
This is the real-world application of the dispute.
5
Note the role of intention
The passage clarifies that if the swearer had a specific intention (niyyah), that intention governs. The dispute only applies when no intention exists.
Intention overrides default classifications.

(فتح) والبطيخ بكسر الباء الموحدة وتشديد الطاء المهملة سواء كان أخضر أو أصفر. والمشمش بكسر الميمين يعني زرد آلو و الخوخ والإجاص والتين. (ط) زمان لا لا العنب والرمان والرطب إلخ أي من حلف لا يأكل فاكهة فأكل عنباً أو رماناً لا يحنث عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يحنث في العنب والرمان والرطب؛ لأن معنی التفكه فيها موجود و به قالت الثلاثة؛ ولأن معنى التفكه فيها قاصر؛ لأنها صالحة للتغذي والتداوي، فلا يدخل في اسم الفاكهة المطلقة، وقيل: هذا اختلاف برهان، والخلاف أيضاً فيما إذا لم يكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى فعلى ما نوى بالإجماع، وقوله: 'والقثاء والخيار' أي ليسا من الفاكهة؛ لأنهما من البقول تبعاً؛ لأنهما يوضعان على الموائد مع البقول، فلا يحنث بأكلهما. رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٧/١] (مستخلص)
And fruit: apple, watermelon, apricot; not grapes, pomegranate, fresh dates, cucumber, and ghidha' (vegetable). That is, what is enjoyed after a meal – they are not fruit according to Abu Hanifa. And condiment (idam) is what is used as a dip like vinegar, salt, oil; not meat and eggs. [Unclear words] ... The word 'fruit' is a name for what is enjoyed after a meal or before, i.e., for pleasure. This meaning is established for apples and other fruits except watermelon, concerning which they differed. From Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi: it is not fruit because if its dry form is not fruit, its fresh form is not fruit. In al-Muhit: the dry fruits of trees are fruit, except watermelon, as it is not customary to consider its dry form fruit in most lands. From al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, there are two opinions. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/15) Apple: with damma on ta', shadda on fa', and important ha' in Persian 'sib'. (Fath) Watermelon: with kasra on the first letter and shadda on the important ha', whether green or yellow. Apricot: with kasra on both mims, meaning zard alu, peach, plum, fig. (T) ... [Fragment] ... Grapes, pomegranate, fresh dates, etc.: That is, if he swears not to eat fruit and eats grapes or pomegranates, he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa. The two [students] said: He breaks his oath with grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates, because the meaning of enjoyment is found in them, and that is the saying of the three. And because the meaning of enjoyment in them is limited, as they are suitable for nourishment and medicine, so they do not fall under the absolute name of fruit. It is said: This is a difference of proof. The difference applies when he has no intention; if he intends, it is as he intends by consensus. And his saying: 'cucumber and ghidha'': they are not fruit, because they are vegetables by subsequence, as they are placed on tables with vegetables. So he does not break his oath by eating them. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/357) (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on what counts as 'fruit' in an oath. The Hanafi position, per Abu Hanifa, is that fruit includes apples, watermelon, apricots, etc., but excludes grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and vegetables. The basis is that these items are used for nourishment or medicine rather than purely for enjoyment. The two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) include grapes and pomegranates, considering them enjoyable. This difference is about the definition of fruit. Custom plays a role; for example, watermelon is considered fruit by some but not others. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools have different views. The ruling is only relevant when the swearer has no specific intention.

Key Terms
الفاكهة (al-fakiha): Fruit; in Hanafi law, defined by custom and language as items eaten for enjoyment after a meal, excluding those primarily nourishing or medicinal.
التفكه (al-tafakkuh): Enjoying fruit; the legal criterion for classifying something as fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Apple
B) Watermelon
C) Grape
D) Apricot

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of fruit in oaths depends on whether the item is enjoyed purely for pleasure or also serves as nourishment; Abu Hanifa's position excludes grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates.

Condiment in Oaths: Definition and Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on idam definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of idam in oaths and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding solids like meat, eggs, and cheese.

Core Idea
Condiment (idam) in Hanafi oaths is only liquid that is eaten subsidiary to bread—solids that can be eaten alone are excluded.

This passage defines what constitutes 'idam' (condiment) in the context of oaths. The majority of Hanafi scholars restrict it to liquids that are not eaten alone, while Imam Muhammad includes any food customarily eaten with bread. Understanding this definition is crucial for determining whether an oath is broken. The passage also addresses the hadith about meat in Paradise and the dispute over cheese.

Guided Path
1
Define Idam from the Matn
Identify the explicit definition: 'والإدام ما يصطبغ به' (idam is what is used as a dip). Note the two conditions of subordination: not eaten alone, and mixed with bread.
The matn is the base text; commentary expands.
2
Apply to Liquids and Solids
Vinegar, oil, and salt satisfy both conditions. Meat and eggs do not because they can be eaten alone. Check each against the conditions.
Salt dissolves in the mouth, achieving mixing.
3
Identify Muhammad's View
Imam Muhammad defines idam as anything commonly eaten with bread, regardless of being liquid. He includes meat, eggs, and cheese based on custom and the hadith about meat in Paradise.
The text calls Muhammad's view 'the saying of the three' (other schools).
4
Analyze the Hanafi Rebuttal
The majority responds that the hadith refers to Paradise, not this world, and being called 'best of idam' does not make it idam—it is metaphorical, like calling the caliph master of non-Arabs.
This is a linguistic argument about reality vs. metaphor.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
If the swearer has no specific intention, the majority says eating meat does not break the oath (no hanth), while Muhammad says it does. If the swearer had a specific intention, that intention is followed by consensus.
The dispute only applies when no intention is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإدام ما يصطبغ به [ أي يختلط به الخبز وهو من الصنع (عيني)] أي لو حلف لا يأتدم ولا نية له يقع على مائع يختلط الخبز، فلا يحنث إلا بالمائع كالخل؛ لأن الإدام ما يؤكل تبعاً للخبز، وحقيقة التبعية تكون بشيئين: أحدهما: عدم الأكل وحده، وثانيهما: الاختلاط، فالخل والزيت بهذه الصفة؛ لأنهما لا يؤكلان وحدهما، ويكون به الخبز وهو الاختلاط، والملح أيضاً كذلك؛ لأنه لا يؤكل وحده عادةً ويذوب في الفم، فيحصل الاختلاط، وقوله: 'لا اللحم والبيض' فإنهما ليسا بإدام عند الطرفين؛ لأنهما يوكلان وحدهما، فلم يوجد معنى التبعية فيهما، وعند محمد: ما يؤكل مع الخبز غالباً فهو إدام، وقد يؤتدم بهما عرفاً فهما إدام، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وأخذ الشيخ أبو الليث به، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف؛ لما ورد في الحديث: 'سيد إدام أهل الجنة اللحم'. قلنا: هو في الجنة، وكلامنا في الدنيا، ولا يلزم من كونه سيد الإدام أن يكون من الإدام كما يقال: الخليفة سيد العرب والعجم وإن لم يكن هو من العجم، وقوله : 'والجبن فيه ثلاث لغات أجودها: بضم الجيم وسكون الباء وتخفيف النون، والثانية: بضمتين والتخفيف، والثالثة: بضمتين وتشديد النون، وفيه خلاف محمد أيضاً، وهذا الخلاف في هذه الأشياء الثلاثة إذا لم ينو الحالف، وإن نوى فعلى ما نوى إجماعاً. (فتح، عيني) والجبن بالجيم والموحدة المضمومتين وتشديد النون بالفارسية: 'منير'. (عيني)
And condiment (idam) is what is used as a dip [i.e., it is mixed with bread, and it is from preparation (Ayni)]. That is, if he swears not to eat condiment and has no intention, it applies to a liquid that mixes with bread. So he only breaks his oath with a liquid like vinegar, because idam is something eaten subsidiary to bread. The reality of subordination consists of two things: first, not being eaten alone, and second, mixing. Vinegar and oil have this quality because they are not eaten alone, and bread is eaten with them, achieving mixing. Salt is also like that because it is not usually eaten alone and it dissolves in the mouth, achieving mixing. And his saying: 'not meat and eggs' - they are not idam according to the two parties (the first two), because they are eaten alone, so the meaning of subordination is not found in them. According to Muhammad, what is usually eaten with bread is idam, and they are customarily used as a dip, so they are idam. And that is the saying of the three (the other three imams). Sheikh Abu al-Layth adopted this, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf, based on the hadith: 'The best condiment of the people of Paradise is meat.' We said: That is in Paradise, and our discussion is about this world. And it does not follow from its being the best of condiments that it is itself a condiment, just as it is said: The caliph is the master of Arabs and non-Arabs, even though he is not from the non-Arabs. And his saying: 'cheese' – there are three pronunciations: the best is with damma on jim, sukun on ba, and tanwin on nun; the second: with two damma and tanwin; the third: with two damma and shadda on nun. There is also a difference from Muhammad about it. This difference regarding these three things (meat, eggs, cheese) applies when the swearer has no intention; if he intends, it is as he intends by consensus. (Fath, Ayni) And cheese: with jim and damma on both letters and shadda on nun, in Persian: 'panir'. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on what constitutes 'condiment' (idam) in oaths. According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i?), condiment is a liquid that is eaten subsidiary to bread, such as vinegar, oil, and salt. Meat and eggs are not condiments because they can be eaten alone. However, Imam Muhammad considers anything commonly eaten with bread as condiment, including meat and eggs, based on custom and a hadith about meat in Paradise. This difference is resolved by noting that the hadith refers to the afterlife. The same dispute applies to cheese. When the swearer has no intention, the ruling depends on the school; if he intends a specific meaning, that intention is followed by consensus.

Key Terms
الإدام (al-idam): Condiment; a liquid substance eaten subsidiary to bread, like vinegar, oil, salt.
صِبَاغ (sibagh): Dip; something used to flavor bread by mixing.
تبعية (taba'iyya): Subordination; the quality of not being eaten alone but only as an accompaniment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, if a man swears not to eat 'idam' and then eats a piece of cheese, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because cheese is commonly eaten with bread.
B) No, because cheese is not a liquid and can be eaten alone.
C) Yes, but only if he intended to include cheese.
D) No, because cheese is not a condiment in any school.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, idam is defined as a liquid that is not eaten alone; solids like meat, eggs, and cheese are not idam according to the majority, though Imam Muhammad disagrees based on custom and a hadith.

Rulings on Oaths Regarding Meal Times
Defines meal times and conditions for oath violation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of ghada', 'asha', and sahur meal times and apply the conditions (time, amount, type) to determine if an oath is violated.

Core Idea
A meal oath is broken only when eating occurs within the fixed time, exceeds half-satiety, and matches local custom.

Oaths regarding meals are common in daily life. The Hanafi school provides precise definitions for meal times and the threshold for violation, based on custom and a hadith. This lesson unpacks the rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, showing how time, amount, and food type interact to determine whether an oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the meal times and the ruling on violation
The text defines three meals: ghada' (dawn to noon), 'asha' (noon to midnight), and sahur (midnight to dawn). If a person swears not to eat a particular meal and eats during its defined window, he violates the oath.
Times are based on the sun's position and custom.
2
Understand the amount threshold (more than half satiety)
One or two mouthfuls do not constitute a meal; the amount must be more than half of a normal person's satiety (nisf al-shabi'). This prevents trivial violations.
The threshold is based on customary understanding of a meal.
3
Understand the food type condition (custom)
What counts as a meal depends on local custom. Milk is a meal for a bedouin but not for a city dweller. Therefore, a city dweller who drinks only milk does not violate, but a bedouin does.
Custom (urf) plays a key role in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyze the hadith proof and linguistic distinction for sahur
The hadith that the Prophet prayed 'one of the two 'asha' prayers' at noon proves that 'asha' time begins at noon. For sahur, if pronounced with damm (suhur) it means the act of eating; with fatha (sahar) it means the food, requiring interpretation when used in an oath.
The linguistic distinction affects how the oath is interpreted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب. والغداء الأكل من الفجر إلى الظهر، والعشاء منه إلى نصف الليل، والسحور منه إلى طلوع الفجر. فإن حلف لا يتغدى فأكل في هذا الوقت حنث، وإن أكل قبله أو بعده لا يحنث. ومقدار ما يحنث به أن يكون أكثر نصف الشبع؛ لأن اللقمة واللقمتين لا تسمى غداء عادةً. وجنس المأكول يشترط أن يكون ما يأكله أهل بلده عادةً، حتى لو شرب اللبن وشبع لا يحنث إن كان حضرياً، وإن كان بدوياً يحنث. والعشاء منه أي من الظهر؛ لأن ما بعد الزوال عشاء، ولهذا الظهر إحدى صلاتي العشاء في الحديث الذي روي عن أبي هريرة أنه قال صلى بنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إحدى صلاتي العشاء، وفسرت بأنها الظهر. والسحور منه: فلو حلف لا يتسحر فأكل في هذا الوقت حنث. إن كان بضم السين فهو اسم الأكل في ذلك الوقت، فيصح حمل الأكل عليه بلا تأويل، وإن كان بفتح السين فهو اسم لما يؤكل فيه، فيحتاج لتأويل.
Chapter of oaths regarding eating and drinking. Al-ghada' is eating from dawn to noon, al-asha' from noon to midnight, and al-sahur from midnight to dawn. If he swears not to eat al-ghada' and eats during this time, he violates; if he eats before or after, he does not violate. The amount that causes violation is more than half satiety, because one or two bites are not normally called ghada'. The type of food must be what the people of his town normally eat, so if he drinks milk and becomes full, he does not violate if he is a city dweller, but if he is a desert dweller, he violates. Al-asha' is from noon, because what comes after noon is 'asha', and for this reason noon is one of the two 'asha' prayers in the hadith narrated from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah prayed with us one of the two 'asha' prayers, and it was interpreted as noon. Al-sahur is from it: so if he swears not to eat suhur and eats during this time, he violates. If (sahur) is with damm on the sin, it is the name of the eating at that time, so attributing eating to it is valid without interpretation. If it is with fatha on the sin, it is the name of what is eaten, so it requires interpretation.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof, defining the time periods for the three main meals (ghada', 'asha', suhur) and the conditions under which an oath not to eat a particular meal is violated. The text begins by fixing the times: ghada' from dawn to noon, 'asha' from noon to midnight, and suhur from midnight to dawn. If someone swears 'I will not eat ghada'' and eats between dawn and noon, he breaks his oath; eating before or after that window does not break it. The amount necessary to break the oath is more than half of a normal person's satiety, as one or two mouthfuls are not customarily called a meal. The type of food is also tied to local custom: what constitutes a meal for a city dweller may differ from a bedouin. For example, a city dweller who only drinks milk and becomes full does not break his oath, but a bedouin would, because milk is a meal for him. The text then provides a proof for the timing of 'asha' from a hadith: the Messenger of Allah is reported to have prayed one of the two 'asha' prayers at noon, indicating that the 'asha' meal time begins after noon. Finally, the passage distinguishes between the two pronunciations of sahur: with damm (suhur) it means the act of eating at that time, so the oath is about eating; with fatha (sahar) it means the food eaten, so the oath requires interpretation. All rulings are based on custom and the literal meaning of the oath.

Key Terms
الغداء (al-ghada'): The midday meal, from dawn to noon.
العشاء (al-asha'): The evening meal, from noon to midnight.
السحور (al-sahur (with fatha) or al-suhur (with damm)): The pre-dawn meal, from midnight to dawn. The pronunciation affects meaning: with damm it is the act of eating, with fatha it is the food itself.
نصف الشبع (nisf al-shabi'): Half of a normal person's satiety; the threshold for breaking an oath regarding meal type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'I will not eat 'asha'' and eats between noon and midnight, what is the ruling?
A) He does not violate because 'asha' is only from sunset.
B) He violates because the time for 'asha' is from noon to midnight according to the Hanafi school.
C) He only violates if he eats more than half satiety.
D) He violates only if he eats after sunset.

Key Takeaway
Oath violation for meals depends on precise timing (custom-defined), amount (more than half satiety), and type (customary food).

Specific Intention in a Generic Oath: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on specification in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on specifying intention in a generic oath and explain why the specification is rejected entirely according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
A verb’s implied object (iqtida') carries no generality, so a specific intention cannot restrict the oath.

When someone swears using a generic verb like 'if I eat' without an object, and later claims they meant only a specific food, does that claim matter? The Hanafi school splits on this: Abu Hanifa says the intention is meaningless both in court and in private, while Abu Yusuf accepts it in private. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and usul reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Articulate Abu Hanifa’s ruling: the specific intention is not accepted at all – neither qada'an (in court) nor diyanatan (in private). The oath is violated by any instance of the action.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf (along with al-Khassaf and al-Shafi'i) holds that the swearer is believed diyanatan – i.e., in private he is not considered a violator if he only does the intended specific act.
The difference hinges on how the verb is analyzed in usul al-fiqh.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that the verb in a conditional clause ('if I eat') implies its infinitive (masdar), which is an indefinite noun (nakira) in the position of the condition. In Hanafi usul, a nakira in a conditional is general ('amm) and can be specified by intention.
Thus, the intention restricts the oath to that specific object.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa counters that the masdar is not explicitly mentioned; it is understood by iqtida' (inherent implication). Iqtida' does not create generality – it merely indicates the essence (mahiyyah) of the action, not its particulars. Hence, there is no general meaning to be specified. Moreover, intention only operates on what is actually uttered, and the object is not uttered.
The key distinction: explicit mention vs. implied meaning.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, if someone swears 'if I wear, my slave is free' and intended a specific garment, under Abu Hanifa he violates by wearing anything; under Abu Yusuf, in private he is not a violator if he only wore the intended garment, but the court still considers him in violation for divorce/manumission cases.
The consequence differs only in private/religious duty (diyanatan), not in judicial enforcement (qada'an) when the oath involves legal consequences like divorce or manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن لبست: أي لو حلف وقال: إن لبست فعبدي حر أو أكلت أي أو قال: إن أكلت فعبدي حر. أو شربت: أي لو قال: إن شربت فعبدي حر، وكذا لو قال: إن نكحت أو اغتسلت فعبدي حر. معينا: بأن قال: نويت الحرير في الأول والخبز في أكلت والقصاع في شربت ونحوه. لم يصدق أصلاً يعني لا قضاء ولا ديانة فيحنث بأي شيء أكل أو شرب. أي من حلف إن لبست فعبدي حر، وكذا إن أكلت وشربت فعبدي حر، وقال: نويت شيئاً دون شيء لم يصدق في القضاء ولا في الديانة؛ لأن دلالة هذه الأفعال على هذه الأشياء بطريق الاقتضاء، والمقتضى لا عموم له، ولا يجوز أن ينوي دون ثوب أو أكلاً دون أكل؛ لأن المصدر الثابت في ضمن الفعل دل على الماهية دون الأفراد، فلا يكون عاما قابلا للتخصيص؛ ولأن النية إنما تعمل في الملفوظ ; لأنها لتعيين المحتمل، والطعام ونحوه غير مذكور، وعن أبي يوسف محله: أنه يصدق ديانة، وبه أخذ الخصاف والشافعي لأن ذكر الفعل ذكر للمصدر، وهو نكرة في موضع الشرط فيعم، فيقبل التخصيص، ولأنه مذكور تقديراً وإن لم يذكر صريحا إلا أنه خلاف الظاهر، فلا يصدق قضاء فيما إذا كانت يمينه بطلاق أو عتاق، ولا تعلق للقضاء في اليمين بالله تعالى.
If I wear: i.e., if he swore and said: If I wear, my slave is free, or if I eat: i.e., if he said: If I eat my slave is free. Or if I drink: i.e., if he said: If I drink my slave is free, and similarly if he said: If I marry or if I bathe my slave is free. Specified: by saying: I intended silk in the first, bread in 'I ate', and a cup in 'I drank', etc. He is not believed at all, meaning neither in court nor in private, so he breaks the oath by anything he eats or drinks. So whoever swears If I wear my slave is free, and similarly if I eat and drink my slave is free, and says: I intended something without something, he is not believed in court nor in private; because the indication of these verbs to these things is by way of implication (iqtida'), and what is implied has no generality, and it is not permissible for him to intend something other than a garment or eating other than eating; because the verbal noun established within the verb indicates the essence (mahiyyah) without the individuals, so it is not general (amm) capable of specification; and because intention only operates on what is uttered, because it is for determining the probable, and food etc. is not mentioned. And from Abu Yusuf (his position) is that he is believed in private, and al-Khassaf and al-Shafi'i took this; because mentioning the verb is mentioning the infinitive, and it is indefinite in the position of a condition, so it is general, so it accepts specification; and because it is mentioned by implication even if not expressed explicitly, but it is contrary to the apparent meaning, so he is not believed in court when his oath involves divorce or manumission, and the court has no involvement in oaths by Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of specifying an intention in a generic oath. The Hanafi position attributed to Abu Hanifa is that if someone swears using a verb like 'if I wear/eat/drink' without mentioning an object, and later claims he intended a specific thing (e.g., silk, bread, a cup), that claim is not accepted at all—neither in court (qada') nor in private (diyanatan). The reasoning is that the verb implies the object only by way of iqtida' (inherent implication), which does not create generality. The masdar (infinitive) within the verb indicates the essence of the action, which is a single concept without particulars. Therefore, the oath is violated by any instance of wearing, eating, or drinking. In contrast, Abu Yusuf (and also al-Khassaf and al-Shafi'i) hold that the swearer is believed in private (diyanatan) because the verb in the conditional context is a nakira (indefinite) and is general ('amm), thus can be specified by intention. However, since this is contrary to the apparent wording, the court does not accept it, especially when the consequence involves divorce or manumission (where qada' applies). For oaths by Allah, there is no judicial intervention anyway, so only private belief matters. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: the specification is not accepted at all, and the oath is violated broadly.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): Inherent implication: the verb implies its object by logical necessity without explicit mention.
المصدر الثابت في ضمن الفعل (al-masdar al-thabit fi dhil fi'l): The infinitive that is established within the verb, indicating the essence of the action.
النكرة في موضع الشرط (al-nakira fi mawdi' al-shart): The indefinite noun in the conditional clause, which according to some (Abu Yusuf) is general and accepts specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'If I drink, my slave is free' and later says he meant 'water only', what is the ruling?
A) He is believed in private, so his slave is not freed if he drinks only water.
B) He is not believed at all – his slave is freed if he drinks anything, including water.
C) He is believed in court, so his slave is free only if he drinks something other than water.
D) The oath is invalid because the intention contradicts the apparent meaning.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a specific intention cannot restrict a generic oath because the verb's implied object carries no generality – the oath is violated by any instance of the action.

Explicit Object in Oath: Believed in Private
Ruling on explicit object in oath with diyanatan acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the acceptance of intention when the object is explicitly mentioned in an oath, and explain the distinction between diyanatan and qada'an based on the usul principle of the indefinite noun in the condition.

Core Idea
When the object is mentioned in the oath, the swearer’s specification is accepted diyanatan but not qada’an because the indefinite in a condition is general.

In the previous lesson, we learned that when the swearer does not mention the object, his specification is not accepted at all. Now the text moves to a scenario where the object is explicitly mentioned in the oath using an indefinite noun. This changes the ruling: the specification is now accepted in private (diyanatan) but still not in court (qada'an). The reason lies in a fundamental usul principle: the indefinite noun in a conditional clause is treated as general ('amm), similar to the particle of negation. This generality can be specified by intention, but the judge follows the apparent general meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The swearer includes an indefinite object in the oath, e.g., 'If I wear a garment' or 'If I eat food'.
The object is a nakira (indefinite noun) in the conditional clause.
2
State the ruling
The swearer is believed in private (duyyina) when he claims to have intended a specific type of that object.
Diyanatan means between him and Allah; qada'an means in court.
3
Distinguish court vs. private
In court, the judge does not accept the specification because the wording is general and the judge rules by the apparent meaning.
Thus, the oath is considered violated in court if any instance of the general term occurs.
4
Analyze the linguistic principle
The indefinite noun in the condition is general ('amm) like the particle of negation. Therefore, it can be specified by intention in private, but the apparent general meaning remains for legal enforcement.
This is a key Hanafi usul rule: النكرة في الشرط تعم كالنفي.
5
Connect to previous case
Contrast with the case where no object is mentioned: there the specification is not accepted at all because there is no general term to specify.
The presence of an explicit indefinite noun makes specification possible in private.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زاد ثوباً أو طعاماً أو شراباً دُيّنَ أي قال الحالف: إن لبست ثوبا أو أكلت طعاماً أو شربت شراباً فكذا، ثم قال: نويت شيئاً دون شيء صدق ديانة؛ لأنه نكرة في الشرط، فتعم كالنفي أي كما تعم فيه، لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي بنية التخصيص ; لأن اللفظ عام، وإنما صحت نيته ديانة; لأن تخصيص مثل هذا العام يصح إلى أن يبقى واحد
And if he adds 'a garment' or 'food' or 'a drink', he is believed in private: i.e., the swearer says: If I wear a garment or if I eat food or if I drink a drink, then (his) is such, then he says: I intended something without something, he is believed in private; because it is indefinite in the condition, so it generalizes like the negative, i.e., just as it generalizes in it, but it is against the apparent meaning so the judge does not believe him regarding the specification; because the wording is general, and his intention is valid only in private; because the specification of such a general term is valid as long as it remains one (individual).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of oaths with specific intentions. It states that if the swearer explicitly mentions the object in his oath (e.g., 'if I wear a garment', 'if I eat food', 'if I drink a drink'), then his claim to have intended a specific item is accepted in private (diyanatan), but not in court (qada'an). The reason is that the indefinite noun in the conditional clause is general ('amm), similar to the particle of negation. Therefore, it can be specified by intention. However, because the literal wording is general, the qadi cannot accept the specification, as he judges based on apparent meanings. The court will rule that the oath is violated by any garment, food, or drink. In private, however, the person's intention is considered valid, so he is not considered a violator before Allah. This is a refinement of the previous ruling: when the object is not mentioned, the specification is not accepted at all; when it is mentioned, it is accepted in private. The difference is due to the presence of a general word that can be specified.

Key Terms
دُيّنَ (duyyina): He is believed in private (diyanatan), i.e., between him and Allah, not in court.
النكرة في الشرط (al-nakira fi al-shart): The indefinite noun in the conditional clause, which is considered general ('amm) and thus can be specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the swearer is trustworthy, so the judge believes him.
C) Because the object is mentioned, so it becomes specific.
D) Because the oath is invalid if the object is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between diyanatan and qada'an based on the principle that the indefinite noun in a condition is general, allowing specification by intention only in private.

Haqiqa vs Majaz in Oaths: The Case of Drinking from the Dajla
Intra-Hanafi dispute on linguistic interpretation in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether an oath not to drink from a river applies only to direct lapping or any method, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In oaths, the literal meaning is preferred by Abu Hanifa, but the common metaphorical usage prevails in the relied-upon view.

When someone swears not to drink from a river, does the oath cover only putting one's mouth directly to the water or any method including a cup? This lesson examines the difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions on the interpretation of 'drinking from' in oaths, a dispute rooted in the principles of haqiqa and majaz. The passage also shows how the ruling changes when the wording includes 'water of' the river.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
The question is whether the phrase 'drinking from Dajla' in an oath applies literally (direct lapping) or metaphorically (any method).
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: only direct lapping violates. The two companions (and three other schools): any method violates.
3
Understand the usul basis
Abu Hanifa prefers haqiqa (literal) over majaz; the companions prefer acting on the common majaz to give generality.
4
Analyze the exceptions
Note that if the oath is 'water of Dajla', there is consensus on violation by any method. Also, note the distinction between the river name and its water.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that of the two companions, supported by the other three schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يشرب من دجلة أي حلف لا يشرب من دجلة، فيمينه تنعقد على الكرع عند أبي حنيفة حتى لو شرب بإناء لا يحنث، وعندهما: من على الكرع والغرف والشرب بإناء، وهذا الخلاف مبني على الأصل الذي قدمنا أن الشيء إذا كان له حقيقة مستعملة ومجاز متعارف، فالحقيقة أولى عنده وقالا العمل بعموم المجاز أولى فالكرع أي تناول الماء بفيه من موضعه حقيقة مستعملة، فيحنث عند أبي حنيفة ولا يحنث بالغرف والشرب بإناء، وعندهما يحنث بالشرب بأي صفة كان للعمل بعموم المجاز المتعارف وبه قالت الثلاثة. بخلاف ماء دجلة: فإنه يحنث بالإجماع كيف ما شرب بإناء أو غيره; لأن من شرط شرب الماء منسوب إلى دجلة والغرف لا يقطع النسبة. ولو حلف لا يشرب من الفرات فعلى ما تقدم الخلاف. ولو حلف لا يشرب من ماء الفرات فيحنث اتفاقاً بالشرب كيف ما كان. ولو حلف لا يشرب من الفرات فشرب من نهر يأخذ من الفرات لا يحنث في يمينه، ويحنث لو قال لا يشرب من ماء الفرات. ولو حلف لا يشرب ماء فراتاً فهو على شرب ماء عذب من أي موضع كان.
He does not drink from Dajla: i.e., if he swore 'I will not drink from Dajla', his oath is confined to drinking by lapping (kar') according to Abu Hanifa, such that if he drinks with a vessel he does not violate. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the oath is on lapping, scooping, and drinking with a vessel. And this difference is based on the principle we mentioned earlier: that when a thing has a used literal meaning (haqiqa) and a common metaphorical meaning (majaz), the literal is preferred according to him (Abu Hanifa), and the two say acting on the general metaphorical meaning is better. So 'kar'' i.e., taking water with the mouth from its place is the used literal meaning, so he violates according to Abu Hanifa, and he does not violate by scooping and drinking with a vessel. According to the two, he violates by drinking in any manner, because of acting on the general metaphorical meaning, and the three (i.e., the other imams) also said this. Contrary to the water of Dajla: he violates by consensus, however he drinks, with a vessel or otherwise, because the condition of drinking water attributed to Dajla is that scooping does not cut off the attribution. And if he swore 'I will not drink from the Euphrates', the same difference of opinion applies. And if he swore 'I will not drink from the water of the Euphrates', he violates unanimously by drinking however it may be. And if he swore 'I will not drink from the Euphrates', and he drank from a canal that takes from the Euphrates, he does not violate his oath; but he would violate if he said 'I will not drink from the water of the Euphrates'. And if he swore 'I will not drink sweet water', it applies to drinking sweet water from any place.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (and also mentions consensus with other madhhabs) regarding the meaning of 'drinking from a river' in oaths. The issue: if someone swears 'I will not drink from the Dajla River', does he violate by drinking directly with his mouth (kar') or by any method including using a cup or scooping? Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is restricted to the literal meaning of 'drinking from the river', which is direct lapping (kar'). If he drinks using a vessel, he does not break the oath. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the oath is violated by any manner of drinking, because 'drinking from the river' is a common metaphorical expression that includes all methods. They rely on the principle that when a word has both a literal and a metaphorical usage, and the metaphor is commonly used, the metaphor should be given effect to act on the generality. Abu Hanifa prefers the literal meaning. The text notes that the other three imams (al-thalatha, meaning the other three Sunni madhhabs) agree with the two companions. Then the text distinguishes between saying 'from Dajla' (the river itself) and 'from the water of Dajla'. If the oath is 'from the water of Dajla', there is consensus that it is violated by any method, because the attribution to Dajla remains even if scooped. Similarly for the Euphrates: the same ikhtilaf applies if the oath is 'from the Euphrates', but if it is 'from the water of the Euphrates', it is violated unanimously. Also, if the oath is 'from the Euphrates' and he drinks from a tributary, he does not violate, but if he says 'from the water of the Euphrates' he does. Lastly, if someone swears not to drink 'sweet water' (mā'an furātan), it applies to any sweet water from any source. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position of the two companions (and the consensus of the three), because it is more precautionary and aligns with common usage.

Key Terms
الكرع (al-kar'): Drinking water directly from its source by putting the mouth in the water and lapping it up.
الحقيقة المستعملة (al-haqiqa al-musta'mala): The commonly used literal meaning of a word.
المجاز المتعارف (al-majaz al-muta'arif): The common metaphorical usage that is widely recognized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He does not violate because the oath only covers direct lapping.
B) He violates because the oath is violated by any method of drinking.
C) The oath is invalid because the method was not specified.
D) He only violates if he drinks more than half a cup.

Key Takeaway
When interpreting oaths, the Hanafi school applies usul principles of haqiqa and majaz, leading to an intra-school dispute; the relied-upon view favours the common metaphorical usage to cover all methods of drinking from a river.

Oath Not to Drink from a Specific Vessel: Four Scenarios
Analyzes four scenarios of an oath regarding a specific vessel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios of an oath not to drink from a specific vessel and apply the Hanafi rulings regarding violation, including the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Change of attribution or impossibility of fulfillment prevents violation of an oath.

The Hanafi school carefully analyzes oaths based on the possibility of fulfillment. This passage presents four scenarios where someone swears not to drink water from a specific vessel. The ruling depends on whether the water was present, whether the oath was time-bound, and whether the water was poured out. This lesson will walk through each scenario and the ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the four cases: (1) time-bound (today), no water; (2) time-bound, water poured; (3) unconditional, no water; (4) unconditional, water poured.
2
Apply the principle of tabaddul al-nisba
Understand that if water is poured into another vessel, the attribution to the original vessel changes, so drinking from the new vessel does not violate the oath.
This principle applies to cases where water was present and then poured.
3
Analyze possibility of fulfillment
For each case, determine whether drinking the water of the specific vessel was ever possible during the oath period.
Impossibility from the start voids the oath; later impossibility due to pouring does not cause violation if the water's attribution changed.
4
Identify the ikhtilaf
Note that in cases 1–3, the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold no violation, while Abu Yusuf holds violation. In case 4, consensus on violation.
The ikhtilaf centers on whether an oath with impossible fulfillment is void or still binding.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the majority) for cases 1–3.
This is the safer position, giving benefit of the doubt to the swearer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلف لا يشرب ماء هذا الكوز فصب ماءه في كوز آخر فشربه لم يحنث لتبدل النسبة. فههنا أربع مسائل: إحداها رجل قال: إن لم أشرب ماء هذا الكوز اليوم فعبدي حر، ولا ماء فيه، والثانية: أنه حلف كذلك، وفي الكوز ماء فصب قبل انقضاء اليوم وقبل أن يشربه، والثالثة: رجل حلف مطلقاً: إن لم أشرب ماء هذا الكوز فعبدي حر، ولا ماء فيه والرابعة أنه حلف كذلك، وفي الكوز ماء، فصب قبل أن يشربه ففيما قيد باليوم ولا ماء فيه لم يحنث، وكذا فيما قيد به وفيه ماء فصب وفيما أطلق ولا ماء فيه ففي هذه الصور الثلاث لم يحنث عند الطرفين، ويحنث عند أبي يوسف به، وفيما أطلق وفيه ماء فصبه حنث إجماعاً، =
And if he swore 'I will not drink the water of this vessel', and he poured its water into another vessel and drank it, he does not violate because the attribution has changed. So here there are four issues: First, a man said: 'If I do not drink the water of this vessel today, my slave is free', and there is no water in it. Second, he swore likewise, and there was water in the vessel, and he poured it out before the end of the day and before drinking it. Third, a man swore absolutely: 'If I do not drink the water of this vessel, my slave is free', and there is no water in it. Fourth, he swore likewise, and there was water in the vessel, and he poured it out before drinking it. Regarding the first case, restricted to the day and no water, he does not violate. Similarly in the second case, restricted and water poured out, and the third case, absolute and no water – in these three cases he does not violate according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?) but he does violate according to Abu Yusuf. And in the fourth case, absolute and water poured out, he violates by consensus. =
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on an oath not to drink the water of a specific vessel (kuz). It presents four scenarios. The first scenario: the oath is restricted to the day ('today'), and the vessel never had water. The second: restricted to the day, water was present but poured out before the oath was fulfilled. The third: unconditional oath (no time limit), no water present. The fourth: unconditional, water present but poured out. The text states that in the first three cases, according to the two (presumably Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, though it says 'al-tarafayn' which means 'the two sides' or 'both parties'?), the swearer does not violate. According to Abu Yusuf, he does violate. In the fourth case, there is consensus that he violates. The passage is incomplete, but it introduces the principles of impossibility of fulfillment (due to absence of water) and change in the identity of the water (pouring out). This topic requires careful analysis of whether the oath can be fulfilled or not. The text ends abruptly, so the full reasoning is not available.

Key Terms
تبدل النسبة (tabaddul al-nisba): Change of attribution: when the water is transferred to another vessel, it is no longer attributed to the original vessel.
حنث (hanath): Violation of an oath, requiring kaffarah (expiation).
يمين (yamin): Oath (by Allah or conditional).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Time-bound oath, water present but poured before drinking.

Key Takeaway
An oath concerning a specific object is not violated if the object's attribution changes or if fulfillment was impossible from the start, according to the majority of Hanafi imams.

Intention in Oaths with Indefinite Nouns: Diyanatan vs Qada'an
Ruling on the validity of intention in oaths for indefinite objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the specification of an indefinite noun in an oath and distinguish between diyanatan and qada'an acceptance.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in a condition is general; specific intention is valid before Allah but not enforceable in court.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common scenario: a person swears using an indefinite noun like 'a garment', then later claims he intended a specific garment. The Hanafi school distinguishes between what is accepted religiously (diyanatan) and what is accepted judicially (qada'an). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this distinction and the principle that the indefinite in a condition is treated as general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the indefinite noun
The swearer uses a nakirah (indefinite noun) like 'garment' in the conditional phrase. This triggers the ruling.
The indefinite noun is the key element.
2
Understand the apparent generality
In Hanafi usul, a nakirah in a conditional context gives generality, similar to a negation. Thus, the oath covers all instances of the indefinite noun.
This is a core Hanafi principle.
3
Apply the specification claim
If the swearer later claims he intended a specific instance (e.g., a particular garment), this is a claim of specification (takhsis) of the general.
Specification is allowed as long as it leaves at least one remaining instance.
4
Distinguish diyanatan from qada'an
The specification is accepted diyanatan (before Allah) because it is possible, but the judge (qada'an) does not accept it because the apparent wording is general. The judge goes by the literal meaning.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زاد ثوباً أو طعاماً أو شراباً دُيّنَ [يعني يصدق الحالف ديانة إذا قال: عنيت شيئاً دون شيء. (عيني)] أي قال الحالف: إن لبست ثوبا أو أكلت طعاماً أو شربت شراباً فكذا، ثم قال: نويت شيئاً دون شيء صدق ديانة؛ لأنه نكرة في الشرط، فتعم كالنفي أي كما تعم فيه، لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي بنية التخصيص ؛ لأن اللفظ عام، وإنما صحت نيته ديانة؛ لأن تخصيص مثل هذا العام يصح إلى أن يبقى واحد
And if he added a garment or food or drink, he is believed (religiously) [meaning the swearer is believed in religious matters when he says: I intended something specific. (Ayni)] i.e., if the swearer said: If I wear a garment or eat food or drink a drink, then such-and-such, then he said: I intended something specific, he is believed religiously because it is an indefinite noun in the condition, so it is general like the negation, just as it is general in it, but it is contrary to the apparent meaning, so the judge does not accept his specific intention because the wording is general. His intention is valid only religiously because the specification of such a general term is valid as long as it leaves at least one (instance).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage states a key Hanafi ruling on oaths involving indefinite nouns. When someone swears using an indefinite noun like 'a garment', the literal wording is general, encompassing all garments. If he later claims he intended a specific garment, he is believed in religious matters (with Allah) but not in a court of law. The reason is that an indefinite noun in a conditional context is treated as general, similar to a negation. The Hanafi school distinguishes between what is acceptable 'diyanatan' (religiously) and 'qada'an' (judicially). This ruling highlights the principle that the specification of a general term is valid as long as it leaves at least one instance (i.e., it does not completely empty the general term of meaning).

Key Terms
نكرة (nakirah): An indefinite noun in a condition, which is treated as general according to the Hanafi school.
ديانة (diyanatan): According to religious conscience, as opposed to judicial ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of an oath taker's intention when he swears using an indefinite noun like 'a garment'?
A) The judge accepts his specific intention.
B) It is valid only religiously (diyanatan) but not in court.
C) It is invalid entirely.
D) It depends on the value of the garment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an indefinite noun in an oath condition is treated as general; specific intention is accepted before Allah but not in court.

Oath on Drinking from a Jug: Four Scenarios and the Principle of Possibility
Four cases of oaths involving a jug of water with differing rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios for an oath on drinking from a jug, explain the principle of imkan al-birr, and determine in each case whether the oath is broken according to Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The possibility of fulfillment (imkan al-birr) is a condition for contracting an absolute oath and for continuing a time-bound oath.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq analyzes four scenarios involving an oath not to drink from a jug. The ruling hinges on whether the sworn act is possible at the time of the oath and remains possible. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require possibility, while Abu Yusuf does not. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying oath jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Label each scenario by time condition (absolute vs. time-bound) and presence of water initially. The four are: (1) time-bound with no water, (2) time-bound with water poured before day ends, (3) absolute with no water, (4) absolute with water poured later.
The fourth scenario is agreed upon by all.
2
Determine the ruling according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
In scenarios 1-3, the oath is not broken because fulfillment is impossible from the start or becomes impossible within the time frame. In scenario 4, it is broken because fulfillment was possible at the time of the oath and was later lost.
The two require imkan al-birr for the oath to be contracted or to continue.
3
Determine the ruling according to Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that in all four scenarios the oath is broken: in time-bound cases after the time expires, in absolute cases immediately, because he does not require imkan al-birr as a condition.
Abu Yusuf's view is considered weak (marjuh) in the school.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The principle: For an absolute oath (without time limit), imkan al-birr at the time of utterance is a condition for the oath to contract. For a time-bound oath, continued imkan al-birr is a condition for the oath to remain binding. Expiation is only a substitute when fulfillment is possible but not done.
This is a rational inference from the purpose of oaths: obligation of fulfillment, not expiation.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Given a new scenario (e.g., swearing to drink from a well that dries up), determine whether imkan al-birr existed initially and whether it continues. Use the rules from the four scenarios to derive the ruling.
Consistent application requires careful analysis of possibility at each stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلف لا يشرب ماء هذا الكوز فصب ماءه في كوز آخر فشربه لم يحنث لتبدل النسبة. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٨/١] مثلاً. (عيني) إن لم أشرب: أي لو قال رجل: إن لم أشرب إلخ. عيني فكذا : أي فامرأتي طالق أو عبدي حر ولا ماء فيه: أي والحال أن لا ماء في الكوز. (عيني) لا يحنث في هذه الصور كلها لعدم إمكان البر. (ط، عيني) فههنا أربع مسائل: إحداها رجل قال: إن لم أشرب ماء هذا الكوز اليوم فعبدي حر، ولا ماء فيه، والثانية: أنه حلف كذلك، وفي الكوز ماء فصب قبل انقضاء اليوم وقبل أن يشربه، والثالثة: رجل حلف مطلقاً: "إن لم أشرب ماء هذا الكوز فعبدي حر"، ولا ماء فيه، والرابعة: أنه حلف كذلك، وفي الكوز ماء، فصب قبل أن يشربه ففيما قيد باليوم ولا ماء فيه لم يحنث، وكذا فيما قيد به وفيه ماء فصب وفيما أطلق ولا ماء فيه ففي هذه الصور الثلاث لم يحنث عند الطرفين، ويحنث عند أبي يوسف به، وفيما أطلق وفيه ماء فصبه حنث إجماعاً، = وإن كان فصب حنث، والأصل في هذا وأمثاله أن كون البر متصوراً شرط في انعقاد اليمين المطلقة عن الوقت، وفي بقاء المقيدة بالوقت عند الطرفين؛ لأن حكم اليمين وجوب البر، فأما الكفارة فهي خلف عنه عند عجزه، فإذا لم يمكن البر؛ لعدم تصوره لا تجب الكفارة، وعند أبي يوسف كونه متصوراً ليس بشرط؛ لأن اليمين قد ينعقد على ما لا يتصور عادة كقوله: "والله لأمن السماء أو لأحولن هذا الحجر ذهباً" ينعقد ويحنث في الحال لعدم إمكان البر عادة، ثم فيما إذا لم يكن في الكوز ماء فالبر غير ممكن، فلا يحنث عندهما خلافا له سواء ذكر اليوم أو لا، وإن كان فيه ماء، فإن ذكر اليوم فالبر إنما يجب في الجزء الآخر من اليوم، فإذا صب لم يكن البر متصوراً، فلا يحنث عندهما خلافاً له وإن لم يذكر اليوم، فالبر إنما يجب عليه إذا فرغ من التكلم لكن موسعاً بشرط أن لا يفوت في مدة عمره، والبر متصور عند الفراغ من التكلم فانعقد اليمين، فإذا صب فقد فات البر فيحنث إجماعاً، والحاصل أن عند أبي يوسف: يحنث في الكل، لكن في المؤقت بعد الوقت، وفي غير المؤقت في الحال.
And if he swore not to drink the water of this jug, then he poured its water into another jug and drank it, he does not break the oath due to the change of attribution. [Reference: Rami al-Haqa'iq 1/358] For example. (Ayni) If I do not drink: i.e., if a man said: If I do not drink, etc. Ayni, then such-and-such: i.e., his wife is divorced or his slave is free, and there is no water in it: i.e., the condition that there is no water in the jug. (Ayni) He does not break the oath in all these cases due to the impossibility of fulfillment. (Tahaawi, Ayni) So here are four cases: First, a man said: If I do not drink the water of this jug today, then my slave is free, and there is no water in it. Second, he swore similarly, and there was water in the jug, then he poured it before the day ended and before he drank it. Third, a man swore absolutely: 'If I do not drink the water of this jug, then my slave is free', and there is no water in it. Fourth, he swore similarly, and there was water in the jug, then he poured it before he drank it. Regarding that which was conditioned on a day and there is no water, he does not break, and similarly that which was conditioned with water and it was poured, and that which was absolute with no water, in these three cases he does not break according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), but Abu Yusuf says he breaks. And in the absolute case with water and he poured it, he breaks by consensus. And if there was water and he poured it, he breaks, and the principle in this and similar cases is that the possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the contraction of an absolute oath and for the continuation of a time-bound oath according to the two, because the ruling of the oath is the obligation of fulfillment. As for the expiation, it is a substitute for it when he is unable. So if fulfillment is not possible due to lacking conception, expiation is not obligatory. According to Abu Yusuf, its being conceivable is not a condition, because the oath may be contracted on what is not usually conceivable, like his saying: 'By Allah, I will certainly ascend the heaven or turn this stone into gold' – it is contracted and he breaks immediately due to the usual impossibility of fulfillment. Then, regarding when there is no water in the jug, fulfillment is impossible, so he does not break according to the two contrary to him, whether he mentioned a day or not. And if there was water, and he mentioned a day, then fulfillment is only required in the remaining part of the day, so when he poured it, fulfillment is not conceivable, so he does not break according to the two contrary to him. And if he did not mention a day, then fulfillment is required upon him when he finishes speaking, but it is extended on condition that it is not lost within his lifetime. Fulfillment is conceivable when he finishes speaking, so the oath is contracted. When he poured it, the fulfillment is lost, so he breaks by consensus. The conclusion is that according to Abu Yusuf, he breaks in all cases, but in the time-bound one after the time, and in the absolute one immediately.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed analysis of oaths regarding specific objects, such as a jug of water. It enumerates four scenarios involving a jug with or without water, with or without a time limit. The key legal principle is that the possibility of performing the sworn act (imkan al-birr) is a condition for the validity of absolute oaths and the continuation of time-bound oaths according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two'). Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the oath is contracted even if the act is impossible, using the example of impossible acts like ascending heaven. The practical outcome is that in three of the four scenarios, the oath is not broken according to the two, but Abu Yusuf holds it is broken. In the fourth scenario (absolute oath with water that is poured), all agree the oath is broken because the possibility existed at the time of the oath and was later lost. This shows the importance of the condition of possible fulfillment (imkan al-birr) in Hanafi oath jurisprudence.

Key Terms
إمكان البر (imkan al-birr): The possibility of fulfilling the sworn act; a condition for the validity of an oath according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamin al-mutlaqah): An absolute oath without a time limit (e.g., 'I will not drink this water' without specifying a day).
اليمين المقيدة (al-yamin al-muqayyadah): An oath restricted by a time condition (e.g., 'I will not drink this water today').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), in which of the following scenarios is the oath broken?
A) Time-bound oath with no water initially.
B) Time-bound oath with water poured before the day ends.
C) Absolute oath with no water initially.
D) Absolute oath with water that is poured later.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (majority view) holds that the possibility of fulfillment (imkan al-birr) is essential for an oath to contract (absolute) or continue (time-bound); without it, the oath does not bind and no expiation is due.

Oath on Customarily Impossible Acts: Hanafi Ruling and Rationale
Ruling on oaths involving customarily impossible acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths involving customarily impossible acts and explain why the oath is contracted but breached immediately.

Core Idea
Swearing to do the impossible is valid in Hanafi fiqh because the act is essentially possible; the impossibility is only customary.

This lesson examines a unique category of oaths – those concerning acts that are impossible by normal human ability, like climbing to heaven or turning stone to gold. The Hanafi school treats such oaths as valid and immediately breached. We will explore the reasoning and the difference from the views of Zufr and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The text states: if a person swears to ascend heaven or turn a stone into gold, the oath is contracted (valid) and he breaks it immediately. This is the core ruling.
2
Distinguish positive vs. negative oaths
The ruling applies only to oaths to perform a positive act. If someone swears to leave (refrain from) an impossible act, the oath is not contracted because leaving is only conceivable when the act is within one's power.
Oaths of abstention on impossible acts are invalid.
3
Understand why customarily impossible acts are considered possible
These acts are possible in essence (bi al-dhat) by divine power. The text gives examples: angels and some prophets have ascended heaven; Allah can turn stone to gold. Thus, they are conceivable contracts for oaths.
The impossibility is merely customary, not essential.
4
Apply the distinction between absolute and time-bound oaths
For an absolute oath (without time condition), breach occurs immediately upon swearing because fulfillment is customarily impossible. For a time-bound oath, breach depends on the expiry of the time; if the swearer dies before, no breach.
The text specifies 'fi al-mutlaqah' (in absolute oaths).
5
Analyze the dispute with Zufr and al-Shafi'i
Zufr and al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) argue that the oath is not contracted because customary impossibility makes it effectively impossible. The Hanafi response: the act is truly conceivable, so the oath is valid; breach is due to customary inability.
Practical difference: Hanafi requires expiation; opponents say no expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حلف ليصعدن السماء أو ليقلبن هذا الحجر ذهباً حنث في الحال، قيد بالفعل؛ لأنه لو حلف على الترك بأن قال: إن تركت مس السماء فكذا لم تنعقد يمينه؛ لأن الترك لا يتصور في غير المقدور، ووجه الانعقاد في "ليصعدن" ونحوه؛ لأن صعود السماء ممكن، ولهذا صعدتها الملائكة وبعض الأنبياء، وكذا تحويل الحجر ذهبا بقدرة الله فإذا كان متصوراً انعقد اليمين ويحنث في الحال للعجز الثابت عادة ووضع اليمين في المطلقة؛ لأن المقيدة يتوقف حنثه فيها على مضي الوقت حتى لو مات قبله لم يحنث، وقال زفر: لا ينعقد؛ لأنه مستحيل عادة فأشبه المستحيل حقيقة قلنا: إن البر متصور حقيقة ويحنث للعجز العادي، وقال الشافعي في قول: لا ينعقد ولا يحنث
He swore to ascend the heaven or to turn this stone into gold, he breaks the oath immediately. It is restricted to a positive act because if he swore on leaving, such as saying: If I leave touching the heaven, then such-and-such, his oath is not contracted because leaving is not conceivable regarding that which is not within one's power. The reason for contraction in 'I will ascend' and similar is that ascending the heaven is possible; for this reason the angels and some prophets ascended it, and similarly turning a stone into gold is possible by the power of Allah. So when it is conceivable, the oath is contracted and he breaks immediately due to the customary established inability, and the position of the oath is for the absolute oath; because the time-bound oath, its breaking depends on the passage of time, so if he dies before it, he does not break. Zufr said: It is not contracted because it is customarily impossible, so it resembles what is actually impossible. We replied: Fulfillment is actually conceivable, and he breaks due to customary inability. Al-Shafi'i said in one opinion: It is not contracted and he does not break.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths on acts that are customarily impossible, such as ascending heaven or turning a stone into gold. The Hanafi ruling is that such oaths are contracted and broken immediately. The reason is that these acts are possible in themselves (by divine power), even if not by human capacity. The impossibility is only customary, not essential. Therefore, the oath is valid and since fulfillment is not possible by custom, he breaks immediately. Zufr and al-Shafi'i (in one view) disagree, arguing that customary impossibility makes the oath invalid. The text emphasizes that the ruling applies only to positive acts (swearing to do), not to negative acts (swearing to leave), because leaving an act is only conceivable if the act is within one's power. This shows the Hanafi approach to oaths involving extraordinary matters.

Key Terms
المستحيل عادة (al-mustahil 'adatan): That which is impossible by custom, but possible in essence (e.g., ascending heaven).
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamin al-mutlaqah): An absolute oath not tied to a time condition; breaking occurs immediately upon impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The oath is invalid and no expiation is needed.
B) The oath is contracted and broken immediately, requiring expiation.
C) The oath is valid but he must wait until he actually does it.
D) The oath is valid only if he can do it by supernatural means.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath on a customarily impossible act is valid and immediately breached, requiring expiation.

Oath on Not Speaking to Someone: Waking the Sleeper and Permission without Knowledge
Ruling on oaths about speaking to a sleeping person and permission without knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an oath not to speak to a sleeping person involves waking him, and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding calling without waking.

Core Idea
Speaking to a sleeping person breaks an oath if you wake him; without waking, the chosen view is no breach.

This passage examines two situations: first, when someone swears not to speak to a person who is asleep and then calls him; second, when someone swears not to speak except with permission, but permission is given without the swearer's knowledge. Each scenario has an intra-Hanafi disagreement, and the author indicates the chosen view (al-mukhtar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
The case is: someone swears 'I will not speak to so-and-so.' Determine if the person is asleep or awake.
The oath is general, so speaking to the person even while asleep could potentially break it.
2
Scenario A: Waking by calling
If the swearer calls the sleeping person and wakes him, the oath is broken. The act of speaking and making him hear constitutes communication.
No dispute here – all agree the oath is broken.
3
Scenario B: Calling without waking
If the swearer calls but does not wake the person, Abu Hanifa rules the oath is broken because the person would have heard if awake. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is not broken because no actual hearing occurred.
The chosen view (mukhtar) agrees with the Sahibayn: no breach.
4
Scenario C: Permission without knowledge
If someone swears not to speak except with permission, and permission is granted but the swearer does not know, Abu Hanifa says the oath is broken because 'idhn' derives from 'adhan' (informing) and requires knowledge. Abu Yusuf says it is not broken, analogizing to consent (rida).
The Hanafi position distinguishes permission (external action requiring awareness) from consent (internal state).
5
Derive the operative ruling
For fatwa, the chosen views are: if the call does not wake the sleeper, the oath is not broken; and for permission without knowledge, the oath is broken unless the swearer was informed.
Focus on the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكلمه أي لو حلف لا يكلم فلاناً. (عيني) فأيقظه : بندائه، فلو لم يوقظه لم يحنث وهو المختار. (ط) فناداه أو إلا بإذنه: أي أو حلف لا يكلمه إلا إلخ. (عيني) حنث: أي في الوجهين الأول: أنه حلف لا يكلم فلاناً، وهو نائم فأيقظه بندائه حنث؛ لأنه كلمه وأسمعه، ولو لم يوقظه فإذا كان بحيث يسمع لو لم يكن نائماً يحنث عند الإمام خلافاً لهما، والثاني: لو حلف لا يكلم إلا بإذنه فأذن له و لم يعلم الحالف بالإذن حتى كلمه حنث؛ لأن الإذن مشتق من الأذان الذي هو الإعلام، أو من الوقوع في الإذن، وكل ذلك لا يتحقق إلا بعد العلم، وقال أبويوسف لا يحنث؛ لأن الإذن هو الإطلاق، وإنه يتم بالإذن كالرضاء فيما إذا حلف لا يكلم إلا برضاه فرضي المحلوف عليه ولم يعلم الحالف فكلمه حيث لا يحنث ؛ لأن الرضاء يتم بالراضي، قلنا: الرضاء من أعمال القلب ولا كذلك الإذن؛ لأنه من الأذان وهو الإعلام، ولا يتحقق بدون العلم، ثم الكلام لا يكون إلا باللسان، فلا يكون =
He does not speak to him, i.e., if he swore not to speak to so-and-so. (Ayni) So he woke him by calling him, so if he did not wake him, he does not break the oath, and that is the chosen view. (Tahaawi) So he called him, or except with his permission: i.e., or he swore not to speak to him except with his permission, etc. (Ayni) He breaks the oath, i.e., in two ways: First, that he swore not to speak to so-and-so, and he was asleep, so he woke him by his call, he breaks because he spoke to him and made him hear. If he did not wake him, but he was such that he would hear if he were not asleep, he breaks according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Second, if he swore not to speak except with his permission, and he permitted him but the swearer did not know of the permission until he spoke, he breaks because 'permission' is derived from 'adhan' which is informing, or from falling into the ear, and all that is not realized without knowledge. Abu Yusuf said: He does not break because permission is simply permission, and it is completed by the permission itself like consent in the case where he swore not to speak except with his consent, and the person consented but the swearer did not know, and he spoke, then he does not break because consent is complete by the consenter. We said: Consent is an act of the heart, and permission is not like that because it is from 'adhan' which is informing, and it is not realized without knowledge. Then speech is only with the tongue, so it is not =
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios regarding oaths about speaking to a person. First, if someone swears not to speak to a sleeping person and then wakes him by calling, the oath is broken because he has spoken to him and made him hear. If he does not wake him, but the person could hear if awake, Abu Hanifa rules that the oath is still broken, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. The chosen view is that if he does not wake him, he does not break. Second, if someone swears not to speak except with permission, and permission is given without the swearer's knowledge, then according to Abu Hanifa the oath is broken because the very concept of permission requires knowledge (from 'adhan' meaning informing). Abu Yusuf analogizes it to consent, which is complete without knowledge, but the Hanafi response distinguishes consent (internal act of heart) from permission (external act requiring awareness). This shows the Hanafi attention to linguistic roots (derivation of 'idhn' from 'adhan').

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission, derived from 'adhan' (informing). Requires the knowledge of the swearer to be valid.
الإعلام (al-i'lam): Informing, the root meaning of 'idhn' according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in the Hanafi school, if someone swears not to speak to a sleeping person, calls him, but does not wake him, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because he spoke.
B) He does not break the oath because the person did not hear.
C) He breaks the oath only if the person wakes up.
D) The oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The chosen Hanafi position is that an oath not to speak to a sleeping person is broken only if the person is actually woken (hears), and that permission (idhn) requires the swearer's knowledge to be effective.
